THE RED PAGE.
TBS GARDEN OF YEARS
THE attention paid in America to such verse-
writers as G. W. Oarryl shows how it is that
our own poetical swans, sailing over wide
waters, occasionally arrive with drooping
crests. Now that matter and energy, in accord-
ance with the prognostic here made six years ago,
have been found to be the same thing, one may
hazard a speculation upon the identity of Space
and Time as two aspects of the eternal mask.
The mathematical proof is not beyond conjec-
ture. Meanwhile one can affirm that Space
equals Time in the region of criticism.
Your foreigner is true posterity ; and his intelli-
gent judgments are the judgments of your grand-
children a hundred years hence. That accounts
for the somewhat languid enthusiasm one brings
to G. W. Carryl. His poems have suffered a sea-
change in crossing the Pacific from the warm
atmosphere of friendly New York.
To the great public, Carryl (he died last year in
early middle-age) was better known by his clever
rhymes “in lighter vein.” The Garden of Years
(Lond., Putnam’s; 6s. net) collects his serious
work in a decorative volume, frontispieced by
Maxfield Parrish and prefaced by E. C. Stedman.
Stedman is pleasant, personal, and friendly: he
was too close to the subject. “Byronic,” he calls
the work. Bossibly, but too much watered:
Byron without pulse and passion, agreeably orna-
menting attenuated American emotions. Grand
Poetry has been civilised and cultured out of
America along with the babies. Carryl scarcely
ever rises to Poetry in our sense : he is moral and
descriptive, he is fanciful and rhetorical: some-
times he is poetical; but we find just one line of
Poetry:
The Seine in silence blots away the past.
It was quite worth while writing a volume to
have written that one line.
It is Paris that seems to inspire Carryl to his
best: there he is caught .and lifted a little beyond
himself:
PARIS .
I knew when first I looked into her eyes,
And she in mine, that what has been must be,
And so let others say she told them lies:
She told no lie to me !
She spoke me fair, of lees as well as wine,
Then, with that subtlest charm of all her charms,
Half-dropped her languid lids, and at the.sign
I ran into her arms 1
Now it is she who flings my window wide
At dawn, and lets the perfumed morning in,
And she who walks so softly at my side,
Through noonday's dust and din.
Bug most, of all, ’t is she, where blue night falls,
Whose firm, imperious fingers tap the pane,
And she whose velvet voice it is that calls,
Nor calls her own in vain !
It is as if the siren understood
llow that she is so strong at this still hour,
That I could not repulse her if I would,
Nor would, had I the power :
As if she knew that, should I try to check
The strength of that enrapt, responsive thrill,
Let her but slide her arm a,bout my neck,
And I obey her will!
So, when she speaks, I answer ; when she woos,
Her voice, like wine, the slow pulse goads and spurs:
I go to meet her through the dropping dews,
And lean my lips to hers.
All the long hours run laughing into one —
The strange, street moment when the evening falls —
And, like a mother summoning her son,
Resistless Paris calls !
“The Garden of Years,” the lengthy title-poem,
is an emotional record of some value, written in a
novel stanza. The phrasing is pictorial:
The blue-robed Indian Ocean slept and sighed,
Decked with her emerald islands, looped in strings
Upon the breathing bosom of her tide :—
Slept all bronze day, and all star-studded night..,
The grim old earth hath seen too many Springs ;
Lovers enough have trapped her charm in words:
To all her flowers the mould of usage clings,
And, to the music of her weary birds,
The burden of reiterated themes ...
Here is the full stanza :
All my life long in memory I shall guard
That sloiv sea-swing that lullabied the heart,
While the thin, thoughtful mast, shrouded and sparred,
Moved in and out upon the silver-starred
Midnight, as if it traced upon a chart:
And the prow forced the fluttering waves apart,
As they had been the leaves of some wise tome,
Wherefrom it read Life's story from the start,
Set to the music of the whirling foam,
Wind-rippled cordage, and slcrw-straining yard.
Carryl has not been able to reconcile us to the
fourth line, which is designed apparently to gain
the effect of a wave at height pausing before
the fall. In practice, it becomes parenthetical
or padded : he cannot train his thought to match
the form. And in the last lines, as Carryl uses
them, one thinks the familiar couplet might have
done better service.
In his inferior passages Carryl can write :
The phases of our duplicated dream
or
Of morning’s mute miraculous intent...
—and so one sees his limitations. He likes ex-
perimenting with unusual words and uses of
words, not successfully :
On her brow is the excellent whiteness of still Sierra
snows ...
or “pinguid sea” (128). This.we hail as anew
epithet from L. pinguis, fat. Carryl refers pre-
sumably to an oily appearance of the water ; but
pinguis, to our mind, conveys too great a sugges-
tion of substance. H ow would “ mtid ” (=sleek)
do?
- J*
The thought is not especially new or deep.
“Ad Finem Fideles,” verses for the graves of
pioneers, is a subject familiar in Maoriland. The
no less familiar “unborn child” motive is less
often treated from the man’s point of view :
How slow upon the air the music dies!
How blind am I, how loath to understand !
The wraiths of dreams denied brush by me, and
I find my unborn bairns in strangers’ eyes !
Exiled, I watch them, romping as they run,
Heartsick for this that now can never be :—
One that should at my coming run to me !
Ah, God ! ah, God ! why hast Thou hid the sun ?
Yet the volume is not to be contemned, and verse-
lovers will find their profit in it. It is worth while
meeting this, for example :
Narcissus, Narcissus, where is thy boyish bloom,
Thy long, slim form that lay beside the pool,
And the lips cold smiling to their smiling image cool ?
Narcissus !
One closes the book with an impression of a
strong, restless, unsatisfied personality, never quite
in tune with life, now harmonised by Death that
can supply a cadence for every chord.
A little easeful sighing
d-rki restful turning round,
And I, too, on Thy love relying.
Shall slumber sound,.
HOMES AND EDUCATION
lIE . higher education of woman is-surely
I extinguishing her race, both directly by its
JL effects on her organisation, and, indirectly,
by rendering early’ marriage impossible for
the average man...
The dutiesof wifehood, and still more of mother-
hood, do not require an extraordinary develop-
ment of the brain, but they must absolutely have a
strong development of the body. Not only does
wifehood and motherhood not require an extra-
ordinary development of the brain, but the latter
is a decided barrier against the proper perform-
ance of these duties. Any family physician could
give innumerable cases out of his experience of
failures of marriage, directly due to too great a
cultivation of the female intellect, which results in
the scorning to perform those duties which are
cheerfully performed, and even desired, by the
unedueated wife. The duties of motherhood are
direct rivals of brain work, for they both require
for their performance an exclusive and plentiful
supply of phosphates. These are obtained from
the food in greater or less quantity, but rarely, if
ever, in sufficient quantity to supply an active and
highly educated intellect, and, at the game time,
the wants of the growing child. The latter before
birth must extract from its mother’s blood all the
chemical salts necessary for the formation of its
bony skeleton and for other tissues,; and in
this rivalry between the offspring and the
intellect how often has not the family physician
seen the brain lose in the struggle...
The highly educated woman seems to know that
she will make a poor mother, for she marries
rarely and late and, when she does, the number of
children is very small... But even supposing that
the highly educated woman were able and willing
to ben and rear her children like any other
woman, she has one drawback from having a
fairly large family, and that is the lateness at
which she marries, the average being between
twenty-six and twenty-seven years. Now, as a
woman of that age should marry a man between
ten and fifteen years older than herself, for a
woman of twenty-seven is as old as a man of forty
for the purpose of marriage, both she and her hus-
band are too old to begin the raising of an ordinary-
sized family. Men and women of that age are old
maids and old bachelors. They have been living
their own lives during their best years ; they have
become set in their ways, they must have then-
own pleasures; in a word, they have become
selfish. And, after having had one or at the most
two children, the woman objects to having any-
more, and this is the beginning of the end of
marital happiness. The records of our divorce
courts show in hundreds of instances, that there
was no trouble in the home while the woman was
performing her functions of motherhood, but that
trouble began as soon as,she began to shirk them.
Hundreds of thousands of 'men at the-present day
are married, but have no wives; and while this
sad state of affairs occurs occasionally among the
moderately educated, it exists very frequently
among the highly educated.
Jt ‘
Is the health of women at the present day
worse than it was in the time of our grand-
mothers? Are the duties of wifehood and
motherhood really harder to perform now than
they were one hundred years ago? Without
hesitation the answer is “Yes.” Not only are
the sexual and maternal instincts of the average
woman becoming less and less from year to year ;
the best proof of which is later and later marriages
and fewer and fewer children ; but, in the writer’s
opinion, the majority of women of the middle and
upper classes are sick and suffering before marriage
and are physically disabled from performing physi-
ological functions in a natural manner. At a recent
meeting of a well-known society of, specialists for
obstetrics and diseases of wometi, one of the
fellows with the largest practice in the largest city
in America stated that it was physicallv im-
possible- for the majority of his patients to have
a natural labour, because their power to feel pain
was so great, while their muscular power was so
little.
On these two questions the whole profes-
sion is agreed, but 1 am bound to say that there
is a difference of opinion as to the reason.
Several of the most distinguished fellows of
the above society claim that the generally
prevalent breakdown of women is due to their
inordinate pursuit of pleasure during the ten
years which elapse between their leaving school
and their marriage. This includes late hours,
turning night into insufficient sleep, improper
diet, improper clothing, and want of exercise.
Tlie writer claims that most of the generally
admitted poor health of women is due to over
education, which first deprives them of sunlight
and fresh air for the greater part of tlie r tame ;
second, takes every drop of blood awav to the
brain from the growing organs of generation ;
third, develops their nervous system at the ex-
pense of all their other systems, muscular, diges-
tive, generative, &c. ; fourth, leads them to live
an abnormal single life until the age of twenty-
six or twenty-seven instead of being married at
eighteen, which is the latest nature meant them
to remain single ; fifth, raises their requirements
so high that they cannot marrv a young man in
good Health...
Another way in which the higher education is
making people unhappy is in the cultivation of
the powers of analysis and criticism. When the
power of analysis is applied to one’s own self it is
especially unfortunate, for then it becomes intro-
spection, a faculty which is carried so far with
some women that their whole life .is spent in
looking into themselves, caring nothing tor the
trials or troubles of those about them. This
produces an intense form of egotism and selfish-
ness. These people are exceedingly unhappy,
very often suffering from what is wrongly called
“nervous prostration,” but which should rather
be called “ nervous prosperity.” When the won-
derful power of criticising is applied to others it
takes the form of fault-finding. Buch a woman
must have many victims; will she make them
happy ?
Jt
There is no doubt that women can do everything
that men can do, and a great deal more ; but the
knowledge of their ability brings with it an
aggressive, self-assertive,- independent character,
which renders it impossible to love, honour and
obey the men of their social circles who are the
brothers of their, schoolmates, and who in the
effort to become ripb enough to afford the luxury
of a wife have to begin young at
business or in the factory, and for whom it is
impossible to ever place themselves on an intel-
lectual equality with the women whom they should
marry. These men are, as a rule, refused by the
brilliant college graduate, and are either ship-
wrecked for life and for eternity by remaining
single, or are only saved by marrying a woman
who is their social inferior, but who, by reason of
her contented mind, in tile end makes them a
much better helpmate than the fault-finding intel-
lectual woman who is looking for an impossible
ideal.
Occasionally a college graduate goes through
the ordeal of a high education, which has de-
veloped her intellect without ruining either her
body or her natural instincts; but, as far as the
writer can see, she is decidedly an exception. To
the average highly intellectual woman the ordi-
nary cares of wifehood and motherhood are exceed-
ingly irksome and distasteful, and the majority of
sued women unhesitatingly say that they will not
marry, unless they can get a man who can afford
to keep them in luxury and supply them with
their intellectual requirements. The gradual dis-
appearance of the home, which any thoughtful
observer must deplore, is, to a large extent, the
result of the discontentment of the educated
woman with the duties and surroundings of wife-
hood and motfierhood, and the thirst for conceits*
theatres, pictures, and parties, which keep her in
the public gaze, to the loss of her health and the
ruin, very often, of her husband’s happiness...
What about the supply of female school
teachers? Is not the very highest education
possible necessary for them ? From the writer’s
point of view most of the women who are now
teaching school should have been married at
eighteen and in a house of their own, which might
have been the schoolmaster’s house. The profession
of teaching was once exclusively in the hands of the
men, and it can not be denied that they have
achieved some great results. But as education
rendered an ever-increasing mimher of women un-
suited for marriage, that is, unwilling to marry
tlie available men, they invaded the schoolmaster’s
rank to such an extent that his salary has been cut
down one half, and nowfhe is unable to marry at
all. Two well-known consequences have followed
this state of affairs; first it is impossible to get
men in sufficient numbers to become teachers for
the boys’ schools ; and secondly, even big boys
being taught by women, the effeminization of
our men is gradually taking place. Although
there are some instances of a mother alone having
formed her son into a manly man, yet as a rule
the boys require the example of a man’s character
to make them manly men. This subject has re-
cently been dealt with in several elaborate papers
by well-known educationalists, to whom it appears
to be a real danger to the coming generation.— P.
S. Smith, in The P.S. Monthly (U.S. A.).
MEMORANDA
Drawings of Albrecht Diirer (Lond., Geo.
Newnes; 7s. fid. net) is: the second book in the
publishers’ “Great Masters” series, designed to
give for the use of artists and connoisseurs some
48 illustrations of each subject, adequately repro-
duced and bound with taste. The present book
justifies the conception and the promise. The
marvel of Diirer : his originality, his fertility, and
his craftsmanship that made Italians (and in
Michelangelo’s epoch) wonder how so good a
thing Could come out of Germany: receives not
unworthy manifestation. The size of the book
enables the whole set of the famous “Green
Passion” to be shown to advantage. Other
treasures are the “ Portrait of an Old Man 93
Years Old,” the Hare and the “Prodigal Son” and
its preliminary study. The introductory notice
is written by Hans Singer, who in the choice
of illustrations has avoided those more familiar
in order to give a variety of studies
and rarely seen, full of instruction
in the pen-drawing and wood-cutting of which
Diirer was master. This rarity of choice gives the
book exceptional value for local students. The
prints have been carefully reproduced, many in
the original colour and extra-mounted.
One of the English magas. is publishing
“authors’ best stories” selected by themselves...
and si. iking our belief that a writer can be a
judge of his own work. Barry Pain selects “The
Kindness of the Celestial.” Now the only very
good “short story” that Barry ever wrote was
“The Girl and the Beetle.”
In Stingaree, E. W. Hornung has added another
to the Barrihm’s collection of monsters which rep-
resent Englishmen’s ideas of Australia. Hornung
has done bright work in his own field : his early
Raffles was good company : but when he gets to
Australia he is always ridiculous. Because he has
the Englishman’s point of view, or because he
doctors his matter to suit the English audiences.
Lacking semblance of reality the adventures of
“ Stingaree ’ m ike a ludicrous book.
Gr. F. Watts (who died worth 80,000/.) willed
many of his hoarded pictures to be distributed
among public galleries ; and N.S.W. Gallery is to
get a picture called Alice —apparently not a
notable work, since one cannot find it recorded or
illustrated in three Watts books examined.
The writer of some recently-published reminis-
cences of Watts says lie detested new brushes, of
which “he would wear the outside bristles down
on a background, or by merely rubbing them on a
hard surface till they became a stiff little pyramid
the shape of a stump used for chalk drawings, and
then they beoame great treasures.” Again,
“when the putty-like pigment which he put on
the canvas in distinct touches was nearly dry, lie
would sometimes take a paper-knife, and, using
the flat part, would rub it over the touches, smear-
ing them together. He would not touch the
painting again till the smeared surface was quite
dry. Then he would work partially over it. In
this way he contrived to get a bloom of atmos-
phere into his painting, a. quality which he always
aimed at.”
Our old friend, the Times Encyclopaedia man,
has broken out in a fresh place. He circularises
people who wrote to him about the Encyclopaedia
(us, too, fortunate possessors of scrap-books and
pictures of “The Okapi; Immature”) with
information concerning The Library of Famous
Literature , another bait on the time-payment
hook. This Library has been here before; and
the public was adjured to buy it for 17/., on the
ground that it was a splendid bargain at the
money. Now, to show what a very hollow drum
was originally beaten, the time-payment man
offers the whole 17/.-worth for SZ, cash —which is
pleasant news for the people who listened to the
drum and purchased the volumes. One flaw in all
such collections is this: the extracts are necessarily
so scrappy that one can get little profit out of them,
so that it is better to put one’s money into com-
plete works by a few of the authors represented.
Another flaw is this: that in trying to please
everybody the editors must include a great deal of
material that is of no use to you, the individual
buyer ; and in buying what you want you pay for
a great deal that is merely a burden. Still, 5 1. is
something like a fair price for the lAhrary—
although a set of the 20 volumes (J-calf) has been
on sale in Dymock’s book-shop for months past at
3/. 10s.
Something very like an organised claque was in
evidence at violinist Hugo Heermannlast recital
in Sydney. Emil Heermann, his father’s son,
who gave a clever rendering, of a Paganini
concerto, was recalled seven times—say six times
more than the performance intrinsically war-
ranted. Hugo Heermann maybe described as a
virtuoso of the German class. He plays admi-
rably, beautifully ; but he plays like a man of
science, who is fond of music. Of musical tem-
perament in the special sense he has little.
What the music gives, he gives correctly and
rather coldly. The people who compared his style
with the style of Melba’s singing were in the right.
We are of those- who like personal emotion in
music. The violin, like t'he lyre, “is a winged
instrument, and should transport.” In Ysaye’s
hands it does; so also in Kreisler’s. These men,
like Paderewski, have the genius of music.
Heermann has only the science. One guesses that
it would have astounded Munich to read the
criticisms (?) in Sydney Daily Telegraph, where
superlatives were lavished galore. When Oairos
Rego comes back, The Telegraph should let his
deputy travel too. The Herald showed a better
appreciation of values, though one would be
better pleased if the writer devoted a little less
space to preliminary-history, and a little more to
details of the performance.
An excellent description of fiddles generally in
The Telegraph was followed by an absolutely para-
lysing com mu ni ca tioty f rom a fiddle enthusiast in
Melbourne, who hoards more lore upon the sub-
ject than anybody ever dreamed was cherished in
Australia. This enthusiast suggests the formation
of a Fiddle Fanciers’ Association, whose members
shall meet regularly (presumably on fences, at full
moon) and compare their treasures. Flippancy in
such a connection is, of course, - deplorable. A col-
lection of old fiddles is as sacred as a collection of
old books. Still, if you have ever met one really
weird fiddle-fancier on the war-path, your imagi-
nation may reel like ours at the idea of the first
meeting of that Association.
In Home Life in France, M. Betham-Edwardes
declares that the native patronage of naughty
French novels would not suffice to keep their
authors going. Russia is by far the best customer,
Germany and England following suit. _ Much of
the worst trash. The Academy believes, is exported
to South America. The worthless Frenoli novel,
in fact (and don’t forget there are a great many
worthy), is an article de Paris provided for the
delectation of visitors, English and other, and
carefully shunned by the French themselves.
Our esteemed contemporary L’ltalo-Austrjiliano
(known to initiates as “The Macaroni Gazette” on the
lueus a non principle : because when you go to Sya.
Italian restaurants you get good soup but no macaroni)
comes of manly age with its 21st number, and grows and
thrives on local news, sensible politics, and the Bonmar-
tini trial—which is still going along in Italy. At date of
last progress report counsel had commenced to read
“ thousands of letters ” which passed between husband
and incriminated wife. Matrimony in cultivated Italy
must be a fine literary apprenticeship.

acknowledge the source of reprinted American articles
such as that on “ Recent Amusing Verse.”

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Charis—Her Eyes.
Those entrancing
Eyes of Charis,
Where the dancing
Sunbeam marries.
With the softer lights they hold
How shall be their beauty told ?
Are the wings of
Any dove,
Though it springs of
Venus’ drove,
Colored by the Paphian day,
Lustred with a tenderer ray ?
Sunset heaven
Paling slow,
Whence are driven
Clouds aglow,
Sometimes holds in upper skies
Such a grey as Charis’ eyes.
Shimmering, welling
(Lake or river)
Love compelling
Are they ever;
Like all eyes in heav’n that shine
Are those two of Charis mine.
VIC.
P.
Our Tryst.
Long ago, and lovingly,
At the gate she watched for me
In the silent, silver glory of the stars ;
And, just half a mile below,
We could hear the sobbing flow
Of the river droning, drifting on the bars.
It was at the garden gate
(Where the countless lovers wait!),
And I held her soft and shapely hand in mine,
As we played the final parts
In the play of Breaking Hearts—
In the play of Youthful Hopes and Auld Lang Syne.
For I whispered for a space
That I soon must leave the place—
Cross the river, and the ridges, and the range ;
Leave the simple folk I knew,
And the valley where I grew,
For the city—with its fever and its change.
Where our fondest mission fails,
How a woman’s love prevails !
(O the love that drives us on of lures us back !)
How it sends us out to roam,
Or would have us rot at home,
While the braver men (and better) take the track !
Yes, and so it is I stand,
Holding once again her hand
In the shadows sweet and silent by the gate;
And her eyes (O superfine !)
Look with yearning into mine—
And the dearest dreams I had will have to wait!
For, pray tell me what is Fame
But a loudly vaunted name—
’Tis a mockery, a delusion, and a sham !
Aye, and I would rather be,
In the Land of Fancy Free,
Just the youthful, happy lover that I am.
And oh ! tell me what is Life—
Not a weary war with strife,
Nor a fevered fight to merit scanty praise ?
My inmost soul says “ No ! ”
For ’tis settled—l will go
With the lovers down the easy, pleasant ways !
Yea, and so to-night are we
Whisp’ring here in ecstasy
In the glory of the same old blinking stars;
And, just half a mile below,
We can hear the music low,
As the river rolls for ever on the bars.
N.S.W.
GILROONEY.
The Derwent River.
I want to go back to the Derwent River,
To the orchard bowers and sun-kissed dew,
On slopes of emerald all a-quiver,
The green, sweet ways that of old I knew;
To the river banks, and the far-flung splendor
Of waving grass where the wild bees roam;
To Spring-time visions so soft and tender—
I want to go home to you.
I want to go hone to the roses blowing
In mazes of crimson, cream and gold,
With the star-like jasmine flowers all glowing
A tangled mass in the garden old.
I can hear the click of the garden gate,
And steps on the path where the peach trees grew;
And oh, the longing that comes too late—
I want to go back to you.
I want to go home to the lilies swinging
In ghostly masses of slatey bloom,
In darkened garden, with woodbine clinging,
By opened windows and scented room;
For a little while of dear youth a flower,
When older things shall seem as new.
For just the space of one little hour
I want to go back to you.
I want to go back—now the voice is calling
All soft a 3 rustle of woodland leaves;
As sweet as musical waters falling,
As sad as sigh of a heart that grieves.
The lilac tosses where soft winds shiver,
The moonlit garden is drenched with dew ;
I want to go back to the Derwent River—
I want to go back to you.
VIC.
KATHLEEN WALKER.

Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
How to Address Betters.
Correspondents to Tiie Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence ” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing, with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
two columns in length, or, say, 8000 ivords, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps arc enclosed But no
MS. ivill be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
. for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Made and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct' reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
ivill be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture tvhich has to be re-drawn—will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions ivill be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, JULY 27, 1905.
THE WHITE MAN’S OUTPOST.
The announcement made by Lord Roberts that
the British army, after all the alleged re-organisa-
tion, is just the same army which failed so miser-
ably in Boer-land, should not come as a surprise to
anyone who has studied recent history. When
the machine went to pieces against a handful
of farmers it was stated, as it had pre-
viously been stated on so many other occasions,
that this time a great lesson had been learned and
it would do the country no end of good. So the
old army was neatly subdivided into different
“ commands” (just as the regiments were named
afresh at another time of upheaval), and the
buttons were altered, and a new bayonet was
introduced which is so short that any other
soldier in Europe who is willing to wait and argue
the point can stick the British infantry-man while
the latter’s weapon is still about a foot distant.
Also a man who used to be called something else
was called an Inspector-General, and that was
about all. As anybody could see, it wasn’t much.
* * * *
The greatest trouble—that the British army is a
voluntary force recruited from the undersized
dregs of the population, and contains not a quarter
enough even of the dregs to make a respectable
force as armies go nowadays—no one dared to
touch. It is impossible to pay enough to attract
really good men—the half-million or so of really
good men that would make Britain reasonably
safe. But to propose the compulsory training of
the whole able-bodied male population would
really be conscription, and Britain has for years
coddled three fetishes —that it hasn't conscription,
that it avoids Protection, and that it never, under
any circumstances, shuts out aliens, no matter
how diseased, ignorant, dirty, criminal or undesir-
able. So the British army remained very much
the same as regards its rank and file. And the
gulf between the dregs in the ranks- and the
dudes at the mess table being too vast to be
bridged over, the officers continued to be a
caste apart men whose qualifications were
largely wealth and fashion, also a knowledge of
polo, and a capacity for doing credit to their uni-
form. The British army now is supposed to'be
just about sufficient to guard the north-west fron-
tier of India provided the trouble there isn’t too
large, and it represents about enough men to
supply the losses of three battles when nations
take each other earnestly by the throat as the
Bear and the Monkey have been doing in Man-
churia.
* * 4 *
And as regards the British navy, it still controls
the sea in name, but as to what the reality may be
there are more doubts than there used to be
Part at least of the downfall of the last Russian
neet was effected by submarines. Most of the
rum of the previous Russian fleet was brought
about by torpedo boats. If the battleship
and the cruiser still dominate the situation,
then Britain is still supreme at sea, with-
out taking any credit for the possible superiority
or the British naval seaman as a fighting
unit when compared with most of his rivals.
If the torpedo boat and the submarine are the
decisive elements, then, assuming the men to be
about equal, British naval supremacy has become
somewhat doubtful. Britain itself begins to show
that it no longer possesses the old cheerful con-
fidence. There is a great calling in of its ships,
and a great concentration of naval force round its
own shores, and a large abandonment of the old
idea that the flag should pervade every sea
and rage up and down the coast of every British
possession. The South Atlantic squadron has
been abolished, and the U.S. flag is supreme
thereabouts save for such hostile argument as Brazil
and Argentina can show against it. All battleships
have been withdrawn from the Pacific, where only
a few cruisers—not, as a rule, of the best
class—are now maintained. Of Britain’s 32
modern and 10 older battleships— 42 in all
—24 are kept cruising in British waters,
nine are kept in reserve in British ports, and nine
are no further away than the Mediterranean. The
best of the cruisers are similarly disposed. It is
openly suggested that when the present naval
subsidy agreement expires, no further arrange-
ments of the kind should be made, and the Aus-
tralian squadron should be called in. Baldly and
undisguisedly the new British naval policy sets
forth that Britain realises great dangers on
its own coasts. Among these are the grow-
ing strength of Germany on one side and
of the United States on the other, the
I rench supremacy in the matter of torpedo boats
—which may jrnt prove more dangerous than the
bulky battleships—and the fact that every attempt
at army reorganisation seems to end up in the
same old paralysis. Among all these troubles, it
is going to take no avoidable chances at sea. The
new arrangement is a blank intimation that
Britain pays for the fleet almost wholly ; that the
fleet exists for Britain’s protection first, and for as
much protection to Greater Britain afterwards as
Britain may feel inclined to give ; and that, while
there is a semblance of danger to Britain, the
colonies, dependencies and all outside may go hang.
• * * *
And Britain is justified in taking a serious view
of its position. It is the worst position that coun-
try has occupied in the last two centuries. The
decay of agriculture through Preetrade and land-
lordism, the inefficiency of such agriculture as
there is, the turning of great arable areas into
sheepwalks, the laying waste of others so that
unpatriotic dudes may shoot deer and maim grouse
and partridges, have made Britain so completely
dependent on outsiders for food that it must keep
the sea open.to it or surrender through starvation.
It must keep the sea secure even if it lets all G reater
Britain go while attending to this one essential.
Therefore Britain, realising the increasing strength
of its enemies and its dubious friends, gathers its
fleet into its own waters. It may not have
sense enough to abandon Freetrade, to strive to
be more self-supporting in the matter of food, to
build up an army worthy of the danger that may
come ; but, at all events, it is taking some strong
measures. Australia, which is in some respects
the counterpart of Brit", .n, isn’t taking anything
except a rest. Britain, if it lost command of the
sea, would be the most helpless country on earth
through want of food. Australia, if Britain lost
command of the sea, or was too busy elsewhere to
keep command hereabouts, would be the second
most helpless country on earth for want of popu-
lation, trained soldiers and sailors, guns and
ammunition, arms factories, iron and steel works,
shipbuilding yards, woollen, cotton, and clothing
factories, boot factories, hat factories, machinery
shops, and everything else that can keep a country
going when imports are cut off, and keep
it defensible when there is no help from out-
side. Almost as bad as all the rest, it
would be wrecked for want of money, having
exhausted its credit in time of peace to
save taxation. And Australia, so far, is doing
nothing to repair its defences. It still imports
instead of manufacturing. It still does without
population, because it can’t get much population
without supplying work for the people, and it
can’t do much in that way without "damaging the
business of the sacred importer. It still borrows,
except in the case of one or two States, because
borrowing is easy. And when Parliament meets
it is understood that the chief business is to be
the injury done to George Reid because his
bloated waistcoat was turned out of office, the re-
distribution of seats, the anti-Soeialist crusade,
and the usual stonewall.
* * • *
This country can’t expect to be the furthest
outpost of the White Man for nothing. To lead
the van is an honorable post if the leader of the van
goes armed in a fashion that makes him respected.
But to sleep on the furthest outpost with an empty
gun and an lOU is only a case of a certain
class of individual rushing in where the seraphim
wisely take a back seat. If Australia had a ser-
viceable fleet of its own, and disaster to Australia
in time of war meant that 20 good war-vessels,
carrying the empire’s prestige with them, had gone
under, it could probably count on British assis-
tance simply on the ground that an outlying
squadron must be supported, and so large a part
of the empire’s naval defence must not be over-
whelmed merely for want of a reinforcement. It
is one thing to aid Australia in its own naval
defence, and quite another to undertake the whole
defence. In the former case, helping in the
defence of Australia might mean keeping the
trouble away from Britain’s own shores; in
the latter, it would merely signify sending
away a fleet to fight a battle at a distance
which could be fought to much greater advantage
in British waters. Britain has little sympathy
with the White Australia ideal, and if the ideal
threatens to bring it into trouble with a dan-
gerous naval power, it will probably think it easier
to coerce Australia which has no ships than the
other State which has many. A White Australia
ideal, with a fleet behind it which could beat
anything else grown south of the line, and even
hold a not altogether hopeless argument with
Japan, would appeal to Britain. A country which
is so completely dependent on the command of the
sea would be appealed to by almost any ideal with
such a naval reinforcement behind it. It is all a
question whether the White Australia ideal is
worth fighting for. The Bulletin reckons it is
worth fighting for to the last man and the last
copper; that it is worth while, for its sake, to
import white population wholesale, to build up
manufactures by prohibitive tariffs so as to find
work for the population, to break up big estates
so as to find land for the population, to pay off our
debts so that we will have credit in the day of
trouble, to train every able-bodied citizen to arms,
to become a naval power, and to pay for all these
necessaries in hard cash.
It is a fact that is difficult of realisation in this
easy-going land that Australia is just as well fitted,
so far as money is concerned, to support a power-
ful navy as Japan is. It has practically the same
revenue as Japan; its people are more lightly-
taxed in proportion to their means; and it has
great land revenues, which Japan does not possess.
The resources are all here, but Japan has the
navy, the military force, the arms and ammunition
factories, the iron and steel works, the ship-
building yards to turn out its own iron-
clads, the fast-growing population, and the
fixed resolve to find work for that population
by building up its own industries Japan
has all these things, and Australia has the horse-
race and the football match. Australia has more
cause to think hard over its position than any
other white section of the empire, and it seems to
think less than most others. If it is merely a burden
in time of war it will be the first place to be aban-
doned—it is the most remote, the one with the
largest coast-line to defend, and the one with the
fewest men in proportion to its coast-line. More-
over, it is the section with which a mostly nigger
and much nigger-loving empire is least in sym-
pathy, and which that empire will drop with
the least compunction. But over and above all
these general considerations there is the broad
hint conveyed in the fact that Britain has begun
to call in its naval resources, and is apparently
going out of the Pacific except for show purposes,
and leaving it a Japanese ocean. Also, Britain
is withdrawing its troops from Canada, which is
another calling in of resources, and another veiled
suggestion to the self-governing States of the
empire that they must hustle for themselves
much more than heretofore.
MORE FEDERAL CAPITAL.
At time of writing the Federal Capital question
had got no further forward, and N.S.W. State
Premier Carruthers is still the obstruction. A
few days ago Sydney Telegraph put the case
baldly and plainly in these words :
The present position is that an impasse has been
reached. The Federal Parliament shows no sign of
abating its condition that an area of 900 square miles
should be cut out of New South Wales for the capital,
which the Parliament of this State has not acceded to,
and is not likely to accede to. The Federal Parliament
declares that Dalgety shall be the site, while the New
South Wales Parliament positively bars Dalgety by re-
fusing to include it among the sites offered.
That was evidently meant to support the obstruc-
tionist attitude which Carruthers has induced
the N.S.W. Assembly to take up, but in reality
nothing much more unpleasant could have been
said about the paltry, shuffling little mis-
chief-maker who disgraces the highest political
office of the State. In selecting the site it did,
the Australian Parliament kept literally to that
proviso of the Constitution which sets forth that
the Federal city is to be in N. S. W. and 100 miles or
any greater distance from Sydney. In askingfor9oo
square miles the Australian Parliament put a very
moderate interpretation on the clause of the
Constitution which provides that the Federal
capital area is to be of any size providing it is not
less than 100 square miles. In all the negotiations
so far the Commonwealth Government and Par-
ment have acted with moderation, fairness and
courtesy, and the N.S.W. Government, and a
section of the N.S.W. Assembly, and a consider-
able portion of the Sydney press, have been un-
couth, surly and bear-like to the last degree.
* » * *
In the beginning of the negotiations the Federal
Government asked the N.S.W. State Government
to suggest a site which it regarded as suitable,
and it surlily refused to take any interest in the
matter. It was months before it could even be
induced to say clearly that it wouldn’t suggest
anything. While this negotiation—if it could
be called a negotiation where one party refused to
negotiate—was going on, and during the period
that followed, the anti-Federal press of Sydney
was full of bitter denunciation to the effect that
the conduct of the Australian Parliament in not
rushing ahead and selecting a site was evidence of
a “Victorian conspiracy ” to keep the capital in
Melbourne. In the end the N.S.W. Government
so far emerged from its attitude of grumpy isola-
tion as to do something. It refused to suggest a
site, or to take any responsibility, but it appointed
a State officer to report on the various districts
that had been suggested. He reported in favor of
the Monaro tableland as distinctly the best, and
this so angered Sydney Telegraph, which de-
sired_ a capital right at the back door of Sydney,
that it went the length of publishing an article, in
which it declared—this when the official’s own re-
port in favor of Monaro was published and avail-
able for everyone to read—that the report really
gave preference to Lyndhurst. The State Parlia-
ment also (still without taking any responsibility)
reserved certain sites for the Commonwealth legis-
lature to select from, one of these being the
Dalgety position, which it now refuses to
recognise in any way. In fact, Carruthers
has the effrontery, despite the published
Gazette notice, to deny that this site ever
was reserved. The Australian Parliament, not
being able to obtain any direct advice from the
N.S.W. Government, either as to the position of
the Federal territory, its size, its accessibility to
the sea or otherwise, followed pretty closely the
theories of the officer whom the N.S.W. Govern-
ment had appointed, and chose a district in
Monaro, which the N.S.W. Government had set
aside, with others, tobe selected from. While it was
dealing with the matter the political atmosphere
still drizzled curses from the Sydney Telegraph
and News about the slowness of its procedure, and
about the “Victorian conspiracy of delay.” When
it had finished—the selection was finally made by
a Government with a N.S.W. man for Premier,
and with only one Victorian in its composition—
the N.S.W. Parliament simply gave a flat refusal,
and then closed up and went away home for a
rest. The Australian Legislature also closed up
for its annual rest, and meanwhile a section of the
Sydney press drizzled curses as before on the
Commonwealth Legislature for its alleged failure
to “act.”
* • * •
When the selection was first made Pre-
mier Carruthers declined to surrender the
proposed site—in the region recommended by the
N.S.W. official appointed to deal with the
matter—because the surrender would deprive
N.S.W. of 40,000 inhabitants, but it was promptly
demonstrated by reference to the census that it
wouldn’t deprive the State of anything like half
that number. Carruthers also declared that
N.S.W. wouldn’t dream of giving up the im-
mense revenue it derived from the district, and he
was convicted of another falsehood, inasmuch as
it was proved that the district yielded less
than the amount spent on it, and was
a dead loss to the Treasury. Just now
Carruthers complains that the region is
too poor and miserable altogether, and that the
Federal Parliament deliberately chose the worst
site—though the N.S.W. official report described
the district as the best—in order to create delay. •
Also, he remarks that it is an outrage that N.S.W.
wasn’t consulted—well knowing that it was con-
sulted over and over again ; and there is again a
plaint about the Australian Parliament’s tailure
to “act”—which it could hardly do, even if it
hadn’t acted already, because it wasn’t sitting till
the other day, its Premier (Reid, of N. S. W.)
having neglected to call it together. Furthermore,
there is much violent language about the pro-
posed site being selected by Victorian influences
and being nearer Melbourne than Sydney—this
despite the best possible evidence that Dalgety is
a good many miles closer to Sydney than it is to
Melbourne. And yet Tumut, which is further
from Sydney and nearer to Melbourne than 'Dal-
gety, and which would be a much greater loss to
N.S. W. than Dalgety could be, is now deliberately
offered by Carruthers. On the evidence there is
only one possible conclusion—that Carruthers
seeks to advertise himself as a strong Premier by
being cantankerous in a petty, childish way, and
that whatever decision the Australian Parliament
had come to he would have been cantankerous,
just by way of showing his little, brief authority.
Moreover, his alarm about the Federal capital
helped to distract attention for a few days from
the fact that the Bill to enable the Land Commis-
sion to call on Mr. Willis to give evidence had
not been introduced, and that, without such a Bill,
Mr. Willis had every chance, if he felt that way,
to become a very scarce Willis indeed.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The N.S.W. Land Scandals.
Now that W. N. Willis is safely out of Australia,
the N.S.W. Government will, no doubt, make the
most serious of efforts to ensure that the Lands
Inquiry is made as searching as possible. With
W. N. Willis out of Australia, and with most of
the documents and books which might throw light
on lands maladministration destroyed, an enthusi-
astic eagerness for light can be quite safely dis-
played ; for there is practically no fear of the
darkness_ being dissipated to show up those now
lurking in the shadows. But this belated dis-
play of robust courage will deceive no one. There is
no merit in being brave when the occasion for action
has passed. The N.S.W. Government has several
highly ingenious explanations for its failure to
push home the inquiry into the land scandals at
the time when the Sims case gave a clue which,
if promptly followed up, would have led to a full
disclosure of the truth. And, to excuse its
latest dilatoriness, it uses freely, and, in many
people’s opinion most unfairly, the name of
Justice Owen. When the Royal Commission was
first appointed, with obviously grudged and insuf-
ficient powers, the answer to the agitation for such
enlargement of those powers as would have made
the inquiry reasonably efficient was always that
J ustice Owen was satisfied ; that he had asked for
no further authority, and, until he did,
it would be improper to give it to him.
As if it were the Judge’s place to come
into the political arena, and suggest to the
Government an executive action ! Justice Owen
had no call to interfere in that direction ; his busi-
ness was to make a judicial inquiry into the facts
by the means supplied to him. The Executive’s
business was to see that those means were suffi-
cient. After Justice Owen’s mantle had been
used as a shield for some time, the Government
reluctantly extended the scope of the inquiry; by
that time many of the documents necessary to
elicit the truth had been destroyed. Then came a
demand for more effective methods of dealing with
recalcitrant witnesses, and again the Government
took shelter behind Justice Owen. The witness
W illis prepared to leave Australia. That fact was
the best test of the sincerity of the Government, and
on the test the Government failed. No step was
taken to keep Willis in Sydney, and again the
Government gave Justice Owen as its reason.
As to how far Justice Owen was persuaded to act
as a mask, there is a good deal of doubt. In the
N.S.W. Legislative Assembly various statements
have been made by Ministers, the implications
from which are that the Government consulted with
JusticeOwENastowhetherWiLLis could be brought
back as a witness or to answer a criminal charge ;
and that Justice Owen said “No.” Now, did
Mr. Owen express any opinion on the criminal
charge matter ? Most probably not, as it
would be singularly improper for a Judge
to give to a Government an opinion as to
the issue of a criminal warrant in a case
which was at the time under his civil review.
Whatever the truth, the N.S.W. Government
certainly is using Mr. Owen’s authority to the
best possible advantage as a shield. But in the
cold, dry light of the facts, not the skirts of half-
a-dozen Judges could protect the Cabinet from
condemnation. That it has paltered with this
matter, and delayed and hampered the inquiry,
is, and is likely to remain, the public conviction.
Again Mr. Tom Mann.
This is the sort of stuff that Mr. Tom Mann is
talking in Victoria nowadays :
Perhaps someone would inquire : What does Tom Mann
talk of—physical force ? Yes, and he was saying that in
his own name, and those who did not like it could “ lump
it. It was high time that a lot of the namby-pamby
twiddle-twaddle going on in the Parliaments gave place
to work—(cheers)—and he would tell the Melbourne
unemployed that they were “dead slow,” and that the
Labor party was “dead slower.” (Cheers.) Thev could
make things hum if they would, but they did not,'and he
would add that it was high time those who professed and
called themselves Socialists showed what mettle they were
made of, and did not talk so much. No bigger farce was
being played than the Parliamentary farce ; but it was
certain that Parliamentary institutions were on their
last legs. (Cheers.) He believed that Parliamentary in-
stitutions were the homes of mug-wumps and general
bosses.
And the same Mr. Tom Mann, very recently—
alas, how recently ! —was the paid servant of the
Victorian Labor bodies, to orate on behalf of the
Labor party that is “dead slow,” and of the
“Parliamentary farce,” and the “ Parliamentary
institutions that are now on their last legs. ” When
Mr. Mann was drawing £3OO a year to tongue-
wag on behalf of the Labor party, he didn’t seem
to recognise that it was “ dead slow ” ; or that the
Parliament he was urging voters to return Labor
members to was a farce; or that Parliamentary
institutions were on their last legs. Possibly Mr.
Mann is sincere, and always means, at the
moment, what he is saying. If that is so, then
his think-engine must have a habit of stopping
when his talk-engine gets going. Anyhow,
whether he is a talker ready to talk anything that
pays, or a reckless, thoughtless whirler of words,
Mr. Mann’s influence on the Australian Labor
movement, which he is supposed to champion,
has been altogether disastrous.

N.S.W. Railways and the Pawn Ticket.
To judge by the things that were printed, the
other day, when some of the figures for the
N.S.W. Railways’ financial year were published,
triumph had been achieved. The remarks that


were made seemed to suggest that those who had
thrown scorn at the old “ permanent and repro-
ductive works ” gag were left at last without a leg
to wave or an arm to throw more scorn with. It
only shows how little is needed to make a triumph
in N.S.W. finance. It isn’t necessary even to
succeed; it is only necessary to make a rather less
disastrous failure than usual. Here are some of
the hard, merciless facts of this railway situa-
tion :
(1) At the end of the financial year 1903-4 N.S.W. owed
Cohen £80,970,962, of which £47,903,758 was in railways
and tramways. The other £33,067,204 was sunk in other
things—including sand-shifting, paint on park fences, &c.
This 33 millions odd cost £1,260,000 in interest; while its
net earnings amounted to only £435,298. Thus only 59 per
cent, of the N.S.W. debt is represented by railways and
tramways: and the other 41 per cent, is only earning
about 6s. Bd. in the £ of the interest upon it that Cohen
calls for.
(2) As for the railways and tramways, which represent,
even nominally, only 59 per cent, of the N.S. Wales debt,
they earned net last year, before paying interest,
£1,619,756. The Auditor-General showed in his last
annual report that for 1904 the interest on the interest-
bearing capital in the undertakings was £1,640,270. This
year it will be about £35,000 more. This will leave a
deficit of £55,514.
(3) In the lean years it was said that the fat years would
level things up and wipe out the deficit. Last year was
one of the fattest years the country ever had, with phe-
nomenal harvests in all parts; and yet it ended with
another railway deficit of £55,514.
(4) For the past 14 years there have been only two years
in which there hasn’t been a railway deficit —and there
were railway deficits for years before that; only the Audi-
tor-General’s figures go back no farther. For 13 years, to
the end of 1903-4, the gross net shortage was £2,154,190.
But instead of the last fat year wiping out any part of the
shortage, it bloated the deficit still further, and made it
£2,209,704.
Yet, in spite of all this, there are cheerful idiots
who believe that N.S. Wales only need go out into
the street, and whistle, and a mysterious syndi-
cate will rush up and take over the whole public
•debt in return for the railways ; wherefore, con-
tinue these polite lunatics, why worry about the
public debt ? And less frank but more dangerous
persons, like Carruthers and his crowd, while
they don’t say this, act as though they believed
it; and the public watch Carruthers add another
couple of millions a year to their vast I O U, and
hardly say a word. Even when they set out to
throw bricks at him they don’t throw this brick.

The Truth About North Queensland.
Australia, as a whole, has been lied about pretty
consistently by the Stinking Fish party, but
North Queensland has been lied about with extra
vigor. The daily press represents it ag a lurid
locality, in comparison with which Hell is as com-
fortable as a Collins-street tea-shop, and has
declared that cane - trashing there is merely an
unpleasant way of committing suicide. These
papers have not, however, published themselves
in North Queensland, wherefore the opinion of a
scientific man, who has lived up North for years,
and who knows his nigger and his climate and his
cane-field, ought to be of slightly greater value.
Consequently one inclines to listen to the remarks
of Dr. T. F. Macdonald, of Gerald ton, N.Q.,
representative of the Johnstone River Central
Mill Association, made recently at the Queens-
land Farmers’ Agricultural Conference. Dr.
Macdonald has the bad taste to differ from the
Melbourne Argus, and the S.M.H., and D.T., and
the other papers that go to so much daily trouble
to keep the white man out of the Australian
tropics. “ The climate of North Queensland, cor-
rectly understood and intelligently handled, is,”
he says, “not only the best, but absolutely the
very best, climate in the whole round world.”
Postulating always that it must be “intelligently
handled,” he is enthusiastic over its qualities :
In the tropics of Queensland nature is lavish of every-
thing, and particularly lavish of the basic elements of
agriculture, viz., land, water, sunshine. It would almost
seem that nature, fearful of having overdone the thing in
sunlight, hastens to correct her error by sending us the
wet season in the very height of summer heat. While
our brothers south of the tropics are being broiled alive,
and those of the western tablelands are frizzling under
the naked sun, we in the glorious north find the heavens
heavy with clouds, the air cool with rain, and drains and
backyards flushed with floods. If we suffer, it is from
having too much of good things.
Clearly this Macdonald individual should be
put down. He smashes cherished idols without
mercy
Man himself forms no exception to the tropical rules of
development. It is a matter of simple fact that people,
under otherwise healthy conditions, develop both in body
and mind in the tropics. Note the development of those
tall, slim children, born of white parents in hot regions.
They grow mostly into young giants ; and, even as
strength comes to the men, so does health and beauty
come to the women, when a reasonable observance of
tropical hygiene obtains in immediate social surroundings.
And this is that North Queensland which was full
of corpses and dry bones and gibbering things,
that scared the average spectator half way to
Kadesh ! Somebody must be lying, and it must
be this doctor. The Fat Press couldn’t lie. It
didn’t lie about Chowth Africa or the wreck of
the Petriana, or the Six Hatters. Further, in his
pestiferous report
In my experience, all races of men, all shades of color,
thrive equally well in North Queensland ; and all alike are
subject to the one adverse element of the tropics, viz.,
tropical disease.
Thv! deplorable person contends that the white
and the Kanaka and the Chow and the Jap and
the mongrel stand on the same physiological
level; and that the climate doesn’t walk round
the other way when it sees a nigger or a half-caste
coming:—
It is not the climatic conditions, not the heat, mois-
ture, nor peculiarity of sun rays that sap the life of men
and women and children in the tropics. For the colored
races are not immune to tropical diseases any more than
the white man. I will further contend that many tropical
diseases are not natural to North Queensland, but have
been introduced by colored peoples ; leprosy, for instance,
by Chinese, and ankylostomiasis (or earth-eating disease)
by Arabians.
Dr. Macdonald proceeds from this point to con-
sider these various diseases and suggest remedies.
His own special study has been the earth-eating
disease, which he regards as vastly more serious
than has been generally supposed. But the larger
conclusion of his whole story of tropical phe-
nomena is that there is absolutely nothing in the
theory that a colored skin is the right skin for the
country.
Australia Imperturbe!
All eyes are open but those of Australia. The
rest of the world notes the unfolding of the Asiatic
power and sets what it means. Here and there
is bestowed on Australia a glance of the con-
temptuous pity which is cast to the beast already
at the shambles. The Englishman, the principal
Anglo-Indian newspaper, commented the other
day:— , >
Is it at all likely that Japan would covet India ? Surely
not while Cochin China, Siam, the Philippines, and
Malaya, to say nothing of Australia, lay, so to speak
under her very nose? The perturbation of Australia has
long been noticeable and is entirely comprehensible. The
establishment of Japanese supremacy in the Far East
raises Imperial problems connected with Australian
defence which can only be solved by the statesmanship of
genius, if at all.
And the comment is just, except in so much as it
refers to the “perturbation” of Australia. The
Englishman has probably got an idea that Aus-
tralia is perturbed from the efforts of The Bul-
letin to make it so; but the idea is, unhappily,
inaccurate. A very small section of Australia
shows any interest in the matter of its threatened
destruction. The great bulk of the people give
their minds as usual to the cricket scores, the
boat race, the football figures, and the weights for
the Melbourne Cup. Not a warship is being built;
not a gun forged ; not an extra soldier trained ;
not an effort made to lighten the fetters of debt
which will leg-iron our steps when the time comes
to march out to fight; not one bloated landlord
has been rooted out of his run to make place for a
village of yeomanry. Australia perturbed ? Not
a bit of it. The London money market for
loans is improving. The Labor party has
sunk the fiscal issue again. The land agents’
doings are giving the people something to talk
about. And Armstrong has made 303, not out.
Australia perturbed ? No; it drifts to its fate
calmly. It is infected with “national agnos-
ticism.”

Westralia and its I 0 U.
Till the t’othersider came, and for some years
even after that, the auditor was one of the very
rare birds of Westralia. Even the Westralian
Bank didn’t have an audit, or, if it had one, it
never published the results. The State had some
sort of an Audit Department, but as it had been
years since even the Savings Bank accounts were
overhauled, all that the department amounted to
didn’t matter. Later on, three years ago, they
borrowed a man from Victoria to tell them
whether things really wanted auditing or not ;
and the report was hot enough to scorch the
pigeon-hole into which a scared Minister stuck it
while he sat down to think. One result was
that a real auditor was appointed to do real
auditing ; and the things he has just begun to
say make interesting reading. Take the railway
accounts, for instance. The annual reports have
shown profits running up to 11'48 per cent, on
total capital expended; last year 4 - 56 per cent,
was claimed. On the strength of these figures not
only has the Government gone to Cohen for
another trifle at various times, but the big
absentee co.’s have perseveringly bitten the
Government’s ear for reduced freights, and the
biting has mostly been successful. Now, however,
the Auditor-General tells Parliament, in his first
report, that it has never yet been informed of the
real position with regard to the railways, and
doesn’t know whether they have been a suc-
cess or a failure. It is certain that the true
position isn’t nearly as bright as the published
figures have shown, and it is on the strength of
these figures that the concession-hunters have had
their demands granted. Thus, though Westralia
has repeatedly paid through the nose in its pawn-
ing transactions, the railways on which the loan
money was spent haven’t been charged with the
cost of raising the money ; and when money has
been put into stores, the railways haven't been
charged with interest; also, they haven’t been
charged with stores which have become obsolete
and valueless. This means that when Bigjohn
paid £50,000 to borrow £1,000,000, he paid it out
of the loan, and made no effort to wipe the debt
out, but went on cheerfully assuming that it was
represented by something “permanent and repro-
ductive,” though it wasn’t represented by any-
thing which a nail could be driven into or an
IO U gummed upon. Also, it means that, when
Westralia put Cohen’s money into railway
stores, and the stores rusted, the loss wasn’t
made good out of revenue; the rust and
white ants were kept in the State’s balance-sheeet
as still worth 20s. in the £ of Cohen’s money.
Under any tolerably honest system of finance,
when the loan wasn’t represented any longer by
anything of value, the amount would have been
made up out of revenue, and the loan repaid—or
the amount put into a sinking fund, which
amounts to the same thing, provided the sinking
fund is nailed down securely enough. But the
intolerably dishonest finance system of Westralia
didn’t stop at that. “ Relaying,” says the Audi-
tor-General, “has been almost entirely carried
out from loan moneys.” Thus the State borrowed
money to build a line, and when that line was
worn out it borrowed another lot of money
to replace the first line, so that two lots
of debt and two lots of interest have
now to be carried by one lot of rails.
Presently, unless that disreputable system is
wholly altered, the second lot of rails and sleepers
and bridges will also be worn out; then a third
lot will be necessary, and that will mean a third
lot of interest. When that happens the people
who will be using the railway will be bled to pay
three separate Cohens ; if they can’t stand it, the
deficit will have to be made up out of taxation.
Any time and anywhere this double-banking of
the bill of sale, and this plastering of three IO U’s
on to one culvert, this making the son go on paying
interest on the bridge that his father wore out,
and on the engine that collapsed under the
weight of the loads chat somebody else’s grand-
father put behind it —anywhere and any time
these things would be preposterous and dis-
graceful finance. But in Westralia such things
are more preposterous and disgraceful than
usual, because the things that are worn out and
not paid for are worn out in skimming the cream
off the country—in gutting the mines of their rich
ores, and rushing the proceeds by the million to
their foreign owners—leaving the two or three
Cohens to be settled with by a new generation
that will have to make the best progress it can on
the skim-milk. All these things The Bulletin
has been saying for many years past; now that
Westralia is told practically the same things by
its Auditor-General, after an examination of the
accounts, the State will have less excuse than ever
for not doing something definite. And that some-
thing must take the shape of a severe remodelling
of the public service, probably an entire recon-
struction of the railway tariff, and certainly a
large increase of taxation. All these things are
unpleasant; but that doesn’t matter. It is only
in the Scriptures that the spendthrift prodigal can
feed On the fatted calf ; and even in the Scriptures
he had to go through a preliminary course of
husks.

The Canada Myth.
A pressman who has been there writes :
v l fc J s time that somebody pricked the Canada
bubble, and it would pay some Australian Govern-
ment or some enterprising Australian newspaper
to send a capable man to that cold spot to find
out tlm truth about it. Because the place is booming
with a fictitious prosperity, unthinking people have come
to regard its prosperity as genuine and lasting. But, of
all boom places, the Canadian Dominion is probably the
worst example. Its good points and the secrets of so
much of its success as is real are (1) the high, practically
prohibitive, tariff, which has enabled it to become, with a
comparative handful of population, one of the manufactu-
ring- factors of the world, and (2) its non-borrowing policy,
or if it isn’t a policy, at least its habit of not borrowing.
And these are the points generally overlooked by those
loudest in their wails that Australia should look to
Canada.
In its land and settlement policy, Canada is, has been
and is still, living on its capital and, when the day of
reckoning comes, it will probably he in a condition
several shades worse that the worst railway-ridden State
of Uncle Sam’s republic. The land available for settle-
ment under tolerably decent climatic conditions com-
prises a narrow strip, one-half of which has been given
away to a private company on the gridiron land grant
railway principle. The other half is being given away to
immigrants from other countries. So freely has this
largesse been dispensed that people who now go to
Canada in the hope of taking up land, find —like a num-
ber of discontented Victorians who tried the place—that
the nearest available blocks are 40 miles from the
nearest railway line (not station). The land offered to the
Victorians was certainly prairie land, and that did away
with the trouble of clearing ; but there were no roads,
and during four to six months of the year it was
absolutely snow-clad. The taking up of such land may
be an excellent speculation for a man with money enough
to hold for a rise, but for the farmer who wants his next
season’s crop to carry him forward to the following year,
it is a cold proposition indeed.
Every writer on Canada, and every person who has
visited the place, either dodges the issue for the man with
only his labor to sell, or else tells a doleful tale. It is
admitted that there is only six months’ work in every
year for the farm laborer, and during the other half year,
when the place is under snow, he has to crowd into the
cities and loaf out his time on what he has saved. Agri-
cultural Canada is essentiallj- a place for the man with
capital enough to start a well-equipped, up-to-date farm
and wait for extended transport communication with
his markets. Even under the rotten system of land
settlement that has hitherto prevailed in Australia, the
lack of rainfall in certain districts and the necessity of
clearing land, this country stands miles ahead of such a
place as the Dominion, where nothing can be done during
half the year and the cattle have to be treated with a care
the Australian cocky does not give his own children.
Canadian soil is productive as far as cereals are con-
cerned, but, as the land is worked, its produc-
tiveness will certainly fall off. The working man who
is not a man engaged in one of the manufacturing indus-
tries had better give Canada a wide berth, and, if Aus-
tralia had a sufficiently high tariff to establish the same
industries, she would get just as big an influx of artisans
anxious to improve their condition. As for the farmer
with capital, there is nothing in the land now available
in Canada to take away any man who can get a block in
the Riverina.

The N.S.W. Lands Commission Again.
Some very curious things have happened in
N.S.W. Assembly over the Bill to extend the
powers of the Lands Commission so far as to
enable it to compel witnesses to attend and give
evidence. The Bill was admittedly aimed in part
at W. N. Willis, the most interesting among all
the probable witnesses, who is on his way to
South Africa. Willis was delayed in Westralia
by two warrants issued against him by a private
N.S.W. citizen, and while this delay lasted,
and while Willis was still in the country,
the Bill which might have given the Court
power to call him back to give evidence was
not introduced. When he was discharged by the
Westralian Court, and in a fair way to resume his
voyage to Africa, the Bill was brought up with an
aspect of wild-eyed, wild-haired haste, and it was
proposed to suspend the Standing Orders that it
might be rushed through with excitement and
celerity. But various things got in the way of the
rush. The first day a Government supporter
blocked progress with an adjournment motion re
somebody named Murphy. The second day the
Carruthers Government itself postponed things
for a few hours, and the haste began to
evaporate badly. Then the Government assumed
an unexpected attitude, and said that it couldn’t
bring W. N. Willis back to give evidence,
because the Commission had given him leave to
go away. This struck the public as quite a new
departure. All that the public knew was that
W. N. Willis called in on the Court one
morning, and declared that if he couldn’t
give his evidence there and then he wouldn’t
be available for some weeks, as he was
about to undergo medical treatment (which
isn’t quite the same as leaving for Africa),
whereupon the Court assured him he would
have an opportunity to be heard later on (which
isn’t exactly the same as leave of absence in a
foreign country). Justice Owen, who presides
over the Lands Commission, also emphatically
contradicts the Government, and says that he
“never sanctioned, recognised, or was aware”
that W. N. Willis intended to leave the State
until he was informed by Minister Ashton that he
was actually gone. One way and another so many
things are happening that it begins to appear as if
the idea that change of air will do W. N. Willis
a lot of good isn’t wholly W. N. Willis’ idea;
and it also looks as if he may be back a
long time before there is any unanimous desire for
his return.

Britain has been in a great fluster through a
report that a foreign syndicate was reaching out
for certain South Wales coal mines; and Bal-
four’s Government was driven by the sudden
fury of the fluster to announce that it wouldn’t
allow anything of the sort to happen. Poor old
Britain has to slough more and more of its Free-
trade folly. Once it opened its arms to the world
and said, “ Let ’em all come.” Now, in the course
of a month or so, it has taken steps to block the
undesirable pauper and the undesirable foreign
capitalist. And, having begun, it won’t stop till
it has been consistent and blocked also the
undesirable joint - product of the two of
them. A curious side issue of this coal
matter, by the way, was the cry of the
S.M. Herald that Britain must take strong
measures to obliterate the German syndicate and
all such syndicates, or it would be lost. Yet that
German syndicate was simply accused of invest-
ing foreign capital in England to develop the
great natural resources of the country—at least
this is the way the S.M. Herald puts it when a
foreign syndicate buys up Australian mines or
other Australian industries. Why does the S. M.
Herald reckon that a policy is good enough for
Australia yet not good enough for England ? That
paper reckons that England should get rid of the
German syndicate in a high-handed and energetic
manner, but when Australia gets rid of a foreign
syndicate, the same paper says that capital is
being driven out of the country by disastrous
Labor legislation.

The Australian (meaning Victorian) Women’s
National League stands committed, through its
secretary, Miss Watson, to a policy of profound
disrespect for the Chamber of Manufactures
party, that commends local products to local
patronage. Charles Atkins, president of the
Chamber, lately referred to the League as
being mainly a body of genteel Toorakiennes,
willing to turn up their toes rather than
tread their native land in “colonial”-made boots.
To which the gilt-edged Miss Watson replied
that he had better be more careful in his remarks
or he would “ alienate ’’ those members of the
League who have thusfar condescended to buy
“colonial” goods, and “whose purchasing power
must represent a considerable sum in Victoria.”
The League, on this showing, is almost as much
of an anti-Australian organisation as Charles
Atkins declared it to be. If the feelings of the
majority are further insulted they will require the
minority to boycott local manufactures by con-
fining their fairy tootsies to imported shoes, and
rejecting all the jams and pickles and things
which have not been branded in Britain. Mean-
while, the encouragement of Australian manufac-
tures is silently permitted by the A.W.N.L.

Bendigo (Vic.) is just now raging at the council-
lors of Kyneton shire, in whose territory lies the
little township of Lauriston. The golden city
boasts a typhoid fever rate just seven times greater
than that of Melbourne, and repeated investiga-
tions have shown Lauriston to be the cause
thereof. Lauriston is situated on the banks of the
Ooliban, whence Bendigo draws its water-supply,
and Lauriston’s sanitary arrangements are de-
scribed as “ primitive, rudimentary and disgust-
ting.” Bendigo has been speaking firmly to the
Kyneton councillors ever since 1899, and the Board
of Health has also insulted them several times;
but Kyneton is a long-settled district of well-to-
do farmers, men of hoggish tendencies and
covv-like habits of thought, who view the
matter with the ox-eye of indifference. This
matter, by the way, has an indirect bearing on the
Federal capital question. The Commonwealth
asks for 900 square miles—-barely enough for a
city, a reservoir, and a water catchment area, so
that it can control its own water supply and keep
it clean. N.S.W. Premier Carruthkrs, on the
other hand, wants to give so small an area that the
water supply of the Federal city shall be in State
territory, and if the Federal city is poisoned through
the State allowing dead cows to accumulate on its
catchment area it will have no remedy save writing
letters of remonstrance. The Commonwealth has
already found that when it writes a letter to the
N.S.W. State Premier it often has to wait as long
as six months for an uncivil answer, and as for
getting anything really done, that is almost hope-
less. And it doesn’t want to have to send the
Federal army to pick the State cat out of its reser-
voir. It has also got to consider the fact that if
it accepts the miserable area that CarruThers
offers, it may have no reservoir at all, except by
the State Government’s permission, and it would
be undignified to have to hire a land agent to get
a grant of a bit of ground to keep its tank on.
