Thus “G.H.”: —
Old King Cole goes to considerable trouble and expense
in order to further his piebald-Australia fad. He pre-
sented me with an armful of statistical and informative
compilations, the other day, all bearing on the Chow
question, and all laboriously arranged by the ancient
book-hydrant himself. King Cole puts his finger on a
vital spot when he says in the yarn about “Cotton
Growing,” that “if we do not effectually occupy the
tropical half of Australia, in time we shall lose it by the
overflow of colored population from the congested coun-
tries to the north of us.” We have got to get there i rst,
and we’ve got to stay there, in order to prod the aliei?
back with a rifle-barrel when he commences to slop over.
Australia must breed more white men, and must attract
millions of better-class white immigrants, and must send
them up to garrison the North. Half of “Cotton-Growing”
is devoted to an exposition of the nobler side of the Chow
character, and King Cole injudiciously remarks (page 11)
that “it is nothing short of dangerous to infer that he
(the Chow) may be easily' forced or driven. No race upon
earth can be more stubborn when angered.” Yet King
Cole implores Australia to import a few million of the
stubborn heathen off-hand, merely in order that the
atmosphere may be filled with whirling hoes and large
clods of Chow profanity when Ah Mino’s pig-tail has been
accidentally pulled, or sacrilege to that effect. There
seems to be something lopsided about the argument that
we had better import the Chow by the shipload, because
he is such a quiet, industrious worker, and that we had
better be remarkably polite and obsequious to the same
vegetable person, for fear he should cave our skull in
with an axe. The two Chows seem to be in violent con-
tradiction of one another ; and Australia had better hang
lire about the importation until King Cole has properly
sorted out his wild, tame, fierce, gentle, angry, meek and
infuriated pagan.
Some suggestive figures have been published re-
garding Maoriland’s imports of clothing. In
1883 they amounted, at manufacturers’ cost, to
£2,013,565. By 1894 they were down to £1,559,966.
But since then there has been an increase, year
by year, of the foreign-made stuff. For five years
the figures have been :
1899 .. .. .. £2,123,135
1900 .. .. .. 2,420,241
1901 . .. .. 2,474,978
1902 .. .. .. 2,004,004
1903 .. .. .. 2,935,118
The Arbitration and other labor laws date from
1894. In other words, these laws date from the
year when the imports of foreign goods began to
increase. Up to that time the tariff had been
enough Protection against the cheap foreigner.
The cheap foreigner was, in fact, fought
with a Protective tariff and with his own weapons
—long hours and low wages. Then the labor laws
decided that those last two weapons should not be
used ; but they left the tariff where it was. They
stopped the Maorilander working long hours for
low wages, but they didn’t stop the Maorilander’s
competitors working long hours for low wages, and
they didn’t shut out the competitors’ long-hours-
and-low-wage goods. They partially disarmed the
local workman, without, to any extent, disarming
the local workman’s enemy. They didn’t carry
the process, as in low-tariff Australia, to the
absurd length of giving the foreigner an actual
advantage in many industries over the local man,
but they greatly reduced the local man’s advan-
tage. Woollen manufactures and the like are
still growing rapidly in Maoriland, but the above
figures show that they might grow a vast amount
more rapidly than they are doing. And no coun-
try should be satisfied until it is doing its best.
Merely to do well isn’t enough.
A letter from Vandemonia :
Hobart Mercury, which is among the stupidest and
least truthful of Australasian newspapers, is at all points
a typical product of Vandemonia. In the old days when
Jack Davies roared on the fringe of Vandemonian society
and politics, the Mercury was at least a positive weapon
in the land. When any man offended Jack Davies, him
the Davies paper straightway set out to suppress. The
suppression was without stint or scruple, and the method
uniformly pursued was the method of the unpopular
practitioner who stands at dark corners after nightfall
with a sandbag. Thus it happened that the various
comparatively honest and intelligent, persons who were
not of the Davies brand in politics and otherwise, were
mostly in hot water in Vandemonia. That day passed,
and Jack Davies passed, and the methods of the Davies
paper changed superficially. The old man had been
hot-blooded and courageous after his manner,
and when society cold-shouldered him he mostly
hit back, using language that was, at times, almost
beautiful to contemplate. The new generation showed
a Christian desire to patch up the family feud with
society, and in that high enterprise still persists. The
paper changed its snarl to a grin, and buried its battered
bludgeon in the back shed. The Mercury became respec-
table. Bill Sikes went out, and the reign of Pecksniff
was inaugurated. The Davies paper ran on rigidly moral
■ lines, with a rigidly expurgated dictionary. Politically,
however, it was and is still the same old print. It holds
strenuously to the opinion that the worker was never
meant to govern himself. Its ideal democracy would be a
multitude of serfs and a Legislature of haughty persons
with Blue Blood. It is a devout worshipper of Blue
Blood, and a devout believer in, the essential superiority
of money. Incidentally, of course, it is compelled to
prevaricate most damnably. It still, for instance, almost
daily deplores the ruin of Maoriland by socialistic
legislation. The idea that Maoriland is prosperous, the
Death is the end of life : ah, why
Should life all labor be ?
Let us alone. Time driveth onward fast,
And in a little while our lips are dumb.
Let us alone. What pleasure can we have
To war with evil ?

GALLAHER UP BELFAST,
BELONG TO NO TRUST OR COMBINE.
Mercury stubbornly refuses to entertain fora moment
Maoriland is Socialistic : ergo, Maoriland is in the soup'
The lamentable point is that the Mercury is honestly
typical of Vandemwiia. In Tasmania, where the old
Vandemoman tradition dies hard, things are goino- steadily
from bad to worse, and it rather looks as if the worst in
the not distant future, will be repudiation and State
bankruptcy. Already the State is not really solvent
There is an incredible incubus of unproductive public
debt ; a large part of the best land of the State is lying
idle in the hands of monopolists ; there is no sound scheme
of taxation ; there is noticeable no ideal of national
honesty and true citizenship ; and the present Government
of nonentities is almost the most ridiculous that was ever
boggled into existence in any Australian State. Having
its keen eye on Vandemonia, Maoriland may be cheerfully
forgiven for persisting in its evil course.

-V
If it were possible to look at a musical
comedy from a new point of view, then The
Bulletin might have something fresh as young
wattle to say of Sheridan’s last production at
Sydney Criterion, “The Earl and the Girl.”
Standing on one’s head gives one point of
view but not a cheerful point. There is the
bird’s eye view and the worm’s point of view, the
dyspeptic point of view and the optimistic out-
look. Sizing up “ The Earl and the Girl ” from the
ordinary point of view, the comedy is bright, far-
cical and successful. The collaborators of the piece
have corraled such characters as may be seen any
day in almost any out-of-door place in London.
An earl and a girl— the girl and scores of other
kinds, a dog-trainer and his girl, an iron-jawed
woman from a circus, a Yankee solicitor—this last,
by the way, is a creation. The writers didn’t seehim.
They just said, “ Let abounderbe,” and abounder
was accordingly. A gentleman whose name is
Crew-Boodle needs little introduction. He is on the
charge-sheet, otherwise the character cast, and his
quids are studded picturesquely about the tavern-
scape. An American heiress is almost the key-
stoneof thecomicarch. Jim Cheese, the dog-trainer,
is persuaded for a consideration to take upon himself
for one night only, the name, state, troubles,
debts, and affairs of the heart, of one Dick War-
grave, heir to an earldom. He does not know of
the troubles until they fall upon him as bricks
from the top of a factory chimney. Mr. Sheridan
acts the Shoreditch earl-apparent; in fact, he, Miss
Maud Amber and Winfield Blake give the Cri-
terion’s patrons a better three-shillings worth than
is good for any audience. Miss Amber has
some not very notable songs to sing and
act, and she does with them all that a beautiful
woman with an attractive voice may do. “By
the Shore of the Blue Mediterranean,” sung bv
Mr. Blake, is one of the sparklets of the night,
and is a capital ad. for a fairly deserving locality.
Miss Florence Faning, as the loud-voiced, bluster-
ing strong woman of a circus, is a capital study.
This young lady is showing distinction in her act-
ings of the unattractive roles allotted to her. Miss
Heba Barlow, as Liza, the dog-trainer’s bit o’
skirt, dovetails neatly into his humorous antics.
Other characters in the musical comicality deserve
mention, and may get what they deserve in another
issue.
At Sydney Royal “ The Midnight Mail ”
makes 60 miles an hour nightly, from a box-office
point of view, and there is no talk of a change of
bill.
* * * *
Sydney Philharmonic had the help of
Watkin Mills and co. in “The Messiah” at the
Town Hall on Saturday night; but the honors
went to the chorus rather than to the principals—
in the first part of the performance at any rate.
Or perhaps the triumph (for the very fine render-
ing of “And the Glory” reached that high point)
was conductor Hazon’s. But Hazon was a sick
man—too sick, after many months of wearing
illness, to keep the chorus up to the standard
which it reached in the early numbers. Mr.
Harold Wilde’s “Thou Shalt Break Them” and
Mr. Mills’ “Why do the Nations?” were con-
spicuous among the solos. So was the blare in
“ The Trumpet Shall Sound,” but not in the same
way. The trumpeter is rare ; and the cornetist
isn't a satisfactory substitute. Santley found one
when he sang the air in Melbourne, and the
veteran was so startled by the discovery as to turn
and pat the performer on the back. Miss Edith
Kirkwood and Miss Lonsdale helped to put red
marks around Saturday night’s performance.
* * SK-
At Sydney Her Majesty’s Andrew Mack
still battles through many vicissitudes from his
claim in Colorado to the earldom and the estates
of Kenmare, where the blarney comes from.
This “Way to Kenmare” is strewn with a
cynical villain who would improve matters if he
weren’t always so cynical, a charming heroine
whom Mack marries at the end of the fourth act,
a long and entertaining Dutchman, a jaunting
car, and a quantity of brogue ; and it is brightened
by four songs which Mack warbles sweetly at
various stages of the interesting journey.
* * * *
The Great Thurston, with an army of
subordinates, a ship-load of scenery, magic effects,
and costumes, opened at Sydney Palace on
Saturday night to a packed house. Mr. Edwin
Geach’s mouth won’t have to go down at the
corners when he thinks of the reception of the
magician and the receipts at the box office.
One of the most wonderful of the mysteries
to slow music is the unpacking of a beautiful
young woman from a box within a box.
One minute she’s on the stage, the next
a high Saratoga glides down a rope from the ceil-
ing, and within this are two trunks, and within
the inmost is discovered, after it is unpadlocked,
the brunette beauty. The beauty, by the
way, is Mrs. Thurston, an American, who
comes of a theatrical family. Her grand-
father was one of the first adaptors and
players of “Uncle Tom’s Cabin” throughout
America. His family was so large— seventeen or
so—that he needed little or no outside aid. From
Degree to baby in arms were his own kin. Thurs-
ton will keep the Sydney public wondering at
fresh wonders for some marvellous days to come.
*& * *
At Sydney Lyceum, in these regenerate
days, you can only see the world, the flesh, and
the Devil through a glass darkly. When The
Bulletin looked in, a lady was doing some extra-
high kicking, and showing quite as much under-
standing as is thought proper round at the Tivoli;
and then she turned a somersault. But, alas ! it
wasn’t warm flesh and blood—it was just a cold
cinematograph picture; the wickedness of the
world put through the freezing process, as it were,
and served up in a form guaranteed not to bite!
It wasn’t all dancing girl, however. There was
the boat-race—not much of it, but enough to
swear by, though for quality not to be compared
to some of the older pictures of which the bill is
mostly composed. Still it was a commendable bit
of hustling to pot even IGOft. of the race and
serve it up the same evening on a sheet of calico.
-X- « «- *-
In the intervals of watching a man trying
to keep the sharp points of the crayfish’s claws
from sticking through the paper, in the tram, the
writer pondered on the struggle to make things
look like something else. Down at the Sydney
Lyceum they were throwing pictures on a screen
and trying to make them look as though they were
alive; over at the Tivoli there was a crowd to see
Milo, who is very much alive, look as though she
were dead, or, rather, cold marble. And Milo
succeeded best. They perch her upon a pedestal
and set her in park-like scenes ; and she is for a
few too-brief seconds Psyche, or the brown Venus,
or the de Medici, or some other of the statued
triumphs of olden days that the artists rave about
and the cigarette fiend would strike matches on
if he could reach them. While Milo is settling
herself down into some new pose, Cruikshank fills
up the blank with a lightning sketch. It’s a
novel “ turn,” artistic and well-managed. Miss
Challis, a descriptive vocalist of considerable
dramatic power, has joined the people who get
their names printed in large letters; and gene-
rally the programme goes several dwt. to the ton
better than the last crushing.
In Spite of tedious waits between the acts,
“The Walls of Jericho ” held all the sympathies
of the crowd at Melb. Princess’s last Saturday.
Alfred Sutro doesn’t lash a new set of degenerates
with a new whip. They are the same de-
generates—in dress and manners brought up
to date whom Sheridan lashed with the
same whip in “ School for Scandal.” The
genial drunken satirist of yore treated them as a
joke, and the circumstances leading to Lady
Teazle’s retirement from the “School” were
humorously put. The whip was lightly applied.
Sutro, the modern satirist, presents the hero of
his play as a society preacher proclaiming the
moral corruption of the Smart Set into which he
chose to marry, whereas Sir Peter Teazle merely
called himself an old fool for being the husband
of a girl who got into the Set after she was
married. The new play is more directly forcible
than the old comedy in the first three acts, but it
ceases to be strong the moment the preaching
ends. The last act is merely a conventional re-
conciliation between husband and wife, the lady
being the reformed party. Seeing that they are
both going to retire to Queensland the author
might as well have let them attend one more
meeting of the Set—as the Teazles did —in order
to bow themselves out.
* * * »
The showy, eloquent character of Jack
Frobisher brings Herbert Flemming back to the
stage where he formerly represented a less serious
specimen of Queensland’s squattocracy in more
respectable English society. That was in
“Captain Swift.” A good actor (likewise an
Apollo) at that time, Herbert Flemming is now a
better actor, who impresses the local eye with his
resemblance to Surgeon O’Hara. The character
is just about up to his age and weight, in a sporting-
way of speaking, and he fires off his tremendous
volleys of caustic remarks very effectively. No Aus-
tralian millionaire in real life ever talked so well or
commanded so much respect for his manner of
putting his foot down. Brough is contrastingly
subdued in the quiet part of the other man from
Queensland. Carter Pickford does well as a pro-
fessional seducer who gets some encouragement
from Lady Alethea, until he grows too demon-
strative, and Miss Beatrice Day is essentially in-
teresting as that Lady. She lacks emotional
power and fine polish in her acting, but she has
magnetism and a handsome, high-spirited appear-
ance. Of the other ladies, Miss Temple is best
fitted to her part; and concerning one or two of
the other men, silence is the most charitable
policy. “The Walls of Jericho” will certainly
prosper.

At Melbourne Town Hall there is to be a
return of Hugo Heermann next week, when
Watkin Mills and liis Quartet will likewise appear
again. In the meantime Branscombe’s Glee Party
will supply one last extra farewell concert in
response to pressing request.
* * * *
“ Trilby,” revived for the second time at
Her Majesty’s (Melb.), is another reminder that
the original Australian production—Melbourne
saw it at the Princess’s—was supported by a
powerful Svengali in Reuben Fox, an excellent
Gecko, and an almost phenomenal voice—the
voice of Regina Nagel in its early richness. Also,
the drama carried more pictorial effect on the
Princess’s stage than it seems capable of else-
where. “ Trilby,” less picturesquely managed and
without a remarkable voice tohelpthehypnotic illu-
sion along, is a rather unsatisfactory drama, and the
third act, dedicated to the decease of Svengali, is
put to very poor account by Gaston Mervale.
Made up with a striped countenance—half white
and half the color of a raw steak—he doesn’t even
look dead when required. Miss Nance O'Neil at-
taches all the importance she can to the colorless
character of the barefooted, tone-deaf young
woman, and the Laird, Taffy and Co. look more or
less like the men Du Maurier sketched. After
the “Trilby” revival “Marie Antoinette” will
be played for a few nights.
* * * #
A good audience gathered on a cold, wet
Saturday night (July 22) to warm itself in the
radiance of “Woman and Wine,” at Melbourne
Royal. Play in question, though lacking the
first flush of novelty, continues popular as an
exciting, showy production in which Bland Holt
masquerades as the Eton-jacketed papa of a
bouncing, affable baby, addicted to kicking hard
with a pair of long, scarlet legs. Walter
Baker, as the gallant young Englishman inveigled
from “ happy home, fond father, and faithful
friend ” by a marble-hearted French adventuress,
looks, as heretofore, noble in the centre of the
limelight, especially when defending himself from
the charge of murder, in an elegant speech full of
alliteration. Another winsome trait in the prodi-
gal’s demeanor is his habit of “uncovering his
head in the presence of womanhood.” Let his
mood be what it will—madness, despair, defiance,
or desperation—a “pure, good woman” need only
approach and the chivalrous wastrel’s alabaster
brow is bared to the breeze, and kept like that till
the tableau is dispersed ! Real life is full of better
men with worse manners.
* • * *
Harrie Ireland sins gracefully in a series
of effective toilettes. Frances Ross and Mrs.
Bland Holt, as the good angels of the play, are
driven to console themselves with the conscious-
ness of superior virtue. In the matter of dress,
they are hopelessly outclassed by the frocky repre-
sentative of the world, the flesh and the Devil.
Mam’zelle of the Marble Heart looks well to the
last, fights for her life in a witching dance-
robe of golden hue, and is hidden by her mur-
derers in a basket of gorgeous blossoms. Arthur
Styan makes Caliban, the ghoul of the flower-
market, just the same uncanny, old sensationalist
as heretofore. Albert Norman is decorative as a
curled and scented miscreant, while Maurice
Kemp, as the blind old father, is so fussed
over by the good angels of his acquaintance that
lie reminds one of a rich old bachelor in a board-
ing house. Jennie Pollock depicts La Colombe,
the heroine of the flower-market tragedy, as a
fine, upstanding virago, proficient in the art of
wearing gay garments and knifing troublesome
rivals. “Woman and Wine” is located mostly
in Paris, but it has a special Horsetralian attrac-
tion in a race-course scene with real gee-gees and
a crook jockey.
* * * *-
The “Players’” three performances of “The
French Maid,” at the Bijou, confirmed the faith
of those who regard Melbourne as a nursery of
musical comedy. In comedy, without music, local
amateurs thrive somewhat amazingly on the in-
struction of Gregan M‘Mahon, and when dancing
is required as well as a reasonable capacity for
stage business there is plenty of material avail-
able from the schools. In this case Mrs. Green
had control of the terpsichorean catering,
and the result was a bright display of
leg work, with no crudity in any of it.
The music was not up to the level either of
dancing or acting. A ragged orchestra was prone
to lag behind the dancers, while the chorus-
singers strayed at times from the tune, and
looked shamefaced till they heard themselves
stumble back again on to the missing melody.
Last week’s revival saw Ada Reeve’s part
entrusted to Irene Dillon, already popular here-
abouts as a chic dancer of decorative stage appear-
ance. As the French Maid she achieved a quick
success, rendering her songs with point and arch-
ness, in spite of a heavy cold on her 17-year-old
chest, and maintaining throughout, the fizz, flutter
and flirtatiousness traditionally demandedof a Pari-
sienne chamber-maid. Early on the second night
the luckless Irene hurt her ankle, but she danced
prettily enough under the subsequent difficulties.
Gladys Hartwell made something of the oddities
of the Admiral’s lady, and Stuart Kilgour cum
others did satisfactory work. The performance of
the cockney sailor by one Greig—there were two
Greigs in the cast—was positively breezy. Lionel
M’Cartney was responsible for a clever dance of
the vigorous type, which when performed by an
amateur, is apt to set nervous spectators shudder-
ing lest the dancer’s more important garments may
go by the board, in emulation of his cuffs, belt,
etc. After saying these complimentary things
about the workers it is permissible to bemoan the
fact that they wasted their intelligence on “The
French Maid ” the weakest and most witless of
musical comedies.

The story of the Man who sold his Soul
to the Devil is of perennial and powerful interest,
and Melbournians will have an opportunity of
pronouncing upon Berlioz’s “ Damnation of
Faust” on Tuesday, Ist August, when the Phil-
harmonic Society will perform this strikingly
dramatic work. The society’s chorus, the male
portion of which has recently been much strength-
ened, will be assisted by a full orchestra, St.
Paul’s choir boys, and Miss Lilian Reid, Mr.
Gregor Wood, Mr. Horace Stevens, and Mr.
Henry Rofe. Mr. George Peake will conduct,
and Lady Northcote is to shed the light of her
countenance upon the occasion.

Adelaide still remains true to the Knight-
Jeffries combination, and it crowded the Royal
to see “Beaucaire,” and the up-to-date Silver
Monarch who requested that the Universe should
be put back on his account. Knight, in mere
modern trousers, w’as something of a novelty. At
time of writing he is engaged in chasing Mercia
round the Early Christian furniture, and the lady’s
speed is something extraordinary considering her
clothes and the piece of wood with which she is
ballasted. At the Tivoli a fair all-round show has
been enlivened by a slight unrehearsed blaze in
the Biograph apparatus. Heermann, the fiddler,
occupied the Town Hall at a tarilf curiously styled
“popular prices” (lowest admission 25.). Also
the two rinks—real ice and roller—compete
desperately against each other w'ith races and
hockey matches and fancy carnivals.
THE WALLS OF JERICHO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A man came up to “Jericho,” where all the “smart’*
folk dwell—
Jack Frobisher, from Queensland, with the means to cut
a dash ;
The people’s mannahs bored him, and their morals !—O !
the smell !
But he entertained them freely, and he lent them lots of
cash,
As squatters do, you know,
When they go to Jericho.
So the daughter of a marquis deigned to take him for her
mash ;
She married him for money, but she loved him fairly well,
And the twain begat a son within the Walls of Jericho.
Now, the Lady Alethea, making light of dear old Jack,
Flirted openly, and gambled in the Jerichoan way,
And she merrily informed him when she caught him
looking black
That she’d like him rather better if he’d look a little gay.
It’s a duty husbands owe
To the world of Jericho ;
Whilst their ladies call the piper, they must simply grin
and pay;
Seeking sinful consolations oil the matrimonial track,
And doing as is done within the Walls of Jericho.
Another man from Queensland came unwarily to pass
Among the gaudy wasters, and was taken by their style.
His friend, old Jack aforesaid, told him not to be an ass,
And warned him that the wasters were incomparably
vile.
But the man from down below
Lost his heart in Jericho.
Lady Alethea’s sister knocked him silly with her smile,
And he didn’t care a button for the warning words, alas !
He preferred to fall a victim to the thieves of Jericho.
Then Jack cut short the chapter of his fashionable life,
To make his home in Queensland, where is virtue —of a
kind.
He swore he’d take the youngster if he couldn’t take his
wife ;
She haughtily protested that she meant to stay behind
In the world of Jericho,
But her tears began to flow
When domestic recollections came a-crowding on her
mind ;
And she put a happy finish to their unconvincing strife
By fleeing with her consort from the stink of Jericho.
The story has no moral, for the wasters didn’t care,
And the other man from Queensland kept the other girl
in view;
The Duchess, and the Marquis with the scanty head of
hair,
And all the younger members of the pestilential crew,
They are still at Jericho,
There was nothing said to show
That they thought of coming Southward to infect the
Kangaroo,
Or shock the tender feelings of the simple native bear.
They are left, beyond redemption, in their putrid Jericho.
The Wild Cat Column.
The tin boom in Melbourne rests on insecure
foundations. Scarcity of tin in the world’s markets may be
admitted. Is north-east Tasmania capable of supplying
the deficiency? Judging by the number of floats, and
the things said about them, it should. Consequently
there ought to be a slump in the value of the metal. So
far, however, there has been nothing of the sort, and it
may be judged, therefore, that the world isn’t taking at all
seriously the statements made of the possible output from
the mines that have come on to the market. These floats
fall naturally into dredging shows and sluicing- shows.
When the history of Tassy’s tin-sluicing mines is looked
up, there do not appear to be any recent successes out-
side of the Pioneer. The record of co. failures is, on the
other hand, dismal, and it can be foretold with safety
that when the summer comes on there won’t be enough
water to go round, even if there was che tin to want
water. It is good advice not to buy into a sluic-
ing claim unless it has an assured water supply,
and unless it has been properly bored and sample 1
by people who can measure up the strength of an engi-
neering problem. Dredging claims require the same essen-
tials and a bit more. They want it shown that the bottom
is suited to dredging, that sunken timber is not plentiful,
that clay is absent, and that the tin can be saved in the
length of a dredge pontoon. No one comes out of this tin
business better than the Tas. Minister of Mines, who felt
it his duty to issue a warning against the “ wild cat ” side
of the boom.
* * * *
Present tin market is higher than the average
of any year in this generation. The jump has been re-
markable :
1896 .. £59 911 1901 .. £llB 12 8
1897 .. 61 8 0 1902 .. 12014 5
1898 .. 71 4 1 1903 .. 127 4 6
1899 .. 122 8 7 1904 .. 126 10 0

iere has consequently been a very notable accumulation
. liquid assets, of which another £200,000 has been put

from foreign parts to Australia itself; and that, again,
won’t happen until the Stinking Fish defamer of Australia

far they have hitherto encouraged the unpatriotic policy

have furnished the money to build the factories, &c., in
foreign countries, to supply Australian needs, w'hile his
own money has been accumulating in banks’ vaults until
the banks tell him they can conveniently do with no
more, and that for what they have they can only offer him
reduced rates ?

The accounts issued by this bank for many years were
a grim joke, capital and reserves which had long
since disappeared being kept in the balance-sheet, as
though nothing had happened ; and now the accounts, if
no longer a joke, are something of a puzzle. For many
years the profit made by the bank’s purchase of its own
deposits at a heavy discount was put to reserves, to be
eventually applied to writing down assets. The purchase
is still proceeding, the deposits are still at a heavy dis-
count ; yet no mention is made of profits from this
source. Presumably they are being used to write down
assets without mention of that fact being made. Six
months ago these old deposits totalled £4,369,950 ; now
£4,085,328, a reduction of £284,622. Of this, £254,000 or
thereabouts represented “A” deposits, paid off in one
lump at par, so that the profit from w 7 hat has been the
A. J.S.’s most profitable branch must have latelv been
less than usual. In 1893 the A.J.S. had £8,498,589 of
deposits. Since then, in 11£ years, £4,413,261 has been
paid off by realising securities, the loss on realisations
being made up by writing-down capital and from current
profit and profit on deposits. The bank is thus left with
£4,055,328 of the old trouble to still struggle with ; and
it hasn’t another £1,013,513 of capital available for writing
off and applying tow'ards the deficit on the sale of further
securities. If the remaining assets weren’t, therefore, any
better than those that have been realised, things would
look very blue. But it is pretty certain that, though things
look blue enough, the present assets are a much better
lot than the old ones. In the beginning of 1894, when
the A.J.S. was just getting its breath after the smash,
only one-seventh of its assets were liquid ; now nearly one-
fifth are liquid. The whole assets yield gross £3 19s. 3d.
per cent. ; and though there isn’t much profit in that,
still assets that yield even that much must include
some very fair ones, and the average can’t be so very
shocking. The new busine s is secured by the whole of
the assets, and as profits are applied to writing down
securities, this guarantee becomes gradually stronger.
As to the general business

security than that. Such a holding partly accounts for
the low earnings—£2 14s. Bd. per cent, per annum on
shareholders’ funds—not enough to hold out much pro-
spect of a dividend.

But though this bank is not in a position to earn more
than it did 12 months ago, advances having, in fact, de-
creased, it at anyrate isn’t paying interest, asmanyothers
are, on more money which it is unable to use. A decrease
of deposits may not look well for a bank, in view of the
comparative abundance of money, but it has its com-
pensations. Capital is £400,000 ; on that and reserves,
earnings have amounted to only £2 19s. Bd, per cent.,
which is low enough to suggest that it wasn’t only the
reserves which should have been written off a while back.
And it is not because this bank has a vast proportion of
its assets in an unprofitable liquid form that its earnings
are small; liquid assets represent, in fact, only 27? per
cent, of liabilities to the public, which is considerably
below 7 the present average among Australian banks.
There is, however, one feature which should give the
depositor reassurance; as much as 33 p.c. c ° u *u be
written off all assets (other than coin, &c., and State
securities) out of shareholders’capital. Put in another
w 7 av if the reserves and half the shareholders’ capital
were written off, the profits would be equal to £6 Is. 2d.
p.c. on the remaining funds, which is very nearly the
Commercial of Sydney’s return. To equal the retuin of
the Bank of N.S. Wales the reserves would have to be
wiped out and £238,650 of the capital, leaving £161,350
(or, all round, £3 4s. 6d. per £8 share) intact. That, of
course, is on the assumption that in arriving at the profit,
as shown in the very skeletonised published accounts, ade-
quate provision has been made for bad debts^

The paid capital, as written down, is now £2,212,631.
The net deficit being £1,177,672, this leaves £1,034,959 of
shareholders’ capital intact, accepting the official esti-
mate of the value of the Trust’s assets. There are 211,735
pref. £lO shares, w 7 hich, if w'ritten down to £5, would re-
present £1,058,675 ; also 190,563 ordinary shares which if
w'ritten down from 10s. to ss. would represent £47,641;
together £1,106,316; so that amount of writing-down
would about represent the real situation.
The Commercial’s business nowadays seems to
be managed with prudence. Twelve months ago the
whole of the furniture item w 7 as wiped out of the balance
sheet, and now £5OOO comes off premises account. It is
always good to see these items reduced ; yet, taking- them
all round, the assets of the new bank seem a very profit-
able lot; for, though 21 per cent, of the assets is repre-
sented by coin and money at short call, yet the gross
earnings on the average assets last half-year w 7 ere £3 14s.
percent, per annum after paying interest on deposits.
Expenses are, how'ever, somewhat high—the bank for-
merly laid itself out to do an enormous business, and did
it, and when it shrank it wasn’t possible to shrink
expenses correspondingly. Still, the business is growing
again ; in 12 months deposits have increased from
£3,190,667 to £3,650,771, or by £460,104, and a satisfac-
tory feature has been the growth also of advances from
£3,644,031 to £3,969,487, or by £325,456, at a time when
most such fig-ures have moved the other way. Profits
after appropriations towards the Trust’s deficiency have
moved thus:—

Seeing that the reserve fund amounts to less than one-
eighth of the paid capital, and that the sum now 7 required
to pay the dividend absorbs 93 per cent, of the whole pro-
fits, allowing a lay-by of only 7 per cent., and that nothing
whatever has at any time been written off the cost of bank
premises, the increase of the div. on ordinary shares to 4
p.c. seems, to say the very least, premature. If there
were any marked upward movement in profits, from
w hich it might reasonably be expected that much larger
additions to reserves would be made in the future, the
increase of div. might have some excuse ; but looking at
the figures as they stand, it is very hard, if not im-
possible, to justify it. The business is, no doubt,
profitable; but still the assets, taking them altogether,
are not of such a character as to do away with
the need to write down premises, for instance. On
the average bulk of assets, the gross earnings last half-
year were at the rate of £2 os. sd. per cent, after paying
interest; and that is not so high as to make it superfluous
to write off something for depreciation of premises, which
are taken in at cost (£254,245). It would be a better use
to which to put any surplus than rushing to increase the
dividend with it. On capital (£1,478,010) and reserves,
net profits per cent, per annum have lately moved in this
way

In mostly non-productive coin, bullion, and balances

deposit rates having fallen, advance and other accommo-
dation rates are not likely to be fully maintained. The
bank’s position is strong ; 32 per cent.' of all liabilities to
the public are represented by liquid assets ; and from the
depositors’ point of view the position is further strength-
ened by the large proportion of the shareholders’ funds in
the business.

The dividend absorbs 87 per cent, of the profit, leaving
0n1y 7 13 per cent, for reserves, and that lay-by is not large
enough—which means that the dividend should not
recently have been increased. Still in this matter the
bank’s position is rather different from that of most
banks, since its own funds in the business are unusually
heavy. No less than 48 per cent, of all non-liquid assets
(advances, premises, &c.) are represented by share-
holders’ funds ; in other words, those assets could slump
48 per cent, below the value at which they stand in the
books before depositors would be touched. And of all
liabilities to the public 35 per cent, are in liquid assets.
From the depositors’ point of view, the position is thus
exceptionally strong. With so little of the public’s
money to work with, and with so much even of what
there is earning little or nothing-, profits per cent, of
shareholders’ funds continue small: —

The advances have increased rather more than deposits,
and earnings should therefore look up; but, then,
Queensland banks are still paying a high rate for deposits ;
and, with their recently enlarged resources, keener com-
petition has set in among Australian banks generally for
business ; so the outlook for better profits is not bright,
after all. The institution looks as though it deserved
public confidence ; but public confidence (which means
deposits) won’t be so very much good to it unless there is
more confidence in Queensland, and until a tariff restored
to something like the old Queensland State level, or even
higher, sets up a keener demand for money to help on
industrial enterprises.
* * * •
B.H. Junction drill-prospecting has made a very
good start; a patch of good ore has been struck at 775 ft.
level, towards the British boundary. But the mine has
been worked to such a complete standstill that several
such strikes must be made before it can be regarded again
as a workable proposition. Mine is almost certain to bob
up again serenely some day, and show a big profit on
present low price of scrip, though it isn’t as low now as it
was when it was mentioned here a fortnight or so ago as
the cheapest thing on the Barrier. Holders will need
patience before they get anything in the shape of divs.
* * * r-
On thinking the matter over the Broken Hill
Prop, people have changed their plans, and will put up
the first zinc-smelter near the lead furnaces at Port Pirie
(S.A.) instead of alongside the coal in N.S.W. Costs pan
out about the same, and as the chief metallurgical staff is
at Pirie, that fact turned the scale.
* * * *
Published statement that the 12,000 nnabsorbed
shares in the new 7 issue of 51,200 shares in Mt. Dr.vsdale
(N.S.W.) have been taken in one lump by H. B. Coles is
not correct. Coles, it is said, takes up 6000, and a local
syndicate the balance. Matters aren’t quite so clear as
they, might be as to whether Coles, who has gone to Eng-
land with some sort of an option, pays his own expenses,
or whether the 6000 ninepences which the syndicate now
puts up are to be devoted that way. So far as can be
gathered, monthly calling power of 3d. a share in new
issue isn’t likely to be rigorously enforced while Coles is
away. But there isn’t anything in Coles’ agreement to
prevent the bagging of the Drysdale’s alleged rich ore.
* * # *
Burra, now Mt. Boppy copper mine, four miles
south of Boppy (N.S.W.), and on the western slope of
the same granite belt, again moves in the hands of
Connelly and Elliott. Old shaft (200 ft.), waterlogged for
years, has been baled, and shows a strong vein of
sulphides at the bottom. Upper levels, which have only
been scratched, also look like a profitable business ; but
rich ores are bunchy and patchy. Probability is there’s
something in the Burra not yet located ; and drives 100 ft.
north and south may throw some light on the matter.
* * * *
Golden Pole, Davyhurst (W.A.), is opening up
rather better than expected. At No. 3 level north, reef
petered out at 175 ft., but made again at 192 ft. It is
worth 32dwt. for 15in. wide, and is still showing in the
end. North drive at 40ft. level from new prospecting-
shaft followed the reef for 70ft., worth 2oz. for 15in. wide.
At this point a winze was put down 7ft. and proved reef
to be stronger and worth 7oz. Cyanide and slimes plants
are going up, and should come into use next month,
when profits will be materially increased, as about 800 oz.
monthly are being left in the residues.
* * • *
Kalgurli (W.A.) is getting its additional treat-
ment plant going. In May co. treated 5400 tons worth
60s. Bd. per ton for a profit of £BBOO, while in June 6200
tons worth 61s. 3d. realised a profit of £11,700. Full
plant is expected to be in working order by end of July,
and, in August, 8500 tons should-be treated for a profit of
£15,000. To date profits of £15,000 only have been
distributed quarterly. When increased plant gets to
work and cost of extensions ceases divs. can easily be
doubled.
* » * *
Perseverance shows the highest costs of any of
the big Kalgoorlie mines. Co. treated during 1904
151,900 tons at cost of 465. sd. per ton, which includes
nothing for depreciation. Chief item is treatment,
255. 3d. a ton, which is more than total local costs of
Fingall, Gwalia and Cosmopolitan. For two years running
no depreciation has been allowed, an extra large amount
having been written off previously, but a plant that
can’t treat ore at under 255. 3d. should have been on the
scrap heap long ago. Gwalia’s treatment costs are
exactly 20s. a ton less, and that means £151,900 more
profit on last year’s output. This gives some idea of what
a white elephant Perseverance plant is. Manager Klug
has, however, materially reduced costs already.
Victory United, Cuddingwara (W.A.), con-
tinues to waste money in sending directors, who know
little of mining, to inspect the show. Latest arrival is
C. A. Ring, who says mine is too uncertain to be relied on.
As a matter of fact, mine has been a sink since 1899, and
is no more promising after six years of prospecting. It is
now 7 said that the famous cable of May 22, giving assays
of 26dwt., was a hoax, sent to take in telegraph operators,
w 7 ho, it is alleged, w 7 ere giving prior information to
brokers. Said cable is reported to have been kept back
tw'o hours while 30,000 shares were bought. That day
real result of 2dwt. W'as cabled, and naturally there was a
scramble to get out. This sort of hoax may mislead inno-
cent as well as guilty, and is therefore hardly the thing.
But, anyhow, Victory United is a very lame duck, and
may safely be left to gamblers.

The Broken Hill Proprietary Block 14 Co. has
finally closed its extensive smelting and ore-reduction
works at Port Adelaide, and has placed the whole of the
machinery and plant in the hands of Messrs. Butterworth
Handcock and Co., Port Adelaide, for private sale. The
list covers engines, boilers, steam-pumps, lifts, elevators,
crusher, brick-making machines, weigh-bridges, ore
scales, iron chimneys, shafting, pulleys, piping, and a lot
of sundries. A post card to that firm will ensure an
extended catalogue by return of post.*

* * * *
The British Broken Hill Silver Mining Co.,
after careful consideration of all types of boilers, have
E laced an order with Babcock and Wilcox for three large
oilers, each of 500 h.p. Superheaters, chain grate
mechanical stokers and economiser have also been
ordered from the same firm. This now makes 40 boilers
of the Babcock and Wilcox type supplied to the various
mines and industries at Broken Hill. Wherever economy
in fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. Hence the recent unanimous decision of the
Expert Admiralty Committee to install Babcock boilers as
the standard for the British Navy.*

* * * *
The Wilfley Slime Table which has been in
operation on the Ivanhoe Gold Mines for some months
past has been giving extremely good results. Mr. H. T
Brett, Metallurgist of the Ivanhoe Gold Corporation, in an
article contributed to the March number of the Chamber
of Mines Journal, W.A., gives particulars of the savings
made, and states that the introduction of the Wilfley
Slime Table will be of great service in treating the low-
grade sulphide ores of the field. Since the future of
Kalgoorlie is so intimately bound up with the most
economical solution of this sulphide problem, this
assurance from Mr. Brett is a most important matter.
The Austral Otis Engineering Co., South Melbourne, are
sole manufacturers for Australasia of this table.*
* * * *
The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discrintion of cast or wrought iron work.*

Brown Hill Extended, Kalgoorlie, instead of
utilising shaft on recently-bought Kalgoorli Prop, leases,
has started sinking new main shaft to prospect east lode.
As shaft has to go down 800 ft., it will be 12 months, at
the earliest, before any opening up can be done. Under
the circumstances scrip is not likely to have a very active
market. Some rich ore has recently been opened up in
extension of Brown Hill shoot, but whether it will be
rooted out and treated has not yet been decided. Co. has
about £40,000 in liquid assets, so can carry on for a long
time without appealing to shareholders.

STILL QUITE FIT.
MRS. OFFSTEIN: “ Barney, you grow unfit for
beezness. You vos so shord-sighded you fail
to see dot dis piece of stuff vot you take for
silk vos half cotton.”
MR. OFFSTEIN: “No, Rachel, I vos so shord-
sighded I failed to see dot der piece of stuff
vot I took for cotton vos half silk.”
“Homo”: The Victorian police were re-
cently instructed to ignore persons who address
them by their Christian names while on duty, and
they seem to be making strenuous efforts to carry
out the order. I know of a man who addressed
« constable by his Christian name of John, the
other day, and the officer chased him half-a-miie
through streets, lanes, shops, alleys, and back-
yards in his anxiety to ignore him.
The Brisbane press goes in greatly for a
cheap object it calls the literary “ cadet.” He is
a sort of boy-reporter, who isn't exactly a boy
and doesn’t exactly report—he rather slings things
together and turns the sub-editor’s head a pale
grey. The cadets were in great form over
Warton’s execution. The Telegraph young man
called the hang-gentleman the “dreaded myrmi-
don of the law,” which was just about as original
as the “devouring element.” The Observer
man said the hangman showed the prisoner where
to stand. The Telegraph said the prisoner rushed
eagerly up to the scaffold, and was directed by
the gaol warder. It also said he walked with an
“elastic step.” The Mail violently contradicted
this, and said he walked with difficulty. The
Observer and the Telegraph said that Warton’s
hands were pinioned behind his back when he first
appeared, while the Mail said that, when on the
gallows, he held out his hands for the straps. The
three papers also gave three different accounts of
the “last words,” but they agreed pretty well
about the fact that the deceased was quite dead
after he had been killed. But, after all, the ideal
hanging report has yet to be written—the report
that will be light and original without being
frivolous ; and will state that the deceased re-
bounded into Abraham’s bosom, and yet not offend
the susceptibilities of the deeply religious section
of the community.
* * * *
There is just now trouble brewing in the
Victorian Labor ranks. The rupture began
through G. Roberts, ex-M.L.A., declining to sign
the Trades Hall balance-sheet as auditor unless a
sum of £l3 was accounted for or made good. This
was done —and Roberts was relieved of his
auditorsliip. Later, Roberts took up charges made
by Lauder, ex-editor of the Tocsin, now in ’Frisco.
These were three—(l) of sweating in the Tocsin
office by the over-employment of boys in place of
men ; (2) of subletting a printing contract to a non-
union office ; (3) of malversation of Labor trust
funds by certain prominent individuals. Roberts
handed to the Trades Hall Council Lauder’s state-
ment, and invited inquiry. The council appointed
a committee, which inquired into the matter (but
not, according to Roberts, fully and fairly), and
reported the charges as being malicious and un-
founded ; whereupon Roberts was expelled from
the council. Now Roberts demands independent
audit of the Eight-Hours’, Trades Hall, and Rail-
way Strike accounts. And that ought certainly
to be granted—no matter what the standing or
the motives of Roberts. If there has been dis-
honesty, it should be fully exposed. If all is
right, the imputations now being made should be
wiped away by an independent inquiry.

At Broken Hill last week a ‘ ‘ turf adviser ”
was sentenced to a month’s imprisonment without
the option for having advertised that he would
tip the winner on receipt of ss. Yet the law
permits anyone to sell a tip for the cure of, say,
cancer.

The Hebrew and the Scotchman are not
so dusty when you consider the Hindu and his
idea of the value of sexpen. One
of him, a casteless Bengali, en-
tered a sixpenny eating-house in
Sydney recently. There was a
three-course Dill : soup, meat,
sweets. Ranji started on a plate
of cut bread and wolfed it be-
fore his order was taken. Seizing
a loaf from the next table, he
broke it small in his hands and
finished it. He sent back his
plate for an encore of soup, and
attacked his meat like a bush-
fire. When he asked for sweets,
the waitress told him sweets
were “off.” She offered him
some cheese instead. Ranji stood
up, shaking in his Bengal wrath.
“No sweets! By crees, what
fraud do I uphold ? Where ees
the bass? ”he shouted. The boss
admitted that sweets were off,
but protested that Ranji had
eaten 3d. worth of bread. The
Hindu slammed 3d. on the
table. “I go now to bring
pelice !” he shouted. “ They
will look into the fraud. I
go now to consult the law.”
Just as he was bouncing off
the step to get a handful of
law, Mary Kelly played three
quarts of boiled cabbage-water on
to his turbaned wicket. The writer
fled. He can stand the odor of Hindu, but not
with cabbage trimmings.
* # * *
Nearly everybody who owns a hack in the
“ select circles ” of Perth (W.A.) goes a-hunting
twice a week. The daily papers gush over the
“runs,” and carefully catalogue all who follow
“ the hounds.” There is a master, deputy-master,
whips, horn-sounders, and all the frill of a fashion-
able meet in a swagger hunting county. As a
matter of fact, the hounds are the remnant of a
pack of beagles imported by an English Johnnie
when Ooolgardie was booming 12 years ago, while
the quarry is the wretched little scrub wallaby,
as tame and no larger than the ordinary domestic
cat. Two of the ladies turned up recently in
bifurcated skirts and sat their horses like men,
much to the joy of a crowd assembled to see the
circus start. .
DIVORCE ITEM.
“ His wife said in her evidence that he was a
two-faced deceiver .”
“ Well, if he’s got another face, what’s he wearing
that one for ?”
There is likely to be trouble in Bananaland
Parliament shortly respecting affairs at the Dun-
wich Asylum. There are upwards of 1000 old
people there, many suffering from incurable
diseases such as consumption, cancer, chronic
rheumatism, and Bright’s disease. There is not
one nurse to look after the comforts of these un-
fortunates. The institution’s doctor, in his annual
report, has pointed out for years that nurses are
essential, to enable the inmates who are sick to be
given proper treatment. But no reform has been
attempted. Another point raised by the doctor
is that necessary measures to prevent the spread
of disease cannot be undertaken, owing to the
absence of a trained staff. When the Old Age
Pensions Commission sat in Brisbane recently,
the one man able to give exact evidence regarding
the aged sick poor (Dr. Stockwell) was not called,
the cross-examination falling upon an officer of
the Home department, who was careful not to
cross the danger line.
* * * *
There is at least one simple-minded
Gentleman One in Victoria. Being lately sued
for the return of £5 said to have been paid to
secure a divorce, he offered £2, without prejudice,
on condition that the magistrate made an order
prohibiting the reporting of the case. The Bench
refused—and, of course, every paper carefully
published the name of Gentleman One and full
details.
“ Mars,” from Victoria ■
Victorian schools are in a bad way ; and
it is about up to T. Bent to effect an ini-
provement by the pre-
sent Teachers Bill. On
the last day of the ses-
sion of 1901 A. Pea-
cock’s Government
passed an Education
Bill abolishing pay-
ment by results, but
shelved a companion
Bill fixing teachers’
salaries. Now the De-
partment has to go on
in a half - and - half
fashion, teachers being
paid by “ re-
sults,"although
“ results ” are
abolished. That
is one mudhole
the Department
is in. Here’s
another:—
In ’93, a so-
called Re-
trenchment Bill
regraded V i c.
schools, arid
made seven
classes instead
of five, but the
classification of
teachers in
charge was not
altered, so the
Act left a large
surplus ot
teachers in ex-
cess of their
positions high-
class teachers in
charge of low-class
schools. New-
comers in the ser-
vice filled up the
VI. and VII. schools
not already filled
by V.-class teach-
ers, but those that
reached VI. class
rose to the sum-
mit of their profes-
sion so far as pro-
motion was con-
cerned, for they
must stay there (the
highest possible salary
is £105) until the over-
plus of V. class is reduced.
By present methods, this will
take centuries, and the only
hope of the VI. and VII. men
is a Japanese invasion and a general slaughter of Y. men
by the enemy.
It speaks volumes for these men and the rank-and-file,
who are going mentally to sleep in the backblocks, that
they have any vigor left in them, much less the energy to
tackle the Director’s new programme, and the work of
about 60 class lessons per day, unassisted.
* • • •
“E.F.” on £h e subject of Shining
Lights:—
A former pillar of the “ Church of Christ ” came before
Prahran (Melb.) police-court the other week, on a charge
of “ unseemly interruptions ” in the place where he used
to pray. Defendant and others were formally expelled
from the Church of Christ because they wouldn’t
renounce their claim to pour forth their gladness in
“ incoherent utterances ” whenever the spirit moved ’em
to take the floor.
They had joined the blessed Church for talking pur-
poses, and when the other brethren desired them, as a
personal favor, not to pray and preach, but to sit and
listen to the beautiful words of more inspired orators,
they formed themselves into an opposition party. Hence
the police-court revelation.
In this enlightened age, when every earnest pietist can
buy a New Testament for 9d., it is impossible to surmise
how a Church came to be founded on the notion that all
its members are entitled to play “ lead ”, turn and turn
about. The blessed Worrd, which these voluble Kerris-
tians are always talking about, offers little or no evidence
in favor of “self-chosen speakers.” Even the Early
Christians in Wilson Barrett’s play are content to leave
all the talking to the oldest snufflebuster with the
longest beard. Truly, the ordinary greengrocer (the
defendant in this case was a produce merchant)
who goes to church for salvation, must learn
to possess his soul in silence whilst his appointed
brother tells him what a miserable sinner he is.
There can be no just cause or reason in a church where
everybody accuses all the other people of miserable
sinning. There must be Lights to lighten the darkness,
and when a produce merchant, in spite of crushing evi-
dence to the contrary, sets up his own bit of darkness for
a shining Light, nothing remains for the congregation but
to put him out.
* * * *
A step is being taken towards the estab-
lishment of a Greater Sydney by the annexation
to the city of the bankrupt suburb of Camper-
down. Camperdown is broke; it has the bailiffs
in ; the rates are practically all annexed by its
creditors; and the streets are going back to the
bush once more—or, rather, the bush is coming to
Camperdown. The proposed terms are that the
city, when it annexes Camperdown, shall get the
loans of the insolvent suburb renewed on easier
terms, put its streets in repair, eject the bailiff,
reduce the cost of administration, and then raise
enough rates to enable it to pay its way. Also it
is proposed that Camperdown should be subjected
to a new by-law under which its narrow alleys are
to be gradually widened to a decent 66ft. This
last condition raised a bitter fight, and opposition
is not yet over. The Camperdown aldermen
didn’t know that they were to be cleaned and
washed as well as relieved of the bailiff, and a
desperate desire not to be washed fills their souls.
* * # *
The lawyer defending a Victorian butcher,
charged under the Factories Act with paying
an employee less than the proper wage, did
his client very little good by proving that the
man in question, who was employed at cutting up,
suffered so badly from lung complaint, and coughed
so dreadfully on to the meat, that he really wasn’t
worth any more than he was getting. Some law-
yers are too enthusiastic in the effort to do justice
to their six-and-eightpence.
MEMORIES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Just a link of golden stars,
Chained across the dark.
Just a glimpse of gleaming bars,
In the moonlight stark.
Just a song from off the shore,
Paint and distance-sweet.
Just a word sung o’er and o’er,
As the twin tides meet.
Memories, sweet memories, of the bay and brine.
Memories—ah—memories 1 These, at least, are mine.
Just a valley warm with sun,
Where the blue gums grow.
J ust a summer-time begun
In September’s glow.
Just a land of flowers a-sway,
As the sunset nears.
Just one perfect, heavenly day
Stolen from the years.
Memories, faint memories. Plowering, fragrant ways.
Memories, old memories, of September days.
Just the early moonlight still
On the paddocks brown —
We alone upon the hill,
As the stars die down.
Just a longing, almost pain,
As the new years rise,
Just to see your face again
And your haunting eyes.
Memories, soft memories, of a face so sweet,
Memories, dead memories. We shall never meet.
Just a throbbing violin,
And a shoulder white.
Just the first sweet fruits of sin
Ere the bitter blight.
Just an ecstasy sublime
Of a love forbid.
Just a moment filched from time
And a passion hid.
Memories, these memories, fain would I forget ;
Memories, dull memories, and a long regret.
N.S.W.
CARO.
THE BREECHES BRAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At a ball given the other day in Kensington Palace, and
- attended by King Edward and every other member of the
Royal family in the British Isles, all the gentlemen wore
evening dress and knee-breeches, thus giving a fillip to a
new fashion that is much favored by the King. . . . Since
the Revolution the democratisatioi) of male costume has
gone steadily on, till now it is practically impossible to
distinguish, by bis raiment alone, the prince from the
waiter. Can it be that this introduction of knee-breeches
is but one more symptom of that “ reaction ” of which
Mr. John Morley has recently been speaking in the pages
of the Nineteenth Century ? Is it possible that the aspira-
tion to return to a more picturesque form of dress is really
an expression of the protest against the monotonous dead-
level of democracy.— Melbourne Argus.
The unknown state of persons great is little short of
shocking,
And still will last until high caste is shown by silken
stocking;
The mob may laugh at s ows of calf, but calves define the
borders
Between the trousered rabble and the knee-breeched
higher orders.
Although we hear the vulgar jeer at leg shows of the lank,
And likewise of the corpulent, still should we show our
rank ;
Though knickers tight annoy a knight whose front is out
of drawing,
His dignity abdominal may help the mob in awing.

James Edwards, described as the “Wa-
ranga (Vie.) ‘murderer’,” and damned as an
“ atrocious criminal ” by 100 newspapers, has been
acquitted on the murder charge and convicted of
manslaughter only. What a waste of bloodthirsti-
ness' is here revealed !
* * * »
The Press, the Public and the Police of
Melbourne have received a compliment that they
ought to have no trouble in digesting. James
Edwards, convicted of the manslaughter of
Skinner at Waranga, has, through his solici-
tor, written a letter to the Sheriff, asking
him to officially thank the three P’s for “the
kindness, courtesy and consideration ” with which
he had been treated from the time of his arrest.
(He got no consideration before his arrest.) The
unfortunate Skinner, the top of whose skull, with
a large hole in it, was one of the exhibits at
Edwards’ trial, did not understand how to treat
Edwards. Skinner seems to have tried urging
him with a shovel, by which he was, apparently,
impenetrable. “Kindness, courtesy and con-
sideration” were the things that Edwards re-
quired, and if Skinner had realised that he might
now be less dead than he is.
* * * *
The saving of the neck of Edwards was a
triumph for barrister Maxwell. The Crown’s
case against the prisoner was a purely circumstan-
tial one. After it had been given, Maxwell, with a
fine dramatic instinct, put Edwards into the box,
and there on oath he told the whole story of the
killing. Edwards’ hands were clenching and
twitching, there was a ghastly pallor on his face,
his voice was gone to a low whisper, inter-
rupted every few minutes by loud, sobbing breaths
as he fought for self-control. The hush of the
court; the strained attention needed to catch
what Edwards was saying; and Maxwell’s soft,
broad Scotch leading the witness here
and there with a well-put question, tied
the jury up in knots. Seldom have 12 jury-
men been so affected. They didn’t actually weep,
but some of them were very near it; while in the
body of the court people blubbered out aloud. The
Crown Prosecutor seemed swept off his feet, and
he never recovered. His final speech was in the
prisoner’s favor rather than against him. Hardly
a jury in the world, after such a display as
Edwards put up, would have sent him to the
scaffold.
* * * V
The sand catastrophe at Moore Park last
week, in which the daughter of Mr. Burgess,
M.L.A., and a young female companion lost their
lives, is not the first of its kind in the neighbor-
hood of Sydney. Some ten years ago a couple of
sweethearts left Waterloo for a stroll and never
returned. It was thought they had eloped.
Many weeks after, however, a sand carter came
across their remains in a sandhill. They had sat
down to say sweet nothings under the lee of the
hill, and it slid down and buried them.

For almost the first time on record there
appears to be a chance of the N.S.W. Law Depart-
ment prosecuting the female who invents a charge
of criminal assault against a man, either to serve
her own ends or for mere amusement. Still there
is no certainty about the matter as yet, and that
kind of female has been allowed to go absolutely
free in so many scores of cases that it would be an
amazing departure from precedent if anything
happened to her.
Art—half-crown art—is to rage in many
colors through the Victorian State schools. The
Education Minister (Sachse) intends to secure
“colored plates of famous pictures,” and allow
parents and friends to buy them for the schools at
2s. 6d. each. What sort of artistic taste will be
developed in the nation that from childhood feasts
its eyes on half-crown chromos ?
Irish writer Edmund Downey spoils a
carefully-faked “ Australian” magazine yarn by
remarking that the heroine, in crossing a road in
a Melbourne suburb, “stopped and handed some-
thing out of her purse to a crossing-sweeper.” The
mere suggestion that Melbourne depends on the
charity brand of road-sweeper common in the
Cold Country is enough to make all corporation
members snort bitterly.
# * * *
A writer in the English Contemporary
throws some sort of a sidelight on the everlasting
birthrate business.' He makes a census of
the homes of the British peers who have’
married American pork-packers’ girls, and
finds an appalling number of empty cradles.
Since 1840 thirty lords and dukes and such
have married Americans; 13 have found a
cradle a superfluity, five have no son, five
more have only one son. The whole 30
couples only muster 39 children among them.
Forty-four more of the pork-packers’ girls
have married courtesy titles or baronets, and
17 of them haven’t had the expense of a
christening. Apparently with the object of
showing that the dear old nobility isn’t to
blame altogether, he mentions that 23 others
of them have married into “ the colonies,”
and they have 63 children, while 30
baronets with colonial wives have a total
brood of 102. There are two ways of
looking at the figures. One is that the
pork-packer’s girl reckons she has done
her complete duty to the old nobility
when she has taken its coronet out
of pawn and provided it with pocket-
money. The other is that the old
nobility which gets its coronet in
pawn is mostly a very much played-
out nobility. It’s really a question
of which side of the Atlantic
ought to bear the blame.
It transpired in N.S. W. Par-

Anglican Bishops appointed to
Australian sees begin their pull
on the Australian purse from
the date of their appointment
in England, not from the day
when they begin to honestly shove
the barrow in the vineyard in Australia.
At least, Minister Hogue informed mem-
bers that Bishop Barry drew £3300 and Piimate
Smith £2672 from Sydney before they even touched
Australia.
* * * *
A Sydney medical man lately ordered a
family of five out of a suburban toy cottage. It
had but three rooms—mere matchboxes in size.
One was devoted to cooking and eating, and
another to sleeping. The family was in bad health,
overcrowding and poor ventilation being the
causes. Yet the third room was kept as a parlor,
with piano and woolwork fripperies, for the enter-
tainment of an “at home ” once a week. It was
rigidly locked up except when so used.
N.S.W. found a leper last ■week in the
simplest way possible. An intimation had been
sent down to Little Bay that a diseased Chinaman
was to be forwarded by a certain train.,
and the authorities were to be on hand to receive
him and give a receipt. For some reason he
wasn’t sent by that train, and for some other
reason the change of plans wasn’t notified, so the
ambulance was on hand at the psychological
moment, and when the train came in the atten-
dant annexed a likely-looking Chinaman and
rushed him to the proper receptacle. He was a
leper sure enough, but he wasn’t the one ex-
pected ; in fact, he was just a casual, or extra, or
supernumerary leper. It creates a kind of shud-
dery feeling when the authorities can fish for
lepers in this off-hand fashion at a railway station
and get a bite the first time a train comes in.
* * * *
President Roosevelt is reported to have
given an Australian interviewer the strikingly
original hint that this country “must either let
the world enter its gates or else keep its cradles
full.” This sets one looking at the United States’
cradle. The U.S. is so amazingly backward in
some things that it doesn’t even keep a record of
births and deaths for the whole country, but out
of its 40 principal cities there are only six which
have more births in proportion to population than
Sydney has; only three have so small a death-
rate ; only one can compare with Sydney in its in-
crease by excess of births over deaths ; and no less
than 12 out of the 40 (Baltimore, San Francisco,
Cincinnati, Detroit, Washington, Toledo, Alleg-
hany, Syracuse, St. Joseph, Los Angeles, Scran-
ton, and Albany) are in the appalling situation of
having more deaths than births. San Francisco
has, roughly, three deaths to two births ; Detroit,
five deaths to three births ; Washington about the
same; while Toledo, Alleghany and Scranton
have a good average of ten deaths to six births.
Either Uncle Sam’s statistics are mad or he might
almost as well sell his cradle to keep the wooden
ham in.
* * * *
Waterloo, one of Sydney’s pocket-handker-
chief boroughs, is in a mess. In 1882 the council
borrowed £4OOO, and built a town hall with the
money. At a recent meeting the council was
staggered with a letter asking when the borough
proposed to buy the land the hall stands on, and
how much it was prepared to give for it. It ap-
peared that the site was leasehold, the property of
an absentee landlord, and the town hall, loan money
and all, will soon fall in with the lease. The
Waterloo council really fancied it had a freehold.
With a Greater Sydney, and one town hall instead
of 40, there would be at least 39 less chances of
foolishness like this.

“Hamer ” : Was in a high-class restaurant
t’other day when the man sitting opposite me
made a discovery. From the excited way he went
on it might have been a gold-mine, but it was only
a hairpin boiled in with his pudding. He waved
the waitress towards him, and pointed with indig-
nation to his find. But that girl was a jewel, and
simply annihilated him. After exclaiming on his
good fortune, she explained that, usually, money
was put in the puddings, but as the stock had run
out, they were trying hairpins instead, and begged
him not to speak loudly about it lest the other cus-
tomers should hear and become jealous. He took
it in a dazed way at first, and then started to eat
in a subdued fashion. But he didn’t eat the hair-
pin, and he didn’t carry it away in his pocket.

Fragments of a slaughtered person,
Strewn upon the sands of time,
Teach us not to breathe a curse on
One suspected of the crime.
When our shriek of horror ceases
Let the fact be not denied
That a body ch opped to pieces
Cannot tell us how it died.
LITTLE GIRL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
So at last you’ve come to town
In your brand new tailor gown,
And your plait a golden crown,
Little girl !
With your purse and parasol,
With your gloves and spotted fall,
You are not yourself at all,
Little Girl!
As you walk along the street
Are the glances that you meet
To your maiden fancy sweet,
Little girl ?
If I met you I should stare.
As it is—l only swear,
Knowing others find you fair,
Little girl!
Ah, the happy days of yore,
When you wore a pinafore !
Ah, the days that come no more,
Little girl!
When you raced me through the hay
At the fading of the day',
As the sunset turned to grey,
Little girl!
We shall never ride again,
As we once did, o’er the plain,
Helter-skelter through the rain,
Little girl!
We were friends then all the day,
Now I go a different way—
Every sunset turns to grey,
Little girl!
VIC.
P.N.R.


Charles Heidsieck

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, July 25, 1905.
My Dear Arini.—
A bouncing Sydney widow—she is a double-
distilled widow m point of fact—was the most
magnificently dressed presentee at the last Draw-
ing Room. One item of her decorations, as told by
press letters, was “trails of true lovers’ knots” on
the train of her robe. Certainly, she wore these
true lovers’ knots behind her. But what is the
significance ? Does it mean that she has put
thoughts of love behind her for ever, or that she
is prepared to turn round in a friendly spirit if she
notices any highly eligible person following her ?
The annual University ball is “ going down ” in
a society sense. Last week’s fixture boasted no
members of the Senate, no Chancellor, no Regis-
trar, no dons. There are some professors’ wives
who make Government House the only objective
outside Woollahra. Distinctions between one
University family and another are too minute to
be followed. At Mrs. (Prof.) David's ball last
week such magnates as the Anderson Stuarts and
the MacCallums made the vacuum that every Uni-
versity candidate abhors. They had prior engage-
ments. When Lord Beauchamp went to this ball
in the sweet, bloomy past, the function was rushed,
and the sale of tickets stopped weeks before. Last
week an advertisement meekly explained where
pasteboards could be bought. Like all balls com-
mitted by men, the males outnumbered the girls
by almost four'to one. Free bar and smoking-
facilities kept many men off the waxed floor. By
8.30 the stairs were filled with squatters-out,
making it difficult for fresh arrivals to squeeze
through. The ball-room was empty, and the bye-
ways densely populated. A perfectly natural
state of things when the squatters are in life’s
young dawn. There were no formalities, no set,
no one to wait for ; the ball just began and rolled
on. There were bewildering numbers of extra
dances, extra tall palms, extra men, extra plain
frocks, extra pretty debutantes with extra showery
bouquets. The most prominent woman there was
Mrs. Schofield, wife of the lecturer on chemistry.
Mr. and Mrs. Frank Gardner and their small
child are staying at Petty’s Hotel. The
gentleman will be remembered as Miss Carrie
Swain’s manager. His union with her was
declared no marriage by the French courts.
The new Mrs. Gardner, a Frenchwoman, is
hurrying around in blue serge and tan boots look-
ing at Sydney's beauty places. She is of good
height and figure, has a colorless face with pale
blue eyes, downward crescent of mouth, and a
quantity of lustreless ash-colored hair. But her
smile and teeth are charming, and the contour of
the face is like that in Clytie rising from the
Heliotrope. In age she might be anything from
28 to 38. The great motor man himself is still
large, and is now iron grey.
Miss Elsie Moore is again in Sydney wearing
the newest thing in smart serge walking suits.
It’s sleeves are so short that they are just shoulder
puffs. A lengthy suede glove shaped to the arm
takes the place of more sleeve. She expects to
leave for America next month.
Fitz-Williain Curtis, who has just been divorced
by his wife, one of the handsome Misses Fattorini
of Sydney, has had a curious career. The son of
a C.E. clergyman, he first adopted the army as a
profession. Coming to Australia he struck a vein
of hard luck, and was for a time assistant care-
taker to a Melbourne cricket ground. Then he
went as groom with horses to Singapore, met
there some English friends, and as a result got a
good post with the Burmese mounted police.
Curtis is a good-looking man, with a great charm
of manner, and made many friends in Australia.
But he does not seem to have treated his wife too
well, judging from his Honor’s remarks in the
divorce court.
An Australian returned from a European tour
tells me that France and England showed a pro-
nounced entente cordiale in welcoming the young
King of Spain—a handsome youth, who might
stand for Byron’s Don Juan in appearance, but is
far too jealously chaperoned to allow him to match
that gay spark in conduct. One Parisian paper of
some standing recorded an emphatic protest
against this excessive chaperonage. “ The King
of Spain on Monday,” it said, “after early Mass,
visited the Louvre, and was shown round by So-
and-So. Then he lunched with the President.
Then he was taken to a Review. Then a State
dinner and the Opera House. On Tuesday the
same—early Mass, official visits, official meals,
and official attendance at an official theatre. Wed-
nesday again the same. Is this our vaunted
French hospitality? Why not introduce the
young King to a representative Parisian beauty
and let him tour the music-halls with her ? ”
The young Spanish monarch is, it seems,
destined for early matrimony, and the name of
the English Princess Patricia is mentioned in that
relation if the religious difficulty can be overcome.
The Queen of Spain must be rigidly R.C.
Our divorce Judge, Mr. G. B. Simpson, is, I’m
told, having a merry time in Europe. A Waler
noticed him the other day deeply enjoying a
French farce, with divorce trimmings, in a Parisian
play-house. Probably Mr. Simpson, from a sense
of duty, is devoting some of his holiday time to an
investigation of the divorce literature of other
countries.
A local man, a friend of Dr. Muecke, writes to
me: “Ada Crossley couldn’t ‘tower’ over her
husband unless she stood on a chair. I am six
feet to a line, and Muecke is taller than I. The
contralto is barely five feet eight. Her slender-
ness, her long neck and pile of hair make her
appear taller than she really is.” A Maori-
lander now padding about London writes me
that she called to see Mrs. Muecke, and was
introduced to “Chummie,” the husband. The
visitor said she wanted an “ impression ” of her—
the bride—for a writing friend, at which Ada
chortled and hid her face on a Persian kitten
“It is evident,” comments the visitor, “that she
is having her perfect hour.”
