Sophie Osmond, the ex-Melbourne presswoman
had had a serial story in London Daily Mail, I
did not know that she had taken the competition
money-prize offered by that paper for the be.-t
x ex , e . V there ” past 200 competitors.
Jolly Sophie has a nice big flat of her own, and
writes novelettes, in which the bold bad baron and
the dark saturnine count go their wicked ways
deceiving girls with svelte figures and big startied
eyes. The English novelette reader must have
these creations, saj s Sophie, and a moated grange
has to be reached down to them occasionally.
Two artistic Melbourne mademoiselles, Nellie
Yeitch and Portia Geach, are in Sydney these
days when the car of Plimbus— as Dan O’Connor
would say—must be getting hot axles from over-
work. (Is anyone else sick of the monotony of
this blue-and-gold, eternal-sunshine weather in
Sydney ? I am. I yearn, with feminine con-
trariety, to feel once more the mud and water go
“ squidge ” under my goloshes.) Miss Veit -h Is
staying with a married sister at Mosmans ; she
brought back from London clothes that might b ■
done into verse and recited by the wearer to a
Chopin nocturne. The artist girl, Portia Geach.
shows originality in a tie. It is of pale fawn
leather, not kid—the wide ends slightly snipped,
and the whole stamped, by a bookbinder, with
tiny gold fleur-de-lvs.
Sydney women are still working like yeast in
aid of the Children’s Hospital charity. Mayoress
Taylor, Miss Margaret Harris and Mr. W. G.
Layton have had a great rush of thought, resulting
in novelties for the Mayoress’ ball on August 1.
Twenty-four girls are to dance a stately minuet to
Mr. Arthur Mason’s accompaniment on the organ.
The Yale College quadrille, the latest fashionable
American dance, will also be seen for the first time
in Australia. Three girls are to give a dress
manipulation with skirtswhich contain collectively
240 yards of silk. One will represent the Devil,
the two others Angels. What the celestial and
Satanic impersonators do in their disguise will be
revealed to those who buy five-shilling ball tickets
or two-shilling gallery tickets. A girl Devil
wearing part of a mile of silk as a tail should be
interesting. It’s the sort of Devil some of us
would like to meet in the next world.
When I wrote last week concerning the general
desire “to make a little more money,” I had not
seen Allen Shaw, one of the wizardly persons
appearing with Thurston at the Palace. Shaw is
described as a coin manipulator. When he wants
more money, either to build a cathedral, or to
cover with new patent leathers those American
plantigrade feet of his, he doesn’t work for it—he
just “ pushes the atmosphere with his right hand ”
and coins appear all over his person. “Pushing
the atmosphere”—it is a lovely phrase, and as
American as a quick lunch of hot cakes and iced
water.
Philharmonic Society’s shows draw more mixed
and interesting audiences than do any other
musical festivals in Sydney. Especially is this
true when the society gives “The Messiah.” The
be-diamouded old lady who shudders at the name
of music hall, the intense young woman who talks
of “ the Master’s work,” the old, unmarried, but
happy female, Methody-looking parsons, laboring
men and boys—they all revel in hearing for the
manyth time “Unto Us A Child Is Born,” “I
Know That My Redeemer Livetli ” and the
Hallelujah Chorus. On Saturday night the
audience experienced the full awfulness of the 76
consecutive bars of Amens. It was like “yap,
yap ” for all eternity. Under Hazon’s sensible
innovation of concluding “ The Messiah ” with the
Hallelujah chorus the vast Amen doesn’t yap.
Mr. George Upward, Usher of the Black Rod
in the Federal Parliament and coach of the Vic-
torian eight for twenty years, is seriously ill at
Hunter’s Hill. He came over intending to see the
big boat-race, but fell sick immediately on arrival.
There are great possibilities m a country which
has a population of 80,000,000. Four American
‘ married women are now under sentence of death
for murder.
The engagement is announced of Mr. A. W.
Jose, the London Times representative in Aus-
tralia, to Miss Abseil. The Abseil girls, one of
whom married artist George Lambert, are uncom-
monly good-looking, in a Puritan-Quakerish way,
and have literary leanings. Mr. Jose has done
some good literary work, and will do more, it may
be safely prophesied, as his post on the Times
gives him excellent opportunities.
Heermann’s violin appears to be the oldest that
has yet reached these shores. It is a genuine
Strad ; built in seventeen something, and shows
no sign of physical decay. When son Emil’s
fiddle went wrong at a recent concert, Heermann
senior didn’t offer to lend his.
Strange as it might seem, some of the
most excited spectators of the Stanbury-Towns
sculling match were women—and middle-aged
women at that. Absolutely the most vocif-
erous party I saw was an ancient lady of
nearly 60, and she got so excited that when
StanDury approached a rocky ledge to speak to
some friend, she bounced wildly into the water
till it reached her waist in her anxiety to shake
hands with the new champion. The variety
of frocking was amazing. It was almost as diver-
sified as the kinds of craft on which people
floated in order to get a glimpse of the
great race. Even the catamaran was there, or
rather two of it were, and fine safe craft they
are, too. But the master-stroke of originality
was where, beside a dapper motor-launch, all
burnished copper and pale-green enamel paint, a
bushy-bearded savage stood upright on a couple
of planks lashed together, which he had pro-
pelled into position with a long pole. He must
have been very tired standing there in the burn-
ing sun, and even his beard ha l to be parted
decimally true to preserve his balance. Down
where the judge’s boat was moored the effect was
that of a floating garden, owing to the blossoms in
the women’s hats.
One of the side shows of London is Jay’s. A
returned Sydneysider tells ine of seeing a model
“ trying-on ” costumes. There are six model tryers-
on, all tall and slender and well-propo.tio.iel.
Sometimes they are not by any means pretty-
faced, but they must have beautiful figures,
with a good curve at the back of the
waist and graceful movements. “A grey silk
and chiffon get-up that I fancied was put
on (out of sight) by the exhibitor, who then swept
in with her head well up. She spoke not a word
during the showing o'f and didn’t even look at us.
The elegant shopman who conducted the show did
the talking, and he directed the turnings and
fixings of the model with a wand. Never did he
touch the costume or the wearer with his hand.
These girls get a pound a week and a few out-door
costumes are supplied to them. Sometimes they
marry well —not as well as the ballet or the chorus,
but good matches all the same.
Seems to me that when I neglect for long to pay
homage to the Blue Mountains, their sheer crags
1 jok more frowningly on me and their cliffs turn
colder profiles from me. The air there is now soft
with a summer-to-be, and the Kanimbla Valley at
Medlow smells of gums and incense ; and if you
want to feel the heart of silence throb with
a soundless word, lie as the lizards lie on
one of the warm flat rocks overhanging
the grey-green and purple depths. The Consuinp-
t:ves’ Home at Wentworth Falls is well worth a
v s.t now that the new “ shelters” are in position.
Like glorified sentry boxes they stand dotted
about the acreage, closed on one—the western-
side, and open on three. And the afflicted people
never see a fire —unless a bush blaze in the sum-
mer. A deeply interested visitor to the Homes
last week was Senator Dobson of Tasmania. He
was staying at the Medlow Hydro. Mrs. Dobson
and other progressive Tasmaniennes are anxious
to inaugurate a sanatorium for consumptives in
their own island, and the Senator did his best to
get his consumptive education liberalised by the
doctor in charge.
An operatic artiste now with us tells how, if you
are in Paris, you may set about getting a six-hun-
dred guineacostumeforabout twelveor twenty-five.
Many of the workers at such famous shops as
Paquin’s or Doucet’s work in their own homes
night and early morning, and others keep obscure
little shops in obscure little quarters. From these
you may, by buying your own materials, get the
replica of the gown that thrilled London drawing-
rooms when Lady J. Bull appeared in it a few
weeks previously. Worst of it is that to Sherlock
out these homes and shops you’ll want more
French than is enough for the reading of a froggy
menu.
An American woman has patented an invention
by which fat persons may lace their shoes without
stooping. To the aged and infirm it will also be
a boon. One lace string is permanent in position,
and pulling the top of it laces the shoe, pulling
the bottom string loosens it.
Apropos of the alleged love of authority, a man
asked me, recently, wherein lay the tram-conduc-
tor’s fascination for women. I replied that per-
sonally I regarded the conductor with great sus-
picion and dislike. He frequently starts the tram
when I have one foot on the step. But the seeker
after human motives was not to be snagged. He
went on to relate how he had just seen three
women, of more than average good looks, and
with the dress and voices of well-bred inhabitants,
board a car, and when the conductor collected their
fares, they made his acquaintance—introduced
themselves, so to speak, by the offer of chocolates.
He accepted with calmness, and proceeded to
chat, and they looked and acted as if they were
the favored persons. “Is it,” asked the ques-
tioner, “ the momentary authority, or the brass
buttons '! Women love uniform. In this instance,
the conductor had no looks or physique to recom-
mend him, but he touched a bell like a man of
destiny, and the vehicle meekly stopped. ” Trying
to reply to such speculations always gives me
brain-fag, so I say nuffink But if women love a
uniform, the simulation of authority, and an ap-
pearance of physical strength, their intellect
ought to totter in the presence of policemen.
An Adelaide correspondent writes :
“ Mayor Bruce’s ball, held on July 20th, wasn’t
in the running with Mayor Cohen’s splash last
year. Mayor Cohen’s third ball was on such a
large scale that it required the Exhibition Build-
ing, but Bruce came back to the Town Hall, and
champagne didn’t flow, and there wasn’t even any
‘ sit-down ’ supper. The debutante was a scarce
quantity; in fact there was only one of her.
Taken altogether, however, Mayor Bruce’s ball
was better stage-managed than Mayor Cohen’s.
Invitations had been sent to one girl only in each
family, and, therefore, there was plenty of room
for dancing. Oue dressmaker complained that
she had 50 fewer dresses to make for the event this
year than last.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent :
“Nothing comes of that Women’s League libel.
Toorak, with toupee bristling on end, has been
awaiting it in vain. The graceful dame most con-
cerned is looking jaunty again, and returning to
prominence with a conqueror’s air, and the gilded

recovers her spirits, but has not yet arrived at
talking the matter over with her pals. It’s
believed that the ghost of that libel is laid.

“ The story of a matron in a swagger hotel
absent-mindedly wandering into a neighbor s
apartment and preparing dreamfully for the

“ The Bridge scandal has reached a climax. In
the principal hotels hereabouts a minute has been
entered into the books that no gambling is to be
allowed in any of the public rooms. The women
players must deal the cards in privacy, and pay
for the privacy. Up to now they have simply
taken all the cosy corners or reading-rooms of the
hotel for their gambols. All that is ended. Ihe
Bridgers are furious, but others are joyful.

year’s matriculation passes that the young vie.
native is hopelessly weak in grammar and any free
exercise of intelligence. "As for weak grammar,
only two or three,’says the examiner, ‘were able
to give an example of an illative conjunction, and
not more than half-a-dozen could state coyicisely
the difference between a gerund and a participle.
And so on. But are illative conjunctions !];hso-
lutely needful in the grown-up curriculum ? This
scribe matriculated with bulging honors not so
many years ago, and she can’t furnish an example
of an illative conjunction nor give any exact par-
ticulars of a gerund, and yet knows enough gram-
mar to alight readily on blunders of speech and all
that. What is the practical use of ‘ illative con-
junctions,’anyhow?
“ The Grey Dame of Stonington is interested in
epileptics. She heads a movement to raise a home
for those so afflicted, and wrote her first letter of
appeal to the papers last week, signing herself
Margaret J. Talbot. People often wondered
what the front name of the lady was. Now they
all declare she looks a Margaret—a perfect Mar-
garet. Fancy the intimate Maggie, though; or,
worse still, the rakish Peg ! Peggie Talbot! it’s
quite blasphemous. And yet her Ex. is said to
have a mirthful, mischievous twinkle concealed in
the corner of her eye.
“Autumn sunsets (mostly damp dirges) abound
in the Vic. Artists’ Exhibition. What’s the mat-
ter with gorgeous summer madrigals, wattle son-
nets of springtime, and the golden rhapsodies that
Australia is given to ? Why so much of autumn
miserably dripping tears and sighs over a sullen
’roo-land ? Why the soggy portrayal of the
sky in sulks? The artists’ fancy in this State
seems to turn naturally to thoughts of
woe. This shows even in the portraits. Albert
Enes perpetrates a most melancholy female. This
lady was never intended to be served up to a
public gallery. Several other woebegone figures
(not woebegone enough to be tragic) stare gloomily
from chair seats. Theo. Brooke Hansen (whose
name is brass-plated on the frame like a molar-
puller’s) has the most colossal ‘ Autumn ’ in the
show—a vast expanse of weather woe. over hill and
dale, relieved only by a fine sky color. Even
Blamire Young, who makes art holiday in poster
extravagance (as a means of artfully rolling on the
grass), puts a mirthless young female in his pic-
ture. The whole show is solemn drab, and in its
best parts mournful.

CURED INCURABLE
Thomas Weeks of Goulburn
Cured of Locomotor Ataxia
by
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
“ A FEW years ago I was a helpless cripple,
/\ paralysed by Locomotor Ataxia. To-
day I am strong, robust, active, and in
perfect health. And the sole credit for
my cure goes to Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale
People.”
This is the simple, sweeping, straightforward
statement of Thomas Bradley Weeks, of Helena-
street, Eastgrove, Goulburn, N.S.W. The facts
of his marvellous case are known to his neighbors,
among whom he is universally respected. He has,
moreover, backed up his words by a solemn oath
before the Mayor of Goulburn.
“A terrible heat wave, one February, knocked
me clean out, and left my spine half paralysed. I
was building a truck-yard at Yarra, near Goul-
burn. At nine in the morning it was 109 degrees
in the shade. Two days later I could not walk.
For over a year I was only able to shuffle about
the house. AH power of movement left my legs.
Once I sat down, I could not get up. If I coughed,
I would fall to the ground. My muscles had no
strength. My spine was paralysed. Sharp, shoot-
ing pains shot through my legs and thighs. In a
few months it was plain that I had that terrible
disease, Locomotor Ataxia.
“ People tortured with Rheumatism came to see
me, and when I told them how I suffered, they
said I had the same disease as themselves. When
I told others about the sharp, shooting pains down
my leg, from my hip to my heel, they said I had
Sciatica.
“ But I knew that I had worse than either. I
knew I had Locomotor Ataxia, and the doctors
say that that is the most painful disease in the
world and absolutely incurable.
“For a year-and-a-half, I was a confirmed
invalid. Month after month, I lay in my bed—
unable to work or even to walk. My spine seemed
to have lost all its power. It was decaying with
Locomotor Ataxia. In three months I lost three
stone in weight. Then, after two of the best
doctors had a go at me and failed, I started
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People. I read
how they had cured a Sydney man named Jarvis
of Locomotor Ataxia by_building up his .spine
through the blood—and that is just what they did
for me. Three boxes worked wonders. Of course,
they didn’t cure me in a week or two. I wasn’t
silly enough to expect that. I had made up my
mind to give Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills a fair trial
if I had to take fifty boxes. From the first, how-
ever. they gave me a wonderful appetite. I must
admit that that was the only good I noticed from
the first four or five boxes. Then I began to sleep
soundly, and my nerves grew stronger and steadier.
By-and-bye, I could feel the new blood in my
veins warming me up and making me feel better
in a dozen ways. Then the shooting pains were
not so bad. Little by little, my spine grew
stronger. I got better control over my nerves and
muscles. The life came back to my paralysed legs
and back, and I could walk about slowly. From
that time on, I improved rapidly. At last I stood
completely cured !
“ Eight months after my cure, I walked 110
miles in four-and-a-balf days—not a bad record
for an old fellow over 60. And the best of it is I
am cured for good. It is seven years now since
l)r. Williams’ Pink Pills drove Locomotor Ataxia
out of my system —and I have never had a sign of
it since.”
When Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills raised Thomas
Weeks to health and strength, after he had been
paralysed by Locomotor Ataxia, it is no wonder
they cure common ailments, like ansemia, indi-
gestion, biliousness, headaches, backaches, kidney
trouble, liver complaint, nervousness, neuralgia,
rheumatism, and the secret illnesses of girls and
women, whose health depends upon their blood.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cure all these because
they strike at the root and cause m the blood.
They don’t bother with mere symptoms. They
don’t act on the bowels. They do only one thing,
but they do it well—they actually make new
blood. That is the secret of their success. Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills are sold by chemists and
storekeepers, or sent, post free, by the Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Co., Sydney, 2s. 9d. a box,
six boxes 15s. 3d.
PERSONAL ITEMS.
Premier Bent of Victoria has made one
more promise. He says that his funeral will not
cost £4O.
Be Willis. Informant J. Haynes, prosecu-
ting attorney R. Haynes, defending attorney S.
Haynes. It never Haynes but it pours.
Thorold Waters, who will be remembered
in Australia as a pressman with a pleasing tenor
voice, has made a successful first appearance in
London as a singer.
When land-agent Nick Willis was making
his first business successes he disclaimed all repu-
tation for ability. “I am only a simple bush
lad,” he used to say.
The patriarch of Norfolk Island, William
Quintal, died on July 6th, aged 86 years. His
grandfather, Matthew Quintal, was an A.B. of
Captain Bligh's ship, the Bounty, and one of the
mutineers under Fletcher Christian in 1789.
Lewis Cohen, the only Conservative candi-
date returned for Adelaide in recent elections,
was complaining of his solitary state: “ I had
hoped to have one, at least, of my colleagues in
with me, but alas! I am the one little Jew
lamb.”
Dr. Gosman, the Hawthorn (Vic.) Con-
gregational parson, at his jubilee celebration, said
he had been told by a Yarra-bank person that he
was fast descending into hell. “If I am,” said
the old man, “it is strange that I suffer so
terribly from cold feet.”
An old Bathurst identity went over last
week in the person of John Edwards, who for a
great many years kept the Bridge Inn at Perth,
four miles from the City of the Plains. He was
amongst the pioneers at Bendigo and Forest
Creek-, and helped to make some history in the
“roll-up ” at Lambing Flat.
Joe Cook, M.H.li., only saw sunlight
once a week when, as a boy, he worked in a
Staffordshire coal-mine (the spread of Socialistic
legislation did away with that form of slavery),
and the gaps between his periods of intelligence
with respect to public facts would show that he
still stays underground in a mental sense for long
periods.
Captain Francis Crossman Doveton, Vic-
toria’s first gold commissioner, died at Melbourne,
the other day, aged 87. He was born at South-
hampton (Eng.), and as a lieutenant came with the
51st Regiment to Sydney in 1838. His uncle was
officer commanding the troops that guarded
Napoleon at St. Helena.
PURVES , K.C., TAKES THE CHAIR.
John Warton, recently hanged at Bris-
bane, executed a difficult fantasia on the prison
organ the day before he was suspended. Frederick
Bailey Deeming used to brag about his love for
Wagner and Yerdi. Charles Peace, murderer
and house-thief, performed exquisitely on the
violin. These musicans somehow drift to a bad
end.
According to Lombroso, at a recent
criminologist convention, “the Czar is a lunatic—
not a criminally-minded lunatic, but an innocent,
melancholy idiot, the easiest of tools in the hands
of flattering influence.” Lombroso also states
that all the reigning European monarchs are more
or less mad, and he brackets Emperor William
and Sultan Abdul-Hamid as criminal lunatics of
the worst type.
Centenarians need to have good memories.
The Sydney snips paper recently published the
yarn of a man who claims to be 105 years old, and
tells in proof how, on the eve of Waterloo, a friend
called on him “to purchase a stamp to send a
letter to the front.” Waterloo was fought in 1815,
and stamps, the Journal of Philately points
out, were invented in 1840.
The rush to imitate the Governor-General
in the wearing of Australian tweeds recalls the
incident of Protectionist Allen, M.L.A. (father of
Alfred Allen, a some-time-ago FreetradeM.L.A.),
going to N.S.W. Government House in Australian
tweeds and being refused admittance—not pre-
cisely because the cloth was locally manufactured,
but because of the cut of the garments.
According to a Ministerial admission in
Vic. Assembly, that fine old Tory, ex-Minister
M‘Kenzie, who had to resign from the Iceberg
Cabinet in January, 1903 (2| years ago), when it
was proved that in his capacity as head of the
Lands Department he had allotted valuable State
lands among his own family, hasn’t yet handed
back the free railway pass granted to him as a
member of the Executive.
Lewis Cohen, M.P. of S.A., strained his
throat whilst electioneering, and since hasn’t had
the best of voices. He talks of going to England
in the near future for repairs, and will possibly
try at the same time to discover the reason why
his title has been delayed.
Recently S. M. Herald published a
column and a half of “ The Public Library ” from
its foundation as a subscription library in Gover-
nor Darling’s time. Though mention is made of
most of the officials, from the founder, Thomas
de la Condamine, secretary to Governor Darling,
onwards, nothing is said of old John Fairfax
who, on arriving in Sydney in 1838, accepted the
position of librarian.
The first Hebrew wedding in Australasia
was solemnised in 1832 between Moses Joseph
and Miss Nathan. Mr. Joseph was a general
warehouseman at 71 George-street, Sydney. The
ceremony was performed by Mr. P. J. Cohen,
afterwards a merchant at Maitland, under a letter
of authority from the Chief Rabbi in London.
The “synagogue” was a store, still standing m
Bridge-street, and now used by Mr. Charles Bath
as an office.
Alfred Deakin’s father used to keep books
for Cobb and Co. in Melbourne in the early
sixties. Deakin jun. used to play marbles, on
strictly honorable lines, in front of a row of
suburban cottages that extended along from
Kirk’s Bazaar to Robertson and Moffatt’s. Eliza-
beth-street was then a cross between a creek and
a gutter, and had to be negotiated by a cab or raft
in the winter-time.
Wood, an old-time South Aus. Public
Trustee, absconded years ago with a colossal
amount, and went to South America, whence he
never returned. Before flitting he, ostensibly in
his public capacity, consulted a lawyer as to a safe
place for an absconder (he didn’t say that he was
the absconder); and, after a while, the Government
received a bill from that legal man : “To advising
Mr. Wood re extradition laws.” Of course it had
to be paid, too.
The story of the foundation of the fortune
of a wealthy Maorilander, as told by himself, is
good. “ A sailin’-ship, freighted with hardware,
went ashore on the coast,” he said, “ and her cargo
was sold by auction. Part of the cargo consisted
of camp-hovens, and I bid two bob apiece for ’em.
‘ Ow many will you take?’ says the man with the
’ammer. ‘ I’ll take the lot,’ I said, and he books
’em to me. Now, there was three acres and a-’alf
of them camp-hovens, and I sold ’em all at four
bob apiece. Times was good, and I used to take
the money from the customers at the door and
then turn them hinto the wilderness of camp-
hovens in the back yard to take their pick. That’s
the germ of me fortune.”
Price, the S.A. Labor leader, is a Welsh-
man with a great natural gift of eloquence, and
he has sat in the Assembly for 12 years. He is by
trade a stonemason, and worked for the contractor
who built Parliament House. Now, however, he
resides on a little block of land in one of the
suburbs, and cultivates the soil. Peake, the
Liberal leader, was for many years a district coun-
cil clerk at Narracooi'te, in the south-east, but is
now an auctioneer at Mount Barker. He is the
very antithesis of the emotional and impulsive
Labor leader with whom he is united in politics,
being a phlegmatic, deliberate and stolid person —
the sort of valuable reinforcement who comes up
behind with a heavy tread after Price has rushed
headlong into the battle with insufficient forces.
Melbourne Herald lately printed an in-
teresting note on a little, old-fashioned church
and churchyard at St. Helena, a less-than-town-
ship between Greensborough and Eltham—only
14 miles from Melbourne G.P.O. Here, in the
bush, way back in ’59, came one Major Beale,
who had been paymaster of the troops that
guarded the “man of destiny” at St. Helena.
When Beale’s wife died, soon after, he erected a
small but beautiful memorial chapel. Outside
the chapel are a few scattered graves—that of
Beale himself was opened lately to receive the
body of his son. Another holds the remains of
late P.M. Graham Webster, whose headstone
tells that he was “ the last of his race, who des-
cended in one unbroken line from father to son
for 779 years.”
“ Wusslean ” : A noticeable feature of the
celebration of Dr. Gosman’s 50th year in the Con-
gregational ministry, which took place recently at
Hawthorn (Melbourne), was the chilling silence
in which references to his work on behalf of the
Anti-Sweating League were received. It was the
eloquent Deakin who broached the subject of Dr.
Gosman’s efforts to ameliorate the conditions of
labor, and I was nearly lulled to sleep by that
silver voice. I was roused by the fall of a solid
substance on my corn. It was my breath, which
had frozen through a sudden change in the atmo-
sphere of the hall. On looking round, I found
almost the whole audience were stiff and coated
with ice. As Mauger gave additional testimony
the silence and cold became Arctic, and a boxed
look and a stare into vacancy became fixed on
every face as its owner pretended to be anywhere
else but listening to what his pastor had done for
the workers. As I left, the audience was being
gradually thawed with hot coffee.

Thomas Bent, talking at Collingwood the
other night, stated that he went to Sunday-school
in the district 50 years ago, and learned about
Jonah, Noah, and the prophets, from Nathaniel
Kinsman, the Melbourne marriage parson of a
more recent period. Having related this as
though it were an interesting conjunction of two
famous names, Bent went on to assure his audience
that it was the truth. Bent’s personal anecdotes
have been so much contradicted that he|}looks
around for disbelief when he tells a new yarn.
And he gets it.
“ G.H.” writes :
I rise to move a vote of confidence in Thomas Price,
Premier-to-come of South Australia. Heard Thomas
speak a piece in Fitzgeralds’ circus building. A plain,
grizzly-bearded man, without enough frill about him to
equip a lady insect for presentation at the Buckingham
Palace of the King Grasshopper. Thomas’s oration was
mainly aimed at young Australia—the section of the
population that talks dog, and horse, and cricket, and
football, and other kinds of leather without ceasing, but
doesn’t know whether it is on the Federal roll or not.
Brother Price exhorted this section of the populace to
attend to the affairs of the country, and to take its best
girl to the pursuit of the muddy globe of wind afterwards.
He apo made some jokes, but he is a failure as a
humorist. Political humorists seem to be a failure in
Australia. Book at Reid, and Bill Wilks, and Con-
roy, and Joke Carruthers, and Joke Cook, and other
vendors of the chestnut that amuses but availeth not
when no-confidence motions are hammering at the Minis-
terial door.
A piquant incident in the life of a Mel-
bourne politician: The M.P. was heading down
Collins-street when he discovered himself to be an
object of particular interest to a tall young man in
a seedy overcoat, and feeling a keen, admonitory
pain as of a bite in his left ear, he increased his
pace. The man in the seedy coat did likewise, till
at last they both broke into a run. Finally the
M.P. sought cover in an oyster saloon, but the
hunter rushed through on his track, spotted his
victim, and pounced on him. Then with the
courage of despair the legislator faced the situa-
tion. “It’s no good, I tell you. I’m bled dry ;
the ear-biters have been at me all morning. I
haven’t got a sixpence. They’ve not even left me
a tram-fare to go home with.” “ Good enough,”
answered the hunter brightly. “ Here’s sixpence.
You can have it if you’ll stand for me for five
minutes. I’m a caricaturist.”

An ancient and well-worn link between
this century and the militant days of parson-poli-
tician Lang snapped at Ashfield (N.S.W.) a week
or so ago. Lang induced several Scotch school-
masters to come out this way—among them the
deceased, a classical scholar, Armstrong, M.A.,
whom he sent up to Maitland. At 50 or there-
abouts the schoolmaster followed Lang into the
pulpit, and hammered out sermons at Tamworth
for another 30 years. It is in Dr. Lang’s old barn-
like Kirk on Church Hill (Sydney) that Dill
Macky shows his calves nowadays.
Dr. Kevin Izod O’Doherty, of Brisbane,
passed out at the age of 81, had an interesting
career. While studying medicine as a young man
in 1848 he joined R. D. Williams in founding the
Irish Tribune, Dublin. After the fifth number
“the Castle” authorities suppressed the paper
and O’Doherty was gaoled on a general charge of
treason-felony. On the third trial the jury agreed
and the sentence was 10 years. O’Doherty was
sent to Tasmania, where he arrived in 1849, and
was released on parole. In 1854 he received his
pardon, conditionally that he did not live
under the British flag, so he went to Paris.
Soon after he secretly invaded Ireland, and
stayed just long enough to marry Miss
Kelly (“Eva,” of the Nation), who survives
him. Receiving a full-blown pardon in 1856,
he took his medical degree at Dublin, and a few
years later went to Queensland. He sat for a
while in the Assembly as member for B isbane,
and afterwards mouldered uneongenially for nine
years (1877-86) in the Upper House. He went
back to Ireland in ’B6 and became M.P. for Meath,
but the bogs didn’t seem the same after all those
years, and in a few months he made for Queens-
land again and hardly ever left it any more.
Some day a politician may arise in N.S.W.
who will shake himself free of party influences,
and go in for opening up the coast lands by strong
means. The best of those lands are in the hands
of mortgagees, who will not sell them, and will
only let them on terms that make small-area
settlements a snare and a slavery. Unmortgaged
owners —who are rare—follow the example of the
mortgagees ; and so the country —pierced with
railways and rained upon with moderate regu-
larity—lies to a great extent under lock and key,
while the country where rain is precarious and
railways are far between, takes in and does for the
man who hopes to make a living on a small area.

Aboriginalities.
“Paul Cupid”: Of the 93 Australian
snakes, a man might be bitten by 31 without the
slightest need to make a will. In the case of 61
others, he need not make his will either, if he
only has some presence of mind. A bottle of

“ Thurla ” boils over :
“Periwinkle” (B. 22 6/’O5) emerges from his shell to
doubt my veracity on things in general, and to pour sar-
casm and ridicule on a harmless bee item. Well as I’ve
had only a thirty years bush experience, p’raps I’ve not
yet developed his acute sense of smell. In my innocence
I wrote of things as I’ve found them, not as I imagine
they should be. Perhaps “Periwinkle” has a well-de-
veloped olfactory organ, and can locate a “ sugar-bag” or
a decayed animal by such means. Still, it would be ex-
tremely dangerous to attempt to pass an apiary with
such a sensitive nose ; it might become over-
crowded. Have seen the semi-civilised blacks
locate nests in various ways besides flight-tracing.
F'rinstance but this idea is rarely practised ow-
ing to slowness—by searching at night. Their method
is to place the ear against the bole of the tree
the humming noise of the swarm betraying its where-
abouts. As regards the “ down ’’-business, this is the
method. Finding bees clustered at a water-hole, the
black will sprinkle particles of flour on one insect,’ and
note the time between flight and return. If the interval
is long, that insect is suppressed and another substi-
tuted until a quick returner is found. A particle of
thistle-down, moistened with the vegetable fluid if handy,
is then fastened to the bee s abdomen. Combo follows
the mark until the nest is discovered. For a wager, once
on the Castlereagh, a blatant individual of “’Winkle’s”
turn of mind, undertook one spring morning to smell out
“ sugar-bags.” All went serene until he struck a wattle
scrub and lost the trail, if ever he had it, amongst the
heavy honey odor of the wattle-. Thereon a sceptical
and blasd person with a shocking lack of smeil found
several nests by lining che flight of departing bees.
“ Periwinkle,” in the words of Shakspeare, “ Go to 1"

An instance of a girl’s remarkable endur-
ance comes from Daylesford (Vic.). Isabella
Evans set out Avith some milk, but put the can
down on the way to chase a coav, and got lost in a
dense fog. She tramped all night and all next
day, but on the second night took shelter in a
hollow log. She Avas hatless and coatless, and her
light dress was saturated Avith rain. When
discovered by Dandenong trackers, she had been
five days without food, and was sitting on a
stump, her clothes almost torn off by under-
groAvth, her boots worn through, and her feet a
mass of blisters. They put her on a pony, and
she rode into (Jlenlyon for refreshment.

“Elmore”: Springvale, the first Vic.
estate ready for closer settlement, is now awaiting
the settler. The blocks (20 of them, of something
over 100 acres each) are long and narrow, running
from the Kiewa river back to the hills, and I guess
the man who gets a block has a long row to hoe.
He’ll want a Big bank account to fence and stock
it, and a big heart to keep going. All the best of
the estate is under water in the winter. At the
present time there is not an acre of the flats above
water ; in one paddock nearly 3000 pairs of rabbits
have been trapped during May and June. If the
rising young cocky takes to the rabbit and leaves
the cows at dad’s, he may do well.
AN EDUCATION ITEM.
NICK OLD LADY: “And do you go to school, my little man?”

Albury (N.S.W.) supplies a story about a
family sow that was stranded on an island during
the recent flood, and, finding the position despe-
rate, rose up like one hog, took her six baby pigs
on her back, and swam for a place of safety.
Possibly the tale was only manufactured to call
attention to the advantages of Albury as a site for
the Federal capital, but it recalls another yarn
with which it has no visible connection. Years
and years ago, in one of the great Murrumbidgee
floods, a young woman was washed away with her
two baby boys. She hung on to a tree all night,
but towards morning she dropped one of the
infants from sheer exhaustion. In after years
she used to say bitterly that the one great mistake
of her life was that she didn’t drop the other one
also, and almost everybody who met him at the
pub. agreed with her.

“ Dem ” : I woke up the other night with
a great start and thought the bottom had dropped
out of the world and that the globe was falling
through into the loose universe. I still had in
my ears the echo of the father of a crash, and
my mate on the other bunk was yelling like
the devil with a stomach-ache nine miles
long. I sat up and asked him what was
the matter. He said that conspirators had
fired cannon at him through the window,
that his head had been amputated, that he
had lost both legs and was covered with
blood and sinking fast, also that he was
dead. I struck a light and found that the
bottle of yeast on the candle-box had ex-
ploded, and that my mate was covered
with the escaped substance and with
broken bottle. When I reassured him and
brought him round, he sat up and said in
a tremulous voice that he never before fully
realised the horrors of war, and he wouldn’t
take Linevitch's job for £lOOO a year. He would
rather stick to ring-barking.
* * • *
“Madcap”: Has any one noticed the
effect of music on milk production ? Recently
bought a nanny goat. Was cheerfully puzzled to
find a great increase in the milk every Wednesday
and Friday morning, the yield being almost double
the quantity given on other days. After discover-
ing and dismissing various theories to account for
it, came to the conclusion that a brass band, that
played in a hall next the paddock where nanny
slept, was the cause. The band practised on
Tuesday and Thursday nights. When it finally
gave up the goat ran dry.
* * * *
“F.A.L.” : At Queenstown, Lake Waka-
tipu (M.L.), recently, I saw a Maori sitting on a
stump, unconcernedly watching his hat being
carried away by a whirlwind. Asked why he
made no attempt to recover the garment that he
wore on his head, he replied: “The wind only
pull my leg. The time I make it the run to get
my hat, he blow somewhere else. No, I stop
here ; and by-and-bye might he blow back again,
when he see he can’t fool me ! ” I saw that
Maori two hours later, on the same stump—still
hatless and hopeful.
“ C.X.G.” : Was much struck, on a recent
visit to S.W. Queensland, with the language used
by the bushmen in those parts. The better friends
they are, the worse their epithets. Was talking
to one of them in his hut, when a man walked in
and called my host a bald-headed old etc. He
also asked him how the Sheol he was getting on.
My host called him a blighted nioigrel and a
cross-eyed old etc. Thought at first they were
deadly enemies, and fled into the fireplace to be
out of the way of the bloodshed ; but they proved
to be old mates who hadn’t seen one another for
some time. After some experience of the district
I inquired earnestly why you were always ex-
pected to call your best friend an illegitimate
cow, or something even worse, and the district
was shocked at the foolishness of the question.

“San ilemo”: Be “A.C.T.” (15/6/’CS)
on queer methods of snake-killing. Years ago,
on Emerald Downs station (Q.), I saw a flash
stockman pick up a Eft. carpet-snake by the tail,
swing it round his head, and crack it whip
fashion. The snake’s head flew off. Till then I
had thought this snake-whip business was a bush
lie. I have heard of a man who did the
same with poisonous snakes, but haven’t met him.
It appears that the joint next the head is the
weakest in a_ snake’s construction, and in the
whip-flick action the full force of the jerk is
concentrated upon that end.

“ Gilrooney ” : The bushman’s vocabulary
of occupation labels is almost inexhaustible. For
instance, a bullock-driver is called a bullocky, a
bovine-puncher, an ox-persuader, a horny-steerer, a
cow-puncher, a buffalo-navigator, a steer-pilot,
and goodness knows what else. But no one has
ever been innocent enough to call him a clergyman.
ANOTHER SCOTCH AGONY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Heoh, si r ! but I was pleased indeed
In your good Bulletin tae read
Yin vera wise poetic screed
Frae Jock M'Fadyen—
It shows the chiel’ has got a heid,
An’ no’ a bad yin.
It s fac as death what Jock indites
Regairdin’ danger tae the whites
Ower whilk we’ve had some bluidless fights
An’ clishniaclaver;
Gude kens, it’s time we got our rights
An’ less palaver.
Ma ain experience gangs tae clinch
Your Jock’s assairtions, every inch,
For ay, mon dear, I’ve felt the pinch
O’ competeetion
Wi’ alien folk, an’ had tae flinch
Frae ma poseetion.
On landin’ here I looked aboot—
Before ma siller a’ rin oot—
For something that ma cairds wad suit,
An’, at a sale-yaird,
I bought an orchard wi’ some fruit,
Forbye a kail-yard.
I thocht, ye ken, I’d grow some k. il,
Wi’ neaps an siclike wares for sale,
An’ dreamed o’ nae sic’ word as fail
When yince in motion ;
But soon a mon wi’ long pig-tail
Dispelled ma notion.
His name, he said, was John Ah Ivee,
Wha no lang syne cam’ ower the sea,
And jabbered little but Chinee
(An awfu’ lingo !) —
He said, “ Me glow good cabbagee ”
The heathen dingo !
He settled near a wee bit creek
No’ far frae me—and, ere a week,
He’d tawtie, onion, kail, an’ leek
In long rows planted.
He seemed tae ken (the yellow sneak !)
What white folk wanted.
As Johnny, like the rats an’ mice.
Could live on tawtie-skins an’ rice,
An’ sell his goods aboot half-price,
It’s no a wonder
That, though his stuff was no ower nice,
He got me under.
I found the game wad never pay—
That things got waur frae day to day,
An’ that I couldna’ pay ma way
Wi’ a’ my hustle ;
I scarce could niak’ enough each day
Tae wet ma whustle.
Gude kens, I wrought baith late an’ lang
As hard as ony mon could bang,
Frae daylight, when the birdies sang,
Till gloamin’ dusky,
An’ hadna’ three bawbees tae gang
An’ buy a whusky !
So, as misfortune pressed me sair,
I sold the kail-yard, in despair,
An’ for the same got little mail-
Than met expenses ;
An’ noo I’m livin’ ony where—
’Mang trees an’ fences.
BUNNY ON HIS LAST JOURNEY.
A record load (2022) of rabbits from Tarana (N.S.W.), on the first
stage of the trip to London.
There’s naething I can get to do—
I’m sick in heart an’ speerit too ;
Alas ! the day I sairly rue
That I left hame,
An’ crossed the ocean wide an’ blue
Tae seek for fame.
Australia’s no’ the land for me
While swarmed wi’ Coolie and Chinee—
Gi’e me the land o’ barley bree
An’ guid thick brose—
The breezy mountain an’ the lea
Whaur heather grows.
But I maun pack ma swag the noo,
An’ shouther ma bit blanket blue
That e’en I seethe daylight through-
fit’s like ma billy)—
An’ I’ll awa’ tae Jamberoo
Or Wollondilly.
Eh mon ! but I wad like tae see
That Jock M'Fadyen ere I dee—
Tae him a hand I’d freely gi’e
Wi’ freendly grippin’,
An’ mebbe juist a drap o’ bree
We’d baith be sippin’.
Weel, Chance, ye ken’s a fickle jade,
An’ frae oor wark wi’ pen or spade—
In fac’, frae ony airthly trade
She’ll sometimes drag us ;
An’ gin we meet (if’t can be made)
We’ll ha’e a haggis !
Sae fareweel Jock—l must be gane,
An’ tramp the country a’ my ane,
Tae wait for wark here’s a’ iii vain—
I’m maist a beggar ;
An’ aiblins ye’ll no’ see again
Auld Rab M'Gregor.

“Jimmie Pan” : Rain has fallen so fre-
quently and so copiously in Broken Hill since the
beginning of this climatically-changed year, that
many of: the folk are showing signs of web-footed-
ness. From the line of lode, a gloriously green
panorama stretches away for miles, dotted here
and there with patches of extremely early white
spring flowers. The reserves have that lush
luxuriance that characterises English rural
scenery. Everything points to a wonderfully
fine spring. So far, this year has given a striking
example of that peculiarly alluring weather and
growth which tempt pastoralists to take the risk
of overstocking for a year or two, in the hope of
making a punch big enough to enable them to
escape from exile for good. I have almost for-
gotten what a dust storm is like. I hope to go on
forgetting.

‘‘ P. Winkle ” : Round the Kyabrama-
pootra (Vic.) fringe the large landowners swear by
reynard as the champion rabbit-exterminator,
whose fee of a few lambs in the frost-days they do
not grudge. According to these friends, reynard
locates the bunny family by sense of smell in the
burrow, and sinks a diagonal shaft down to his
prey. On the other hand, the farmers and poultry-
raisers hurl oaths at mention of fox, and organise
hunts to scour the swamp areas for him every off-
day. A single fox has been known to exterminate
a flock of 40 gobblers in a night.

“Janor”: In reply to “ Werrimee’s ”
query re length of snakes (G/7/’O5). I once shot a
carpet snake at Mount Hulton Creek (Q.) which
measured 14ft. Sin. per rule. If I’d got him in
any other country I would have known him for an
Australian, he was in such a deplorable state of
tick. Couldn’t stick a pin into him anywhere
without impaling one of those parasites.

“Alec Zander”: About two years after
the disastrous end of Burke and Wills’ badly-
managed expedition, one of their camels
made its appearance on the Culgoa, about
40 miles from Bourke. Tim, the blackboy
(who used to bring up the horses), was the
first to see it. He turned up one morning
in a flustered state, and handed in his
verbal report: “ Mine bin go longa lignum
lookout yarrauian, mine bin see ’em debbil
debbil sit down there ; all same yarraman
baal yarraman, all same emu baal emu.
I bin clear out longa creek swim ’nother
side.. Bimeby mine see ’em track long
a yarraman, him bin ‘ whisht ’ go to plurry
’ell.” Which was in the main true, as it
was two days before the horses were found.
They had met the camel and gone
“whisht.” The humpy one must have
travelled 700 or 800 miles, from Innamincka
in S.A. through Queensland into N.S.W.
It stood round and grunted on the station
for several months, and as no one in those
parts had at that time seen a camel, it was
a great curiosity. I never heard what
became of it eventually. As a kiddie I
saw the tree under which Burke was sup-
posed to have died. It was only about
100 yd. from the stockyard at Innamincka
station, then owned by my dad. Wills’
grave was further up the creek, on a
waterhole called Mulcumbah.

The latest scheme of rabbit destruc-
tion is that of veterinary surgeon John
Pottie. The vet. proposes (with £5OOO of Go-
vernment money) to run the rabbits into large
iron rollers, roll them into cakes like oil cake, and
feed them to sheep.
• * * *
The fighting qualities of the horse are
generally overlooked, in spite of the fact that, in
India, “ bad ” horses have, time after time, been
victorious in battles with tigers. There was a
“bad” horse in Ashburton district (M.L.), which
got to holts with a bull one day. The mobile
tactics of his enemy puzzled the bovine warrior,
and the hoofs made him sick of the battle in no
time. There was no chance for bayonet-work
with liis horns, for the horse brought his hind bat-
teries into play at the very beginning, and the
bull went in search of a doctor who could give him
a cure for indigestion. Writer has a keen respect
for a bull with personal intentions, but, if he was
sitting on a fence, and had to chance the teeth
that a bull wears on the top of his head, or the
jaws and back legs of a wicked horse, he would
choose the bull with something like enthusiasm.
“The Prooshan ” : The wild pigs that
“ H.G.” (B. 13/7/ 05) hunted on the Lower Mac-
quarie in the 80’s were only wild at being called
wild, I should imagine. If “ H.P.” ever did any
real wild-pig hunting as I have done on Nap Nap,
he would find the business a little more heroic.
In the first place, “ H.Gr.,” on attempting to run
those pigs to a standstill, would see them do a
magic slew, give a “ chop-chop,” and then charge.
“H.Gr.” would then leave for the doctor mighty
fast too, or some old blue boar would eat him
before he saw medical aid on the horizon. We
had special lances made in Hay, 10ft. long, with
heads 2ft. long, of the best steel, and fit to pene-
trate the hide of a commercial traveller, but we
couldn’t ventilate those hogs with them. A
station regulation required each jackeroo to carry
a lance, but most of us planted the absurd spikes
when we got away from the homestead. The
Snider was the only thing. But I knew one old
fellow who made a big strong bag with an iron
circle at the mouth, and when the pig charged he
let him ram himself into the bag, then, throwing
him on his back, lie drove a long knife into his
sticking spot, and scored that way. We only got
fid. a pair for their ears, which was a ridiculously
small bonus, considering the way they used to
munch up weak sheep.
* * * *
“Fluff”: Starlings in South Gippsland
fly about in flocks of some thousands. Their
method of breeding suggests the wholesale plan of
the poultry-growers’ incubators. A dozen roughly
constructed nests (apparently run up by contract)
are dabbed about the branches of one tree. Each
nest holds about 40 eggs, and a number of parent
birds hatch in one nest. When the fledglings get
a move on them, the atmosphere is darkened by
dense clouds of feathered creatures. Do not know
any other bird that turns out its young by the
bucketful.
* * * *
“ Jimmie Pannikin ” : A few notes on
Milparinka, Gibberburra, and other townships in
the extreme north-west of N.S. W. —the real right
out-back. Striking features are the glass heaps
and the goats. The former make one think that
these townships of many vicissitudes and virtues
must have had a riotous youth before settling
down into a steady, parched, middle age. Some
day, in spite of scoffers and unbelievers, this same
north-west corner will develop into a big, prosper-
ous mining field. Then, of course, the good, gay
times will come—but, alas ! the hardy old pioneers,
the grilled but gritty battlers, the openers-up of
the district, will be resting in the Great Beyond—
that is, if such vigorous folk ever do really rest.
Meanwhile, it is good to see the outback com-
munities so refreshingly free from the petty,
paltry cliqueism that makes mean and miserable
the lives of men and women in caste-cursed towns.
In this terrific remoteness, the station folk, the
police magistrate, the teacher, the doctor, the
postmaster, the constable, the storekeeper and
their woman-kind all pull together socially. They
read, ride, golf, play tennis, meet and mix like
the members of a happy family. The utter
absence of tinpot exclusiveness leads to the blessed
belief that, out on these vast plains, the largeness
of Nature gets into people’s souls and expands
them. Certainly the social life is an eye-opener
to me, used to curious conventions in coastal town-
ships, where the banker’s lady looks down on the
teacher’s wife, who patronises the constable’s
wife, who snubs the cocky’s missus, who slights
the laborer’s woman. Two years’ life and labor
west o’ Darling would be a liberal education for
hosts of comfortable city folk, who know not the
land they live in. Through drought and disaster
the flower of Western hospitality blooms perenni-
ally. It is fresher and fairer and sweeter than
the haw-haw hospitality of many much more
favored spots. It is, indeed, something to have
experienced the hearty, homely, downright
kindness of the outbackers. It is a thing that
helps to make one bigger, bolder and better.

“ Yia ” : Have you ever seen an aborigine
thoroughly scared ? If not, try a diver in full rig-
out on one and note result. Years ago, when the
as. Wentworth was piled up on the North Head
of Port Denison, an agile nigger from the inlands
dived into the water-logged hold. A diver was
roaming round down below, and they met. llie
nigger emerged grey with funk to halfway down
bis back, and lit out for home and mother like a
black telegraph message. He must have spread
the glad tidings, too, for even now the coast
blacks that way say “ Debble-debble belonga
Northead.”

No need to bonus babies in Australia until we’ve tried
bonusing and protecting industries. When jobs are
plentiful babies won’t'be backward in coming D.SiC.:
What is your amateur artist’s address? Peter P.’: No ;
will take some action shortly W.F.W. :_The “animal
suicide ” season is 0ff.... Jeesiah (or something like that) :
Certainly Socialism as you define it—the absolute owner-
ship by the people at large of land and capital and all the
means of production —is good if the people elect rulers
■who make good use of them. Always put in that. The
privately-owned ironworks might make a profit for the
owner if he was the right kind of owner, even if the law
compelled him to pay his workers 60s. a week. The same
ironworks, owned by the State, if it was the wrong kind
of State, might divide all the profit among the workers
and give them only 30s. It is bosh to say that everybody
must be either for or against your brand of complete
Socialism without reservations. But monopolies which
give control over a whole community—such as the rail-
ways—should be in State hands whether the State makes
them pay or not. That is not merely a question of
profit, but of preventing the possibility of an unbearable
tyranny

POLITICAL
POINTS
G. B. Edwards, M.H.R., is placing on the
business paper of the House of Reps, a Bill deal-
ing' with elective Ministries. His idea is to have
both Houses, sitting as a sort of committee, elect
the Cabinet direct by ballot.
-/..1 v,
goods is - promoted by the N.S. Wales
A.N.A., which has secured the Agricultural
Grounds for the display. The movement deserves
the sympathy of Australians. Mr. M. A. Martin,
134 Pitt-street, Sydney, is the secretary.
Sydney Telegraph on P. Fysh :
The empty talk of the urgency for buttressing a few
industries alyut Melbourne with more Protection made
no impressimi upon him.
Anyone who reads this weary hackneyed scoff
about “Melbourne” would think that Victoria
was the only place in Australia that had industries.
DISAPPOINTED IN MARRIAGE.
“Farley looks off-color since he got back from
Europe. Is it that girl of his! Has he been
disappointed in love ?”
“No, he married the girl.”
N.S.W. Premier Carruthers says darkly,
like the villain in the melodrama, that “a time
will yet come,” and that the papers which reveal
the gloomy and cavernous machinations of Bill
Lyne in the matter of providing a residence for the
Governor-General at Sydney will be laid onthe table
one of these days. There is a horrible tendency
to cheap shriek about Carruthers, and his half-
formed inclination to bathe in blood, or to expose
somebody in the fifth act, or to lay some blighting
document on the table, is suggestive of the small
boy’s book about pirates in top-boots. Sydney
Telegraph, which backs Carruthers in this
matter, says it doesn’t know what the horrid
secret is, but that the papers are of “considerable
importance,” that they were discovered in a
“peculiar manner” —doubtless in the haunted
chamber —and that Joey’s action will “ bring
things to a head.”
Mort’s Dock, Sydney, says that with a little
tariff help it can set 2000 men at work boat-build-
ing—about 1400 more than it now employs. That
is a direct challenge to the Australian Parliament
to promptly provide the tariff help.
The Maoriland Farmers’ Union, which has
taken the part of the sturdy beggar pleading for
the freehold with something like menaces is
rousing the Labor Unions to fury. The great
bulk of these farmers who are clamoring for
the freehold are the gentry who took up
on lease the lands which the Govern-
ment secured for them by the expro-
priation of the big landlords. Now the
Unions point out that in the first case
the resumption of large estates for closer
settlement was really a process of land
nationalisation for the whcle
people, and not for a section
of them. To buy land for-
cibly from the large free-
holder and give it to the
small freeholder would be a
hideous example of that “ class
legislation ” to oppose which the
Farmers’ Union exists.
The Maoriland Tory party, which was to
do such great things this session, broke its first
front tooth on Seddon’s armor last week, when it
mustered up 10 votes in the House of Representa-
tives against Seddon’s 53 on the Government pro-
posal to raise the old age pension to 10s. a week.
From the report of the recent Labor con-
ference
Mr. O’Malley, was a Protectionist, Dut was not
going to sacrifice such men as, for instance, Mr. Fowler,
to the fetish of fiscalism.
In other words, Mr. O’Malley reckons that Pro-
tection is good for Australia, but he prefers
Fowler to Australia.
N.S.W. Education Minister sensibly pro-
poses to make an effort to reduce the number of
schools without reducing the facilities for school-
ing, and thus enable a higher grade of education
to be given in more; centralised schools. Unfor-
tunately he cannot touch the most hopeless
schools, those in the far out-back, where the State
has to limp lamely, with an academy and a police
station, after the settlers forced out to the desert
by land monopoly on the coast. It is impossible
to centralise schools there; the only effective
reform is to bring the settlers in a body back to
the coast.
Ex-Australian, It. L. Outlrwaite,
who is to fight Joseph Chamberlain for
West Birmingham next British elections,
will hang: the issue of the fight on the
pro-Asiatic policy of Chamberlain ; and
on that issue Australian sympathy will
be with him.
S. M. Herald’s “On the Land”
column will yet land Granny on the rocks.
Scarcely an issue but an indignant con-
tributor beseeches the paper to insist on
the Government taking prompt action to
place some branch or other of private
enterprise under strict State supervision
and interference; and one country farmer
lately stated that the Butter Commission
revelations were enough to drive all the
people on the land into the ranks of the
Labor party.
Queensland Treasurer Kidston is
running the country on strict business
lines. He recently met r "a budget of
requests for money from local governing
bodies and others with the answer:
“ First pay up your arrears to the State,
and then 1 will talk to you.” At first the
local authorities held over the head of
the stout little Labor Treasurer the ter-
rors of political assassination ; now they
are beginning to see the wisdom of his
determined attitude, and many have
written to thank him for the good advice.
Councils that were adopting a policy of
drift have faced their liabilities, put on
a small extra rate, paid back loans owing
to the State, and secured fresh loans
for progressive works, the repayment of
which has been provided for by sinking
funds on Kidston’s model. If the Better-
ment Tax on unimproved land values
goes through in the coming session, Kid-
ston claims he can slay the deficit, and
keep it dead so long as he is in office. Of
course, if Philp comes back it will be
different. Philp could find a deficit on an
uninhabited island, and could get in debt
on an iceberg.
“Jack” Want, of N.S.W., states
that, during a recent visit to the North,
“he was forcibly struck with the sad
effects of Federation in Queensland.”
But just when Mr. Want might have
become interesting he stopped, and what
effects he alluded to are not explained.
It is true that Federation reduced Queens-
land’s Protective tariff, and thereby partly
wrecked Walker’s great iron foundry at
Maryborough, and many other indus-
tries ; but as Mr. Want says he is a Free-
trader, he can’t surely be alluding to that.
If he had had his way, there would never
have been any Walker’s iron foundry.
St. Kilda (Vic.) is beginning to wonder if
there is any connection between the fact that
politician George Turner is President of the St.
Kilda Yacht Club and the success so far of the
club’s impudent raid on a bit of public land. The
club first got a “permissive occupancy” of a
valuable bit of St. Kilda foreshore. Now it is
proposed that the land be vested in it for ever
without rent, and that the club seems pretty sure
of its ground is shown by the fact that it has put
up expensive buildings.
THIS MIGHT HAVE HAPPENED TO
AUSTRALIA.
SUVA, Monday.—Arrived : Manuka, R.M.S., from Syd-
ney via Brisbane ; Virawa, str., with 900 Indian immi-
grants from f ’ htjf Sailed : Manuka, R.M.S., for

A proposal with a lot to be said in its
favor is before the Vic. Parliament on the motion
of Watt, M.L.A. —that all public pensioners
should be compelled to reside in the State from
which they draw their pensions. Such a provision
would aim one good blow at the absentee evil.
The Victorian old age pensioner has to spend his
7s. a week in Victoria ; and why shouldn't the old
left-off judge spend his thousand or two a year
there also ?
Mr. .Reid who, according to the chief
local Orange bigot, “has proved himself true to
the Orange cause at every turn,” is just now
engaged in brooding over the dying embers of his
anti-Socialism campaign, puffing out the while
quotations from the Pope against Socialism.
Just for the moment G. H. Reid almost loves the
Pope.
Prime Minister Deakin announces early
action to set up a land defence system for Australia
on the Swiss model—so often advocated by The
Bulletin. But possibly he will be wrecked
against the theory that compulsory training is an
outrage on the liberty of the subject. And about
that battleship, the two swift cruisers, and a few
torpedo boats ?
In spite of his bellowing, Victoria’s Thomas
Bent plods pretty steadily on the road to Progress
nowadays, and his feet can often be heard pound-
ing heavily on the up-grade. He is now building
a measure to carry out the Danish system of at
least tackling the outskirts of the unemployed
question by small settlements, with advances for
implements and stock, together with instruction
in the ways of making a living on the land, and
he is prepared to spend a whole £lOO,OOO on the
scheme. If Bent makes even a small success of
this attempt at solving a great problem, this paper
will forgive him some more of the hard things it
has said about him.
If it were not for the bad company he has
kept politically all his life, Victorian Premier
Bent might almost be accepted as a good Demo-
crat in his old age. The other day Boyd, M.L.A.,
whose coal mines are heavily subsidised by the
State, which carries the coal at an unpayable rate,
“offered his strenuous opposition to the State
brick works.” Then, “and to coal mines at
Korumburra ? ” suggested Bent. Boyd said
wildly, “ The Government never put one penny
into that industry,” and Bent became incoherent
in his denunciation: “Wh&t are you talking
about ? How much are you getting every week ?
. . . We will take bricks and coal together. . . .
You of all men ! Upon my word ! ” And Boyd,
the cadger, fled in dismay from the fat man’s
wrath.
A rather remarkable admission crept into
Melbourne Argus, in the shape of a quiet, little,
six-line paragraph, a few days ago. It was to the
effect that the Melbourne produce exporters who
have dealings with South Africa are now asking
the Government to certify to the quality of their
oats, chaff, onions, etc. More remarkable still,
these exporters are offering to pay the cost of this
supervision. And yet it is only a few months
since the Argus was exhausting its rather scant
vocabulary in condemnation of a Bill which pro-
vided for this very thing, and was declaring that
the trade could do far better if left alone.
On that occasion the Dairymen’s Association
waggled its long ears and brayed so furiously that
the Bill was lost. Now the Dairymen's Associa-
tion has apparently got its ears tangled in the
ti-tree outback ; at any rate, its bray has been
silent for some time.
“ G.H.” thinks he sees an analogy :
The political situation in Victoria reminds me of a
patch in disused Greek history. Mr. Pericles, Prime
Minister of Athens —a cold, iceberg-like, Irvine sort of
person—passed in his earthly resignation, and Cleon,
Esq., took his job. Cleon mended bellows, or ran a tan-
nery, or had some sort of connection with the leather
trade. He was popular wi th the crowd because he had a
larg'e, circular voice, which ran downhill with great
rapidity, and left a broad trail of promises behind it. The
fish-and-chips nation was much impressed by Cleon’s
opinion of himself, and gave him a free hand in the opera-
tions against Sphacteria. Sphacteria was a small island,
not half the size of an Australian deficit, off the Greek
coast, and the said island refused to be dragged into the
fried-fish League. Demosthenes, the David Gaunson
of the period, had been bowling blue-metal at the
Spbacts for years, but without doing any particular
damage. So Cleon loaded his voice into a large Athenian
dray and went down and bellowed at Sphacteria in person,
and the island surrendered immediately. This shows
what may be done by bellowing—sometimes.
M.L. Parliament had hardly hung up its-
coat this session when a petition was presented
demanding the cancellation of an the
Supreme Court to destroy the books of the defunct
Colonial Bank next November. In connection
with these books some purple yarns involving
politicians are afloat.
A further instalment of Bent and Swin--
burne’s Socialism is being thrust on the Victorian
farmer, per medium of the Dairy Bill. The
measure sets aside the municipal authorities-
altogether, and places all inspection of dairies,
butter factories, cow yards, etc., under Govern-
ment inspectors. All dairies are to be licensed,
and the fees are calculated to pay for the inspec-
tors —or instructors, for these gentlemen are in--
tended to educate and convince rather than inspect
and prosecute. They are to keep their eyes, not
only on cow-yards and sheds, dairies, amount of
preservatives and moisture in butter and so forth,
but also on diseases of cattle, breeding of stock,
and the maintaining of a regular supply of home-
grown fodder crops. The farmer is to be taught
and trained in the way he should go, and prose-
cuted when he won’t go.
Some items of evidence put before the-
N.S.W. Arbitration Court: —
The wages ot laborers in timber yards . . . lucky ones-
got 255. to 395. per week.
Some were not earning 10s. a week. Where 20 men
were wanted 100 applied.
If boy labor were reduced the local manufacturers
could not compete against imported doors and sashes.
In Queensland girls were employed.
It all resolves itself into a rule of three sum.
Either wages must be kept down so as to com-
pete with the cheapest foreigner and with the-
foreigner who works most economically through
having a large market secured to him and thus-
being able to carry on operations on a huge scale ;
or the foreign competitor must be shut out; or
the industry must die. No blinking of the fiscal
issue can alter that position.
Premier Butler, of South Aus., doesn't
know when he is dead. As soon as the Assembly
met, the other day, Price, leader of the Labor
party, fired a “sudden death” motion at him and
knocked out the long, lean derelict by 24 votes to
17. And still Butler didn’t resign. He had hung
on to office for weeks after being flattened
out in the elections; and he still hung on when
the House trampled on his remains. Price’s
“sudden death” motion, according to S. Austra-
lian practice, was that the House adjourn, which is
a brief way of taking the business out of the
Government’s hands ; and when the House did
adjourn Butler merely smiled and said that it
would suit him splendidly if Parliament adjourned
for 20 years. Yet Butler gets the support of the
people who talk most about “ Constooshnal
Guvment.”
The first questionable land transaction on
record in New South Wales : Governor King,
on the eve of his retirement, made a grant of what
is now Newtown—or the best part
of it—to his successor. Governor
Bligh. As soon as Bligh was in
office he made a grant of land
to Governor King. So both were
satisfied.
SUBJECT TO HALLTUCINATION.
REID : “ Ah, well, there’s an end of it. I’ve
killed it and buried it now.”
MRS. DAILY TELEGRAPH: “ Don’t be too certain,
George. Nobody else seems to think so.”
(Pause.) “ Perhaps—perhaps we only imagine
it! ”
Tasmania opened its State Parliament
with its finances in a dreadful state, and popula-
tion getting out of the island and over to the
mainland as fast as it can—just as it has been
doing for most of the last 40 years. The Gover-
nor’s speech said his Ex. was pleased to note that
the “staple industries” were prosperous. The
next clause said his Ex. was glad that the cow and
bull industry was prosperous. Next one said that
his Ex. was gratified to see that the agricultural
industry was prosperous. After that another
clause said that his Ex. was able to sleep better'
after noting that the mining industry was pro-
sperous. His Ex. then went on to say that it
warmed his heart to learn that the timber indus-
try was prosperous. Also, his Ex. was overjoyed
to hear that the fruit industry was prosperous.
And, finally, to make sure that nothing was for-
gotten, his Ex. said he was pleased right down to-
ll is boots to know that the hen industry was pro-
sperous. And his Ex. said nothing else worth
mentioning-

THE CHAIN-B ANGER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Speaking at Melbourne Trades Hall, Mr. Tom Mann
said he would resort to either Freetrade or Protection, or
both at once, in order to reach the goal of Labor. — Daily
Paper.
“ I’m anti-this, I’m anti-that ; but, all the same, I think
The things I curse are excellent politic food and drink.
Protection is a deadly thing—l advocate its use ;
Preetrade’s a splendid pestilence—l think I’ll let it loose.
I haven’t any policy that’s really definite—
I say that wrong’s the same as right, and argue black is
white.
I haven’t time to sort myself,” the prancing seer explains :
“ I’m far too busy whirling round and banging useless
chains.”

burning sensation and feeling as if a weight was
pressing upon your brain, is generally caused by

filter. This tends to increase the temperature of
the blood, which, carried to the brain, has a

harder and faster, and every throb is like a sledge-
hammer, and your head is ready to burst. You
