SPORTING NOTIONS-
dea . d s P ort > there is an enormous
4V.K U ? lnfc erest taken in the corpse. From Ryde to
SatS?, Vd . the stiQres of the Parramatta Ri/e? on
Saturday, when Stanbury rowed Towns for the champion-
• ''Ti re . ' lr ! ed „ with , an excited multitude. At
Gladesville the land was black with people, and the river
was decked over with boats. The big North Shore ferry-
boats took lip huge crowds and heeled over to an amazing
angle, and the people on the lower decks stood up to
their ankles in water while they cheered as the two out-
went.by. Barring the funnels the boats could
not be seen for the folk who clung to the sides and
swarmed over the roofs like monkeys over a cocoa-
nut shed. Far up on the hill hats were waving in
the sunlight, and faint cheers came from as far
away as Goulburn—or thereabouts. The people who
barracked for Towns never lost faith in their champion
“ IU last, and when Stanbury appeared in the
lead they cheered wildly and said it was all right
George was holding him nicely, and would come with a
wonder-burst of speed in the last half-mile. “Good old
George ! ” they yelled affectionately to the man who
carried their money. Lower down they were a little
anxious. They hoped George wouldn’t let him go too
* ar "'as dangerous to hold any man too cheap ; and
they stopped talking to shout “Shake it up, George!”
Another hundred,yards and they were desperate. They
hurled frantic requests to “George” not to “ chuck the
blooming race away ” through foolishness, but to shake it
up and get in front. When, with a tremendous effort,
Towns did commence to reduce the distance, they cheered
wildly: “ He’s coming ! he’s coming ! My gawd, he’s
cornin’ But the big fellow bent down and lifted his
shell through the water, and Towns’ effort was hopeless.
They shot past the judge’s boat what seemed to this
writer nearly four lengths apart. Then the men who had
howled for Towns stood silent, their hands stole up to
where they carried Towns’ colors, and as the defeated
champion rented his head on his arms his barrackers were
silently dropping his symbol overboard. Five minutes
later, writer couldn’t see a bit of Towns’ ribbon in the
multitude, while the red-and-white was everywhere.
It certainly seemed that Towns was not in
perfect condition, or lacked strength and driving power
compared to Stanbury. If there is anything in the
generally accepted time it was a fast race ; but at no part
of the contest did Stanbury seem to be pushed, and it
never struck this writer that they were making speed.
The fact that Stanbury went to the front early and stayed
there the whole way was probably what robbed the race
of spectacular excitement. It was a well-rowed contest,
but it did not strike one as a struggle between giants.
There is no doubt that Towns held his opponent too
cheaply. Stanbury settled down to nine months’ hard
work and heavy training, and Towns went on with his
usual avocation, and indulged practically in only a few
weeks’ preparation. The man who did the hardest work
won the laurels, but, owing to Towns’ keen business
arrangement, the defeated man takes the lion’s share of
the proceeds. Stanbury will have to drive the same sort
of bargain with the next man who rows him, in order to
make money out of the business—although, with such
enormous crowds as on Saturday, even the jackal’s share
of the loot is not despicable.
It has been written that Towns never had a
hope after they passed Uhr’s Point. This is not correct.
Towns came very near to winning the race right on the
post, as it were, and the championship of the world was
nearly decided by the evil influences of Drink. As Stan-
bury almost lifted his shell out of the water when he saw
Towns making his final spurt, the nose of his boat
crashed into one dead marine and his scull struck another.
From a distance it must have looked as though he had
caught a crab. He lost his stroke, he rocked, slowed
down, and it appeared almost certain that he would go
right over. Then he recovered himself, and only a
champion could have got going again the way he did.
* * * *
Michael Rush, who umpired Saturday’s race,
did some fine sculling work a quarter of a century ago.
In 1877 he raced Trickett on the Parramatta for the
championship of Australia. It was in that race that the
sliding-seat was first used in Australian waters. Ned
Trickett had brought the idea from England, and turned
it to use in his contest with Rush. As he won the race,
sliding-seats became all the go, though they were rude
things compared with the seats of to-day.
Hr -k * *
Prices charged by the Bronzewing, Cobar and
Kirribilli to see the Towns-Stanbury procession, £1 and
10s. per head. Good old Private Enterprise ! Only a few
weeks ago one of the big ferry shareholders stated, with
tears in his eyes, that the company had the public’s wel-
fare at heart. How much does the hard-up N.S.W.
Government get for the use of its river ?
* ♦ * *
The value of a name ! A Bulletin man
went up the Parramatta last week to have a glimpse
of Towns and Stanbury training. The steamer was
crowded with enthusiastic oarsmen and humbugs.
At Glladesville two scullers shot out from behind the
wharf and started down the stream together. Loud
cheers went up from the crowded decks, and enthusiasts
stood on the railings and whooped and waved wildly.
“ Good old Jim ! ” said one in the affectionate tone of a
long-lost brother, glancing towards where the bigger man
of the two had shot across to the other side of
the river. “It ain’t Jim, you fool —it’s Towns ! ” snorted
a man who held a cigarette in one hand and waved his hat
with the other. “It is Jim ; I know him by his long, sweep-
ing stroke” (fiercely). “Nothing in the world like it—
lovely stroke,” he went on, reflectively. “ I tell you it
ain’t Jim Stanbury, an’ I don’t believe it’s Towns either ;
but, anyhow, it ain’t Jim,” snarled the other. “ Oh, go
and talk to the children. I oughter know Jim. Why, I’d
know his stroke anywhere—’mong a million on ’em. Why
—Hell!—no, it ain’t Jim ! ” he gasped suddenly as the
tall sculler caught a basket of crayfish and went right
over. Five minutes later the real champions came along
and diverted attention from the man who could pick out
Stanbury’s stroke among a million.
* * * *
The defeat of the Otago University team by
that of Sydney Alma Mater was a very unexpected
development. The Otago men were not prepared for such
excellent line kicking and sustained passing, and the
prompt way the Sydney pack got down on their attempts
to pass prevented the Otago machine getting really to
work. The result was that a strenuous first half ended
with Sydney Uni. nine points to Otago’s five. In the
second half the Otago boys showed some beautiful
forcing, but compensated for it by dribbling in a
bewildering manner. The game grew faster every few
minutes of the second half, and the Sydney crowd showed
an unexpected combination among the backs. But the
M. youths got their backs going also, and Otago were
scoring fast when time ran out, and the game ended with
Sydney Uni. leading by two points—l 4 to 12. The
Maorilanders started for home that very night. Their
invasion had been a failure, the match against Sydney
Uni. the previous Wednesday resulting in victory for the
Cornstalks by 16 to 13. Altogether the Kangaroo is •
feeling pretty well satisfied.
* * * *
The Maorilander Soccer Jplayers left Sydney for
home in company with the defeated ftugbyites from
Otago University' on Saturday night. The team had
played altogether 11 matches, won 6, lost 3, and drawn
2. Of the three inter-State matches, Maoriland and
N. each won one, and the odd one (Saturday’s) was a
draw—one goal each, secured by desperate fighting,
which shows that the teams were remarkably
well matched. The general result has been to
largely increase the interest taken in the British Asso-
ciation game, which is the prettiest of all forms of football
to watch. What makes it doubly so here is that the
Soccer men have a higher standard of play than any other
footballers in Sydney at the present time, and the man
who goes out to Epping can always be certain that he is
going to see two teams play the game as it ought to be
,
On Saturday the Rugby premiership resumed
its interrupted course in Sydney. The exciting game of
the day was that between Newtown and Sydney at the
Agricultural Ground. Sydney, having won the toss, took
the aid of the wind ; nevertheless the Newtowners made
things very lively at the start and kept the leather in
Sydney’s backyard for quite a time. Then the City pulled
itself together and dribbled right over the Newtown line.
Scoring was prevented by a prompt and skilful force. But
the kick-off found Newtown in difficulties again. Half
time found Sydney points to Newtown’s nil. The
second spell was fast from the jump. Newtown converted
a couple of tries, and its barrackers made strange dis-
arrangements of sounds in their glee. A passing charge
saw Newtown score again, but the shot at goal missed.
Sydney suddenly attacked, and with a splendid passing
charge scored another try. Dead silence while Warton
aimed at the goal, and lost it, and Newtown was still
ahead by one point. Just on the tick of time the suburb
scored again and kicked the goal just as the whistle blew.
Newtown won by 18 to 12, yet it was anybody’s game
right up to the last four minutes.
* * * ■*
With the exception of the Newtown-Sydney
match, Saturday’s Rugby was of the dreary sort. The
result of the shuffle was to keep Glebe at the head of the
list with 16 points, South Sydney second with 15 points,
and Newtown and Western Suburbs level with 14 points
each. University, last year’s premiers, are fourth on the
list with 13 points. South Sydney failed to show anything
like its recent form in the drawn match with Western
Suburbs, and the leather was badly mishandled by both
crow 7 ds. It is not often Sydney sees two draw's on one
afternoon, but Balmain and Eastern Suburbs made the
second one with six points each. Glebe downed North
Sydney by 17 to 8, but it was a tired sort of a match.
Apparently that drawn game against N.S.W.,
or something, has alarmed Maoriland Rugby Union, and
an additional pair of players will be added to the 25 ori-
ginally chosen for the descent on the Motherland.
Whether the addition will make for increased efficiency is
doubtful, but there is no show of suspicion that it will
not increase the bill considerably. But Maoriland takes
its football very seriously, and it is better that the State
should have to be appealed to than the success of the tour
be endangered in any way. Exactly what will happen if
that crowd of footballers gets dished by the Englishmen
is just too horrible to think of. Chaka, the Zulu Napo-
leon, used to butcher troops that returned after a defeat.
Maoriland will probably drop its team into a geyser.
Those minions of the High Grods who irrigate
seem to envy the joyous crowds that watch Victorian
football. Last Saturday, after seducing thousands to roll
up to the different games by fine, bracing weather, they
opened the overflow pipes at half-time, and thence-
forward damp, disgusted, and muddy spectators stolidly
watched two score moist and dishevelled players slipping
all ways and trying to grab a greasy ball to a general
chorus of “ Damn.” The once all-conquering Fitzroy
continued its dowmvard course, and has now three
defeats to its discredit—Carlton accounting for the
latest by 41 points to 39. Somehow ’Roy’s defeats seem
to be a cause of general mirth, its seven victories right off
the reel having caused the team to develop that swelled
head that goeth before a precipice. Colling wood
still piles up the interest on its mortgage on the
premiership, putting down Melbourne by 74 to 41. Closest
game of the day was between “Sowth”and Essendon,
a rough, unmannerly scramble from start to finish, leaving
“ Skinner’s hirelings,” as the South team is called, victors
by 1 point only—7 goals 8 beliinds to 6 goals 13 behinds.
It was a great scrap, and, in spite of rain and slush, spec-
tators were sorry when the bell rang—all but a few over-
hasty barrackers who had acquired black eyes in the
course of ill-advised arguments. In the Association,
West Melbourne, Williamstown and North still lead, equal;
while Richmond and Ports have a chance— viz., Buckley’s.
A pleasing innovation at some of the Association matches
was the institution, as a sort of appetiser, of a game
between State School teams, in place of the weariful,
incomprehensible baseball match. As a rule, the little
nippers play a fair, manly game.
* * » *
Under Australian rules the Y.M.C.A. no longer
leads the van, Sydney having won to that proud place
with 20 competition points on its banner. Newtown and
Y.M.C.A. march side by side behind with 18 to their
credit. Redfern, Balmain, and Paddington are just
visible through the dust on the horizon with 12 each, and
North Sydney and East Sydney are invisible, owing to
the rotundity of the earth, with 10 each. Sydney downed
Redfern by" 53 to 28, and Paddington piled up 46
points to East Sydney’s 28. Newtown’s defeat of
North Shore was very crushing—lls to 50 points.
But the surprise of the day was when Balmain, after
a gruelling game, beat the redoubtable Y.M.C.A. by six
points. The scoring was, Balmain 6 goals 11 behinds,
Y.M.C.A. 6 goals 5 behinds—47 to 41 points. It was
a very fast game, and had the secretary only got on
top of an adjacent roof and held up his hands like
Joshua used to, it might have turned the balance in
favor of the Good Young Men.
* * * *
“Yes,” said the Maorilander as he stepped on
board his red-funnelled steamer bound for his volcano, “your
beastly footballers are going to swoop down on us while
the flower of our crowd is battling in dear old Hingland.
I always was against this policy of foreign aggression ! But
you won’t have any walk over, my word ! ”
* * * *
At an Adelaide football match, the other day,
an aged barracker gave one last, excited yell and quietly
expired. There was no doctor handy, so one of the
players (a medical student) was called off the field. After
performing the unpleasant duty of pronouncing life ex-
tinct he returned to his occupation of goal-kicking.
• * * *
W. J. Bancroft, the great Welsh full-back,
retiring from Rugby football at the age of 30, has re-
ceived a £322 purse from his admirers. He has played in
international matches 33 times, 12 of them as captain.
Statisticians say that he has dropped 45 goals during his
career, kicked 29 from penalty kicks, and “ converted ”
374 tries ; also, in spite of his position in the field, he has
put in 31 tries on his own account.
* « •* *
A good many Australians will say “ditto” to
the remarks of a cockney cricket reporter (London
Referee) on the fast bowling in the first Test. “ I hope
we shall see no more of the monstrous style of bowling
adopted by Cotter on Monday, and rather feebly imitated
later in the day by Jessop. To deliberately pitch half-
way, with no other object than to frighten the batsmen
by making the ball go over their heads, is emphatically
not cricket.”
/ » » *
The idea of throwing out the amateur athletes
who act as journalists is receiving attention on the part
of the English sporting bodies, and it is proposed that all
such scribblers should be branded “ professional.” The
section of the press that depends upon this curious hybrid
for its reports of tours and tests is kicking wildly. The
proposal will make no difference to the Australian
cricketers, they being already regarded as professionals.
The Australian doesn’t care what you call him so long as
the gate receipts are large.
■* . * * *
In the test matches Jackson has won all four
tosses —thus giving to England each time the advantage
of batting first on the good wickets.
* * * *
N.S.W. Cricket Association has altered the
mode of allotting Sydney first-grade competition points
for the coming season, in future 2 points will count for
a first innings win, 3 for a win outright ; 2 points de-
ducted for a first innings loss, 3 for a loss outright ; tie to
count 1 point each. The idea is to encourage fast cricket,
and do away with the dreariness which hangs to compe-
tition matches.
JAMES STANBURY,
Champion Sculler of the World.
It was the reputation of the N.S.W. Minis-
try as a crowd which can be bustled that brought
the recalcitrant Sydney bookmakers down on the
bendable portion of their front pants. Ever since
the strike of Messrs. Ikev, Pearlstein and Goldbug there
have been strange rumblings, which indicated that a
tremendous amount of underground engineering was going
on. Then a whisper completed the debacle, for someone
breathed into the ear of Israel that if the bookmakers did
not register promptly with the A.J.O. at the in-
creased rates a Bill to introduce the Totalisator
would be introduced and rushed through the
Assembly in one nig'ht, and that its passage through the
Council was greased and ready. The House of Noses im-
mediately decided that it would not fight the A.J.O.
“ just now,” and it proposed that a conference of book-
makers and suburban racing club's should fix the fee for
suburban courses. The bookmaker’s cup of affliction is
not only full, but flowing over, and, like the widow’s
cruse, there seems to be no end of it. On Monday last,
when he resumed operations and loudly intimated that he
was quite ready for any flies that wanted web accommoda-
tion, the flies stayed away very badly, and refused most
tempting doubles. On top of all, came the crushing
announcement that the V.R.C. had decided to raise the
paddock-fee at Flemington from £25 to £lOO. Evidently
the winter is only just beginning 1
The Caulfield Cup handicapper has made
generous allowance for the fact that Oxenham’s mare,
Acrasia, is not as young as she was. The mare ran second
to Murmur last year with 7.7 on her back, and this time
she is asked to carry only 71b. more, whereas she has been
raised 131 b. for her victory in the Melbourne Cup. On
paper Acrasia has a taking chance for either Cup—
especially the Caulfield—although she is now in her
eighth year.
* * * *
Quo Yadis, still a young horse, as jumpers go,
is kindly treated with 10.5 in the Caulfield G.N. Hurdle.
He went amiss on the'morning of the V.R.C. event, and
was scratched, but his subsequent track work suggests
that he will be all right at Caulfield.
t * « *
The prospect that betting on racecourses will be
absolutely suppressed in France by September Ist is
nothing for Australian anti-gambling brigades to go
hilarious over. It simply means that the Pari Mutuel
will take the place of the person with the raucous voice,
the pencil and the bag. The bookmaker on Australian
courses would have been a very small creature long' ago
had not the local anti-gambling crowd fought so viciously
against legalising the totalisator.

Ikey Mo. is at his old games. Published in
several Sydney papers during past week that last Caul-
field Cup winner, Murmur, was backed for this year’s
Caulfield Cup (three months ahead) to win £lO,OOO. The
bait won’t tempt shrewd people, who are not likely to
forget that Murmur went wrong soon after his Caulfield
Cup triumph, and was put out of work for six months.
* * * *
First May (verging on eight years old), who won
big event at Canterbury Park, last Saturday, has been a
slave ever since she was able to race at all, and thrives on
it. Since her first appearance (as a three-year-old), she,
up to the beginning of this season, started 120 times for
23 wins, and is shaping better now than ever.

* * * *
O. A. Jeffries writes : Since the publication of
my true, faithful, entirely unexaggerated account of how
my bike bolted down the Horse Range, outside Palmerston
South, Maoriland, I have received several letters which
insinuate in a courteous manner that, had I not been
something' between an idiot and a horse-thief, I would
have avoided all those thrills by sticking my toe into the
front forks over the top of the wheel and stopping the
machine. Well, first of all, I didn’t want to stop—l
wanted the copy. Secondly, if I had, I couldn’t have
done so, for that runaway took place in the days of solid
tyres, and my bike, being a semi-racer, had so narrow a
rim that no ordinary toe—although mine is a narrow,
aristocratic toe —could be inserted between the forks.
Moreover, the machine was a fancy model, and the fork
was out of reach of my toe if I could have got it in, owing
to the shape of the body of the machine. Further, had I
got it in, in spite of all these impossibilities, the hard
rubber tyre and bevelled steel rim would have taken my
toe off ; I tried to rest the soles of my shoes against the
wheel below the fork, and nearly lost the thumbs off both
feet by cutting and burning. I have ridden almost every
model of bicycle that has been made, and I have always
found that a band brake, if properly screwed up and
adjusted, will hold any bike on any slope. But once it
gives way, “ it’s all out.” Mine gave way, and I’m still
here, owing to having banged Macpherson. There was no
necessity to cart brushwood and a length of cord out to the
Horse Range when I had Macpherson there to turn me
round the corner.

The Dunlop Tyre Co. has fixed September 16
for the Goulburn-Sydney bicycle road race. This will
give the successful competitors about two weeks to recu-
perate before taking part in the great Warrnambool-
Melbourne race. The Sydney cycling trade has shelled
out handsomely, and the total bag'will be worth £125.
The distance is 131 miles, and the riders are to be limited
to one bicycle, which will be sealed at Goulburn before
starting, so that there will be no chance of any dishonest
rider changing his jigger en route. Of course, no one
would suspect a professional cyclist of doing such a
dreadful thing, but just to relieve any weakerjessel from
temptation the big company reckons « » ' by

Once more Maoriland has got the
fever and has got it as badly as it once had the war
fever.’ Nothing is talked of by the scorching push hut
test races. On the first Saturday in August no less than
six road races are taking place in Maoriland, stretching
away from the Three Kings to the Bluff-Woodville to
Napier, 100 miles; Paikakanki to Wellington, S- 'll-
- to Invercargill, 69 miles ; Milton to Dunedm,
60 miles ; Cambridge to Auckland, SO miles , Bnghtwater
to Nelson, 67 miles. These are all to select »ders t
compete in the Timaru to Christchurch road-race the
event that has unearthed all the road champions that
have so stood on the tail of the kangaroo recent
years. In fact, Maorilanders are beginning to say mat
after they win the Warrnambool-Melbourne race, e vent

and 50 mile road championships, is a highway flier of more

The inter-club cycling championship contest
was suspended on Saturday so that the wheelers might
help to swell the proceeds of the Stanbury-Towns sculling
match. The excitement will re-commence next Saturday,
when some close finishes are expected ; and at present
the possession of the Dunlop Shield is a matter of much
doubt and considerable wagering.
* * * *
I cannot understand how it is that
boomerang-throwing is not adopted as a branch of athle-
tics. Its novelty and thoroughly Australian charactei
would soon establish its popularity. If the game were
taken up by some of the large schools, interest would
soon be aroused. Points could be given for the closest
return, the longest flight, the best curves, and so on.
Watching the flight of a skilfully-thrown boomerang has
a great fascination—first the long, straight line within a
few feet of the earth ; then a gradually-rising curve until
it reaches its apex as it were, a few whirls of hesitancy,
then a long, hawk-like sweep, the weapon descending
slowly close to the ground, again rising slightly, and at
last falling almost at the starting-point.
* * * *
This coming Saturday (31st) Victoria and
Westralia will both have lacrosse teams in Adelaide.
S.A., which booms the Canadian game to much purpose,
has picked two separate teams to take them on. In a
club match lately one side threw 26 goals past about the
best keeper in the city ; and a State team once put up
the same score against N.S.W.
• * * *
The counter attraction of the Stanbury-Towns
contest on Saturday proved fatal to the day’s lacrosse, the
only two first grade teams to turn out in full strength
being those of Marrickville and North Sydney at the
Northern Oval. The North Sydney girls were there in
hundreds to barrack for their team, and excitement ran
high. It was a fast, strenuous game, but the keenness of
the rivalry was responsible for undue roughness on the
part of the Northmen, who began to get the worst of it early
in the struggle. The Marrickvillers, however, could not
press home their advantage, and a promising quarter
ended with only one miserable goal scored by the team
that had all the best of the play. It was enough to make
the angels weep. In the second quarter the Northmen
scored a couple very rapidly, and Marrickville could only
respond with a solitary, and the quarter found the North-
men leading by one. After that play became desultory,
each side adding a goal, and the end found North Sydney
winners with four to three.
* * » *
An international draughts tournament, 10 a
side, has been played between Great Britain and the U.S.
at Boston. The visiting team won 74 games, America
34, and 284 were drawn. That is the worst of draughts ;
it is about 3 to 1 against a game coming to any definite
end at all. One of the U.S. side retired from the contest
with “nervous breakdown!” It is lamentable to
go and wreck your nervous system at draughts.

Champion sprint swimmer Healy. a day or two
since, for the entertainment of Otago ’Varsity foot-
ballers, accomplished a new world’s 60yd. best by
traversing the distance in 32.2-ssec. (2 4-ssee. better than
the figures assailed). Australian top-notch swimmers are
making a business of record-breaking.
A correspondent writes to the S.M.
Herald about the bitter grievance of Fat. It
seems that, if a certain Bill passes, the N.S.W.
landowner, unless he gives orders to the contrary
(and he often may not know that he can do so), is
liable to have the statement of his stock, &c.
which he sent in for the census, used to check his
land and income-tax return. As the census
doesn’t ask him for any money, he often supplies
it with a true return, and the outrage of having
this used to bowl out the false return sent in for
tax purposes strikes Property as monstrous. So
the Legislative Council is urged to keep an eye on
the Bill, “which is of a most insidious and
dangerous character,” and calculated to prevent
Fat defrauding the revenue.


Why the

is the Best in the World,
Good tobacco can only be grown on virgin soil. This is the reason that most Havana
and Mexican cigars are now so poor in quality and lacking in aroma.
Artificial manures have been used to force the growth of these tobaccos, and this
entirely spoils the quality.
The tobacco from which the “MARSUMA ” cigar is made is grown on virgin soil—
that is, land which has been covered for all known time with rank, wild undergrowth.
This is burnt, the land tilled, and tobacco seeds sown, and only in this manner can good
tobacco be obtained. Every few years' the ground is allowed to return to this wild state,
but in Cuba and Mexico this is not done, and consequently these tobaccos have
deteriorated year by year, and must now give place to EAST Indies tobacco, the finest
the world produces.
Quite different from any cigar offered before, the peculiarity of the East Indian
tobacco is that the dark leaves are equally mild as the light-colored ones.
Only ripe tobacco being used in the manufacture, “MARSUMA” cigars are always
a rich brown or dark brown color.
Being made from picked sweet-scented leaves of the young tobacco plant, the cigars
have a most fragrant, pleasing flavor, peculiarly their own.

The ever useful cat made a startling
appearance in a Sydney police court the other
day, when a lady explained that pussy must have
upset some poison into somebody’s tea.
Apparently the whole of Victoria’s
McOutclieon clan runs to Toryism and financial
incapacity. The one in the Vic. Assembly is a
choice specimen thereof, and lately another moved
The Late DR. KEVIN
IZOD O'DOHERTY ,
of Queensland.
and persuaded Mans-
field Shire Council
to agree that “ each
council shall use its

rowed and boomed, and blew’ huge bubbles, and
threw money round with a shovel, and had no
thought beyond faking the balance-sheet and buy-
ing votes with cockspur railways, is still the ideal
of Vic. Toryism.
The latest complaint of the anti-Federal
party is that the Commonwealth law shows a
narrow and grudging spirit in prohibiting mem-
bers of State Legislatures standing for the
Federal Parliament. The wild humor of the
situation is that the N.S.W. State Legislature
first prohibited Federal members being candi-
dates for the N.S.W. Assembly; then S.A., Tas-
mania and Westralia followed suit; and two years
later the Federal Legislature, irritated by these
mean little pin-pricks, decided that the gander
could do with the same sauce as the goose, and
disqualified State members in its turn. Now cer-
tain politicians of the State which first invented
the mean little disqualification complain that the
Australian Parliament shouldn’t do such things.
The question of a continuation of the
present almost useless Bowen (Q.) railway, which
ends at a hole with some eels in it, to its proper point
(a junction with the Townsville-Richmond line at
Charters Towers), comes up yearly for discussion
in Queensland. Premier Morgan has just dropped
a wet blanket on the latest big deputation.
Queensland is suffering a horrible recovery from
the loan drunks of the eighties. Railways were
splashed around then as a swaggie splashes his
cheque about in the Lambihg-down Hotel, and how
poor swaggie has a brown taste in his mouth as
his reward, and the unfinished Bowen line is part
of the brown flavor. All the same, it is up to
Morgan and Kidston to hustle at the earliest
moment for some more revenue —not loans—and
complete the Bowen line. Queensland can’t live
on repentance and wet blankets.

AT POVERTY POINT.
In place of the intimation on some Australian theatre
programmes that attendants are forbidden to accept tips,
many English programmes inform the public that
“Gratuities are allowed.” Probably the next move will
be an effort to save performers’ salaries by sending Hamlet
around with the hat after he has seen his parent’s ghost.
As backwash to London Drury Lane boom advt. that it
was giving actor George Alexander £250 per week for
appearing in Hall Caine’s “ Prodigal Son,” somebody
rises up to state that an American chorus girl is being
offered £4OO a week to appear on the boards again. Her
special claim to fame and big screw is that she has been
tried for murder and acquitted.
Commodore, best-known and most-beloved pony in
Australia, died of old age the other week. Commodore
was the mainstay of Fitzgerald Bros.’ Circus in their
struggling days, and according to a Bulletin yarn of long
ago the tricky piebald pony constituted the whole of the
show at one time when the Brothers were down on their
young luck. But for Commodore they might have sold
their little tent and gone into a less breezy line of busi-
ness, where their front names would have lengthened into
Daniel and Thomas, and dull respectability would have
claimed them for its own. Endeared to the" bosses by old
associations, Commodore presumed somewhat on their
favor as time went on. He grew very uncertain in his
temper—in the ring and out of it. As the Circus ex-
panded and became associated with elephants and car-
nivorous beasts, the original star performer seemed t >
feel his loss of importance. It wasn’t safe to stand near
Commodore’s hind legs when a lion roared. He kicked at
the sound. A true piebald Australian, racy of the soil, he
objected to those imported aliens, anyhow. But now he
is dead to this world. Never again will he gammon to go
lame in front at the request of a vociferous clown who
earries a balloon concealed about his chest. Probably the
grand old pony is born again, and
The angel children, flitting round
A circus on the Golden Shore—
Bright, smiling nippers, halo-crowned—
Applaud the tricks of Commodore.
“ Ned ” Holloway is 83 years of age, and still acting.
He has been on the stage over half-a-century—- for 20
years with Alfred Dampier. Thirty years ago, when Mrs.
Holloway was alive, it was Ned’s ambition, after they
had put £lOOO together, to buy a farm on the Ha.vkes-
bury and settle down ; but Mrs. Holloway died —she w’as
Ned’s right hand—and the farm is forgotten, and the old
man is still acting.
“ Melb.” : What has become of the droves of girls who
used to flock after Nance O’Neil five years ago? Echo
answers. They came thick as the leaves in Vailombrosa,
and after the play they streamed down Bourke-street l.ke
a thousand joyful ewes in a Riverina freshet. Some may
be married, and have an angel or two. Some may have
gone to China as missionaries. Some may have salt-
cellars in their necks. Some may have found out they
are thirty. Some may have gone up. Some—but every
reader can continue this sort of thing indefinitely.
“ G. Elburg” : Went to see that sugared bon-bon, “Mon*
sieur Beaucaire,” for the first time. The playlet is mainly
a matter of clothes and mild stoush, with Julius of the
legs underneath the apparel but on top of the stoush all
through. How the girls do love J. Knight 1 A row of
sweet young things behind me unanimously gasped “ Oh 1
isn’t he 1-o-vely ? ” every time Beaucaire appeared in a
new suit. And as Julius kept changing his clothes, the
girleens continued gasping until I caught a cold through
them breathing hard on my head. Maud Jeffries has
developed an irritating habit of mouthing her words, with
a weird rise on the last notes.
A recent revival of “ The Cabinet Minister ” suggests
that Pinero had a prophetic vision of Premier Balfour
when he wrote the farcical comedy some fifteen years ago.
The Cabinet Minister complains that the House of
Commons torments him sorely, the newspapers are mostly
unsympathetic, and he has to fly for consolation to the
flute. Balfour is suspected of a weakness for the zither.
Lionel Brough (uncle of Australia’s one and only Robert)
on June 15th celebrated his 50th year on the stage with
about the biggest benefit programme on record ; it is diffi-
cult to name anyone among London’s leading actors who
was not in it. Estimated result to L. 8., £lOOO. Bio-
graphers recall that half-a-century ago there were so
many Broughs on the stage that the new-comer was
ordered to call himself “ Lionel Porter,” to avoid confu-
sion.
“ O’Fipp ” : They are strong on local color at Melbourne
Opera House. A fashionably-dressed vocalist, after sing-
ing “ The Miner’s Dream of Home,” confides this to the
audience : “ Well, I’ve struck it rich, and now I’m about
to take the train to Fremantle, on my way to the dear old
land I left ten year ago.” The W. A. goldfields from which
he is departing, are represented by several “coo-ees,”a
Bret-Harteish landscape of pines, a log hut, and a low
comedian, who seems to have been used to gathering
nuggets in Whitechapel.
“ O’Fipp ” : Various Argus references to the brilliancy
of W. M. Akhurst’s “historical burlesques,” produced at
Melbourne Theatre Royal in the ’6o’s, led me to struggle
through the book of “ The Battle of Hastings.” Its beau-
ties consist mainly of italicised puns. Here are a few
samples
“ And for this son and heir I’ve set a s-n-n-are.”
“ Your Highness. Yes, your eye on his crown you’ve
got.”
“ Although you play first fiddle in the State,
I tell you, King, you are a vile ingrate.’’
Nearly every line contains puns, many of them worse than
those quoted, and yet an Argus writer says Akhurst was
“ always good,” and wonders that none of his burlesques
have been revived during the last quarter of a century.
Perhaps Akhurst’s early connection with the Argus as
musical critic blinds it to the mediocrity of his stuff.
Some recent musical comedies have been poor enough,
but almost anything would be preferable to a resurrection
of the Akhurst tripe.

Australia’s Two Favourites.
MISS MAY BEATTY '
AND...
Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy.
the Tomer Praises the Latter.
VERYONE in Australasia knows Miss May Beatty and admires her clever acting.
Her admirers cannot help laughing at her humour, nor can they fail to be im-
pressed when she gives them sober advice. Her many friends will be pleased to read
an extract from a recent letter:—
f e Last Thursday I was again suffering so muoh with
Bronchitis that I thought it would be impossible for me
to appear that evening. A friend in the Hotel* seeing my
® condition, suggested that I try Chamberlain's Cough Remedy. As you know, I have
suffered very much this winter with my throat, and really think I have tried every
line of Patent Medicine advertised for that complaint, but none of them gave me relief.
Thinking it no harm to try another advertised remedy, and especially when it
was so highly recommended by Mrs. A—, I secured a bottle of Chamberlain's Cough
Remedy and commenced taking it at once. To my surprise and great relief, by Six
o'clock I was feeling much better; my throat was not so inflamed and I had a less
desire to cough so often.
I was able to go to the theatre and went through my part without very much
trouble. I continued taking Chamberlain's Cough Remedy for a couple of days and by that
time was completely cured, and have not had the slightest trouble with my throat since.
Hereafter I will always see that I have a bottle of Chamberlain's Cough Remedy
with me, for one cannot tell when same may be needed.''
Melbourne, July 3rd, 1905.
“ Priam,” on the Yic. State Ministry :
Bent is a statesman obscured by grossness, ignorance,
vanity, and fat.
Gillott is a landlord, and that ends him. His talk
sounds like a bleat; but it is a mistake to suppose him a
sheep. You need only live in one of his houses to find
that out.
Swinburne is an amiable man, with some commercial
training. He has tried to understand the bastard politi-
cal creed to which he was supposed to be attached, but
he has tried in vain. So he frames his'legislation methodi-
cally, and being simple and without prejudice, he gives
surprising satisfaction.
Murray is a burly fellow, who aspires to achieve dis-
tinction without effort. Whether as a Radical or a Tory,
he has always followed the line of least resistance. He
bungles when he schemes, but he has gone farther than
others who schemed more cleverly.
Sachse has a curly moustache and a fluty ■v oice. He has
learned a whole set of platitudes, pumped into him by
Tate, the tireless Director of Education. With either
brains or experience, Sachse might become a drag on the
Educational wheel.
Mackey, though an hon, Minister, is often alleged to be
the brains of the Cabinet. He has a well-stored mind,
like the library of a man who gloats over the covers of
his books. He assimilates new facts slowly and thor-
oughly, and doubtless finds them improving to himself.
His mind bears no external fruits.
Davies is a respectable solicitor, who is said to be just,
impartial, and honorable—except by those who speak
differently.
Pitt, Langdon, M‘Leod, and Cameron are men with
whiskers arranged in different styles.

Parson Mason, of Otahuhu (Auckland), is
Maoriland’s champion water-finder with the bent
twig. Still, as a six-year-old kiddy will drown,
except on the hills, anywhere if he is allowed to
sink a hole in Pig Island soil for half an hour, there
is very little demand for the aquatic twigger in
Maoriland. Yet there have been mighty twig-gists
in that wet laud. One Charlie Stewart, from
S.A., was in some request as a damp-locater in
earlier days. Charles once got to work for a West
Coast publican, who only wanted to see how his
magic would answer. The water-finder solemnly
followed his twig round a eoujjle of paddocks, and
then back to the pub. again, where, despite his
frantic struggles, it dragged him through the bar-
door and in behind the counter, and pointed
fixedly to the whisky cask. The disturbance that
followed was memorable, and the twig was trodden
to death before the trouble blew over.
“ JS".”: The autobiography of Chief
Inspector Cavanagh, of Scotland Yard, has a goou
deal about E. T. Smith, Dr. L. L. Smith’s father.
He was an ex-superintendent of police, sen of an
admiral, and first came into notice keeping the
Red Lion Inn, “where he used to have some
peculiar attractions on hand. One time it was the
living skeleton, at another the fat woman. Then
he bloomed into a successful lessee of Drury
Lane, etc. Cavanagh joined the force as a
policeman He had to wear a glazy bell-topper,
and swallow-tail coat, with a belt, having an
odiem, big buckle in front. On his first beat, by
night, he had a burly bobby, Tom Tully, doing the
other side of the street. Says Tom, “ Any
roosters ? ” This meant swells who gave the
police tips out of pure good nature. Then says
Tom, “There are twelve dabs on my side of the
street, and eight on yours.’’ These meant free
drinks available every night. The policeman’s
life was a happy one then despite the glazed hat.
Jb'rom far-off Valparaiso :
Struck Tun Bulletin at Conception, in the south of
Chili, and glad to find you still battling- against humbug
and snuffle.
Looking from a distance, it s:ems to me that Australia
has got too many rules and regulations screwed on to its
barren surface. Over in Argentina most everybody sells
liquor seven days a week and 24 hours a day, and no one
gets drunk. If you do spot a drunk, you can bet a shm-
plaster he’s a boy of the bulldog breed. Believe if you got
rid of all the red-tape licensing laws of Australia you
would have less harmful boozing-.
In Argentina and in Chile I rarely see imported brands.
Practically none but local makes are used. The beer is
as like Melbourne lager as may be, and' the wines could
be labelled Australian and no one know the difference.
British people here drink these local productions with
gusto—the same people who would sniff at Australian
stuff. Why can’t you knock good sense into that Federa
tariff of yours, and force Australian makes into fashion ?
The Englishmen here treat the semi-yellow people of
these republics like dogs—unless the dogs are rich. I find
that English people hate Australians likewise. Believe it
is because we are not sufficiently ’umble.
What a lot of non-patriots Australia sends abroad !
Almost all the Australians I meet in Argentina belong to
what you call the Stinking F.sh party.
Travelled on steamer with a Victorian squattocrat, who
got sick every time rabbits came on the table. He ran
down Australia from Wellington to Cape Horn, but, after
cruising round the Pampas for a fortnight, concluded
that Australia was a safer place for him. Victorian bush-
workers are free with their tongues, but not so ready
with the knife as our gauchos.
Hear much talk to the effect that many Australians are
selling out there to take up land here. Can’t .find them.
All I know come to billets in freezing, or mining, or har-
vesting machinery concerns. Salaries are high here, but
cost of living is quite double that of Melbourne or Sydney,
and social life doesn’t exist outside of Buenos Ayres.
Knowone Australian on theland, “Commotion" Pearson,
of the “ same old ” Essendon. He runs 20 leagues of camp
for an English company, and is quoted all over the Re-
public as a most successful estaneiero. Grows Australian
wheat, and reaps it with Australian strippers, and so on.
Was the first man to introduce the Australian wool-press
into South America.
A. strange result of landlordism is reported
from Bartdykes, Lanarkshire (Scotland). The
ground landlord, presumably a proud and bloated
eipitalist, declined to renew the lease of Messrs.
Merry and Cunninghame, who have been working
the ground for coal these many years. The closing
of the coal-pits means that “a village of work-
men’s houses affording accommodation for 600
miners will be razed to the ground.” What a fir.o
progressive paradise of the working man is
Britain ! But how rich it is in spite of its big-
landlords ! On the same day that the closing of
Merry and Cunninghame’s pits was decreed,
another 1500 acres of good house-coal were practi-
cally proved at the Burton-on-Trent colliery,
which provides the noble Earl of Carnarvon with
visible means of support. The new discovery is
reckoned to represent an addition of 150 years to
the life of the Carnarvon mine, and so “ there is
great rejoicing in the district.” The inhabitants
revel in the thought that their descendants unto
the third and fourth generation will be able to toil
at digging wedth for future earls from the bowels
of their family estate. They don’t want any

Since the Berringa mines (Vic.) have got to
using 23 cubic feet of broken quartz as a standard ton
their statistics of working costs have a value. Whv
managers should have ever penalised themselves bv
reckoning up to 33 cubic feet as a ton cannot be grasped
U « Ie A S lt t s w , a ? a dread that the Public would be frightened
os the field because the grade was low. But the field
now has a reputation quite as much for its low costs as
tor its low grade ore, and still more because some of the
mines, notably Williams’ Fancy and Birthday Tunnel
pay divs. steadily. Last six months’ work at the
.tunnel cost 11s. 3d. per ton, including 2s. lOd. for
(levelop ent and other capital charges. Quartz treated,
11,805 tons ; yield, 2745)0z., or just under sdwt. Value
of gold, £11,536; divs., £3OOO ; cash in hand, £521.
therefore the return to shareholders was over 26 per
cent, of the value of the g< Id won. Not bad at all; but
with a mine with such f r.n itions, what is wanted is to
push ahead with the shaft, gjt a big - reserve of stone, put
on good battery power, opm up 200 ft. lifts, and then
make the mill do a big tonnage. Unfortunately tbe
ruling power on the board is wedded to old ideas and to
paying out profits as soon as earned, so it is not much
Use expecting all this.

Of the old Glenfine South (Bitfield, Vie.)
school, only one remains oh the eo.’s board—the astute
James Rahdell. It seems well that such a change has
been made, when the balance-sheet of to-day is com-
pared with that of the past. A mine that gave £65 000
In divs, ought never to have got so financially short as
aid the Glenfine South; Started as an alluvial mine, it
Struck hen qti&rtas, find produced g"old worth over
£200,000. Last half-year it had to make calls amounting
to £2500 to open up wash once again. W. A. Bryant
brother of the manager of Main Leads North, run's the
show nowadays, Alcock having-gone to the Jubilee, and
Jonah Ward being interested in trying to prop up the
bottom of the Berry Extended. Bryant is modest about
his wash. He tells that two trucks and 12 dishes gave
16)dwt. gold, and is content to .only say that he now
sees that the ground is payable. What occasions pain to
the boys of the new brigade on the board is the unreadi-
ness of the public to invest iri Glenfine South.
No. 2 South Great Eastern, Gympie (Q.), has
had its general meeting. There was no row this time
about the amalgam reserve. Continued absence of speci-
men stone has wiped the reserve out long since. There is
the same need to-day, however, as there was a year ago
for hotter methods fit the 2 S. Great Eastern. But nobody
takes all interest ih reform nowadays. Those who are
left in feel too sick.

One of the Melbourne broking crowd was politely
told “No ” by the Mt. Lyell secretary and clerks when he
wanted a bit of news about the developments at North
Lyell. His. wail thereupon was that co. didn’t send news
to Exchange, to be posted there. But did he intend to
undertake that philanthropic duty, or would he have told
his .priricipals first ? Anyhow, he wasn't given the news,
jtnd there has been d hullabaloo. Yet, for once at least,
the co.’s officials did right. There was not the least call
to give news to a broker, who, in that capacity, wanted it,
when the intelligence was not being handed out to the
shareholders.
* * * *
An East Coast Tasmanian, who says he doesn’t
want to be compelled to quit that pleasant part through
the odor from defunct wild-cats, writes.: —“This place is
at present over-run with ‘ experts,’ who are whipping-up
a tin boom all they know. Speculators should, however,
keep in mind that of the many ‘ dividends-must-soon-
follow ’ claims on this coast that have been floated within
the la.it few years, most have closed down, and others are
hanging on by the skin of their teeth. Among the former
are Cream Creek (twice floated), Star of Peace (ditto),
Liberator, Cambria, &c. Where also are the dividends
from the Australian, the Hunt P.A., Frome River, &c. ?
One can count the div.-payers on the fingers of one hand.
Among others just floated is the Weldbrook, near Weld-
borough. This was floated about five years ago. Co.
kept wages men on for about 15 months; then it
Was worked on tribute ; finally, about a year ago, it
tvas sold by tender and brought £6s—plant, water rights,
laces, everything; Now it has been refloated (after the
last 66: ana tribiitorS and purchasers have picked oilt
Such eygS as it ever, possessed), with a capital of
£21,000, . and more noise is being made over it than
was made around the walls of Jericho. It is reported
in the papers that local residents are falling over
themselves to buy shares. This is quoted as a proof that
the show is held in high estimation by them. Why
local residents did not offer a bigger price than
£65 some 12 months ago for it, is not explained.
At writing, shares are £l5, making the mine nominally
worth £52,500, or over 800 times as much as it was bought
for a little more than a year ago. Preliminary puffs are
beginning to appear about the Laffer —a little one-horse
show, that has been battling along for years, just manag-
ing to pay its way. How it can be turned into a large
yielder is a mystery. Interesting, too, to note the
efforts to focus public attention on the old Sarah
Ann claim, situate at Main Creek, about three
miles from Derby. This has been lying idle for
years. People began to call it the North Briseis, but
evidently came to the conclusion that that horse was
befng ridden to death. It has starved out a number of
parties in its time, but on paper it is still ‘ promising,’
and more. On same cre'-k is the Abbotsford. This was
sold about 18 months ago for £35, and has since been
worked by tributors, who have hardly made tucker since
Xmas. Preliminary puffs are out, which is a certain sig n
that business is meant. It seems not to be generally
understood on the mainland that the Briseis and
Pioneer formations are deep tin drifts, whereas the above
and other shows are on high country, and are but small
creek runs (and to a great extent worked out at that),
and cannot have a 16ng life.”
* * * *
Brisbane’s new paper, the Daily Mail, isn’t in
the profit-paying stage yet. Co.’s second report, just
issued, shows a deficit so far of £12,182—£5000 repre-
sented by a paper item, “copyright and organisation”;
the other £7182 debit on profit and loss account. Co. has
a paid capital of £10,277, and lately allotted £15,000 of
debentures, on which instalments of £7856 have been
paid. Besides the instalments that haven’t matured, co.
has £3336 cash in hand to play with.
Bendigo (Vic.) is almost as treacherous as to its
surface as a sleeping volcano. Perhaps there are not
very many covered up mining shafts around the city—
but no one knows exactly how many there are or where
they are. Recently a blacksmith threw down a heavy
drill in his smithy and was staggered to see a 2ft. hole
gape before him and his drill disappear into the bowels of
the earth. When he recovered, he plumbed the shaft and
found that he had been taking chances for years with a
60ft. cavern beneath his feet.
•» * * *
At South Melbourne the other day, the patentee
of the “A.Z.” Agitator gave an interesting demonstra-
tion of what his invention can do with 30 tons of sand in
a vat containing 35 tons of water. Officially described—
“ The apparatus is made to fit all vats, from 15in. diame-
ter and 6in. deep to 26ft. diameter and Bft. deep. The
only moving part is a propellor of the auger type, which
is fastened to a vertical shaft and fitted to work freely
inside a cylinder open at both ends. The cylinder is
raised, in working, from the bottom of the vat according
to the nature of the stuff requiring agitation. Two
deflecting plates of a special type extend across
the vat (nearly to the bottom of it), and it is the combina-
tion of propeller, cylinder and deflecting- plates which
give the important result achieved. The agitation can
be stopped in a moment, and started in a moment.
There is no time lost in re-starting. The apparatus is also
an aerator, and te ration takes place during the agitation,
which means a saving of cost, the oxyg-en being supplied
free.” Claimed for the “A.Z.” contrivance that it will
effect a great saving- of time for the complete intersection
of the cyanide solution with the tailings. The maximum
result of gold is obtained as quickly as possible. Practical
nf M ,1 be P el ’ suad , e . d that the inventor (A. Z.
Ciarif of Melbourne) has hit the mark, and on the
strength of his circular and photos, of his first apparatus
the h pftent ady reCeiVed an offer for his rights in

South Kalgurli (W.A.) is having a rough time
vyth its new treatment plant. This consists of eight
Merton furnaces, built back to back, and three No. 8
lirupp nulls with usual accessories. Part of plant for-
merly did duty oil Lake View Consols, and lay on scrap
heap for years. As re-erected, it was expected to treat
100 tons daily, at very low cost. Whole system is auto-
matic, but, apparently, something is faulty, as, so far,
only about one-third predicted quantity is being treated.
1 iant may eventually be put to rights, but scrip, in any
case, seems to be over-valued.

Turondale is about all that is left of the Turon
River (N.S. W.) rush of the ’so’s—a straggling village of
about 25 houses, whose people live mainly on the memo-
ries of the times when 5000 whites and a couple of thousand
Chinese made things lively. The mines there and at
Quartz Ridge (six miles out) have been abandoned for
years; but a bit of Victorian capital has lately been
risked, and the old shows are being overhauled again, with
no lively prospects so far.

“ The only Society person luing in the Artists’
Exhibition this year is Mrs. Albert Miller. Iler
strikingly good likeness has been done by Mrs. J.
Barrett* The dame snowed early, and her smooth
white hair is drawn silkily back from a firm and
vigorous face. The Miller clan is large, and it
takes its frilly chums along to view the lady as
she is hung up at the show. The Society girl
doesn’t sit much now for the easel brethren/ She
finds the posing a bore. Miss Geva Stanley’s pic-
ture was the last to see the light at the Gallery,
and then it didn’t show her countenance with any
excessive kindliness.
“Society gathered thick at the ‘Jericho’ show.
Man is a godly character in the story, and
every point in his appraisement Was Waggishly
applauded by the male portion of
audience. ‘Man must rule’ drew a guffaw.
Woman is shown as a wicked worldling, gam-
bling and marrying for hard cash. One of her
wears an eye-pane; All smoke and luxuriate
on lounges full length, after heavy Bridge losses.
Miss Beatrice Day looks more than twenty-
five years of age and three years of matrimony.
Miss Winifred Fraser fails in chiffon expectancies,
but she treads the stage as if it were her own little
back room, and is a treat as far as voice and the
feminine whimsicalities of her part go. -A wonder-
ful supply of wired rose and wattle spray was
passed up to Miss Day and Miss Temple at each
curtain.
“ The pill and phial brethren have had a dance
on the top of the dental hop. Mayor Pleasance
(himself a pill-dispenser—his pharmacy is in
Collins-street) looked in on the scene and cracked
brotherly and professional jokes with his homoeo-
pathic and other pals.
“The Vic. Artists are forward with a mask
ball, timed for August 3.
“At the South Melbourne Mayoral hop a
pretty brunette decided to retire from active ser-
vice and be a wall-flower, whilst a faithful friend
hurried home to get a pair of old, commodious
shoes for her. The new and lovely ones were so
tight that she kicked them off for her feet’s sake.
“A wealthy Bendigo bachelor, past his green
youth, shortly leads to the altar a bright, chirpy
little widow'—that is, a law-widow, which kind
does not go into weeds and mourning stationery.
“The French Maid (Miss Irene Dillon) at the
‘ Players’ ’ amateur performance came' a cropper
in her first whirl of dash and devilment on Friday
night, but she sprang back at once to the perpen-
dicular, and smiled reassuringly at the audience,
and danced with desperate vim to the end of her
starry course. But back of her lay a grimace of
pain, and close watching revealed how she
endeavored to spare as much as possible an aching
limb. The Bijou badly needs renovating and
Spring cleaning Audiences crowded to that
amateur ‘French Maid,’ and packed the dingy
stalls way back, but snortfully.
“ After all the blow about Victorian voices,
where do they exercise ? Amateur comic opera,
open to all mummers, doesn’t get them. The
choirs don’t. Is each canary made a Melba of in
her own ’umble thatched cottage?
“ Mrs. Charles Moore gathered the she-generals
of the Women’s Political League together on
Thursday eve for a general account of stewardship
and for two rounds of buttered male oratory and
unspecified rounds of toast and coffee-roll, also
buttered. Woolly M.L.A. Fairbairn talked some.
He spoke cheerfully against she-suffrage, but went
on to say that since the sex had the Federal vote
(the evil haying happened and being unalterable
as it were) it behoved them to watch and work in
the interests of their country and their homes.
(Applause.) That is to say, his crowd w'ere against
allowing women the vote, but since women had
got it from the Labor people, they should vote for
his crowd. And the women applauded ! It shows
with what a small bit of intelligence Providence
can make a fashionable woman.
“ The Dante Society shows its head again after
a period of oblivion, and says, in a still, small
voice, that it chatters on Wednesday (26th) at the
Austral Salon, where all young associations exer-
cise their little tongues.
“News arrives of another Victorian widow
bidding for second martyrdom abroad. This
time it is Mrs. Eager, a daughter of Mr. W.
M'Culloch, who marries a Mr. Thornhill. Mr.
Thornhill has many green acres—or his dad has,
and that is almost as good.
“ The Isaac Isaacs are definitely breaking away
from the household pots and pans, and are lodged
cn famille at the Grand.
“The butchers have a grievance. Some good
old customers have caught a Yank fad, and the
cry is, ‘No red meat.’ Tripe, trotters, and other
chaste white trifles of meaty consumption are in
demand, but as cattle can’t furnish all tripe or
trotter and no beef-steak, the majority of the red
beast will be wasted if the fad spreads.
“A big chiffon merchant has been feeling
the pinch of the times in ‘ the Lane,’ and the
extravagant needs of a large, robust family. The
old home where money talked in tapestry, and
damask, and rosewood, was sold off not long ago,
and the garter merchant drifted with his domestic
dependencies into flat life. Now- the girls are
opening a suite of rooms where the dressed dummy
revolves on its patent axis, and fashion journals
lie in fresh piles on the wicker tables. By the
way, an ansemie johnnie (aesthetic is the feminine
epithet) plans out clothes for lovely woman in this
city. He studies one’s curves and complexion,
and designs chiffons ala Worth. Another male
person in the trouser line (his ad. is solely for
trousers) has been turning out bridal outfits, and
the legitimate petticoat-makers are in tears over
this rivalry.

thanks Heaven her husband failed at being an
actor. ‘He went on the stage because he loved
me, and he quitted it because I loved him and
couldn’t bear to see so fine a fellow wasting his
time there.’ That’s how she puts it. He is now
in America doing w-ell at law, and the fair one
lives partly on food and partly on the hope of a
speedy reunion.

Modern Applications of Electricity.— ln the use
of electrical methods of treatment for medical purposes
Sydney has lagged far behind other cities, and those who
visit England and the Continent, cannot fail to be struck
by the costly and elaborate nature of the appliances
employed. The work done in such establishments,
supervised by some of the most eminent English, French
and German physicians, has long passed the experimental
stage, and is now an exact science. Phototimrapy, or
Light treatment, Electrotherapy, or Electrical treatment,
and Vibrotherapy or Massage, are three of the most use-
ful aids in the treatment. A suite of treatment rooms,
known as Moore House, 16 and 18 Castlereagh-street,
have been fitted out with the most modern appliances
obtainable by the proprietors, Electrotherapy Ltd., and
nothing- has been left undone to make the establishment
the most perfect this side of the world. Inspection is
cordially invited, and everything of interest will be shown
and explained to visitors by the Manager, Mr. G. L. N.
Harvison, and his assistants.*


“G.H.” : Be a Pale Australia. Walked
along Exhibition-street, Melbourne, the other
day, from Lonsdale-street towards Carlton. Had
my hat knocked off by a Chinaman, who rushed
out of an alley with a large, newly-made meat-safe
swaying on his shoulder. Further on, three ice-
cream miscreants, armed with two bad violins and
a worse harp, were putting one of Gilbert and
Sullivan’s operas to a painful death. Several
Assyrian children occurred next, with two frowsy
she-parents in the background. A Jap happened.
He was about four feet high, but he had been
reading the war cables, and his upper weskit pro-
jected about seven furlongs ahead of him. _ He
had gold rings on most of his fingers, and, inci-
dentally, wore goloshes. Subsequently I met sun-
dry Chinese half-caste children, from eighteen
inches to two feet high, all wearing loud blue
pants. Also met a villainous-looking edition of
Ram Chunder, with a turban that hadn’t been
washed since Antony sent the theatre tickets to
Cleopatra. Three or four adult Chows also trans-
pired, one peddling feather dust-whisks, one sell-
ing silk handkerchiefs, and another clutching a
bank-book. I saw a good many whites who didn’t
call for remark—and I saw two other whites. One
was drunk.
“ Caro ” lays down the law as to gems :
Like colors in fabrics, gems must be suited to the per-
sonalities who wear them. The most beautiful of all
gems, the ruby, if worn by a brunette, sparkles like a
bit of gaudy glass. On a blonde it looks bizarre ; but on
a mezzo-tinted maid its true fire will flame and smoulder
—wine in a goblet of fire. But it is with the diamond
women make most mistakes. It looks almost universally
out of place—a pitiless stone, to be worn only by your
pale, gold-glinted blonde, whose heart could be accommo-
dated easily in an egg-cup. Only brunettes of “pallor”
can wear pearls as they should be worn —softly and in
fragile setting ; on a blonde they resemble blobs of candle
grease. To wear emeralds you must be of the children of
the world—bright, trusting, generous, to give divine fire
to this most coveted gem. If you have not ruddy hair,
tinted skin, and blood like to electricity, let sapphires be.
Turquoises—those great, uncut beauties that every sen-
sualist hungers for—must be worn by your silky woman,
who is hot and cold, but never indifferent —one whose
eyes and mouth are constantly at war. They are the
most passionate of all stones, with the exception of l he
opal; on a pallid wearer they deaden like beads. Opals,
with their allurement of superstition, are almost the
strangest of all. On a cold, material being they wane and
die to grey ashes, but worn by a highly-sensitive, hysteri-
cal, selfish woman they are alive, and as waves of emotion
sweep through the nerves, will burn, glow, and gleam
like globes of fire. Garnets are for the saucy maids—the
ballet girls of the world who play only in the sun. Topaz
for the placid inanities ; amber (though not a gem) for the
cruel-hearted; cairngorms for the studious. But study
and you will see that there is a soul, a fire, a something in
the heart of every stone, to be called forth only by a
fitting personality.

Joseph H. Longford, concerning the “ Commer-
cial Morality of the Japanese,” in the Con-
temporary Review : —
The average Japanese trader continues to be what he
has ever been, and to-day, when Japan’s foreign trade has
more than quintupled on what it was 30 years ago, when
she is enjoying a degree of commercial and industrial
prosperity then scarcely visioned even in dreams by the
most enthusiastic optimists, the old story, told over and
over again, not only by the direct sufferers, but in a long
series of consular reports and in the writings of
such great authorities as Chamberlain and Brinkley,
of shamefully violated obligations, continues to
come with wearisome and disheartening reiteration
from our own commercial representatives in Japan. . . .
Neither wealthy self-interest, nor patriotism, has even
modified their inherited and deeply-ingrained incapacity
for grasping the primary tenets of commercial integrity.
Whether as buyers or sellers, they are equally distrusted
by their foreign compeers. No foreign bank in Japan
accepts their bills ; no Japanese bank, unless it is protected
by the most abundant collateral security. Not a bale of
imported goods would be delivered to them without pre-
vious payment of the full price. Not a bale of silk, not a
chest of tea, not a roll of matting, would be received,
even from those whose annual sales may be reckoned
in hundreds of thousands of dollars, until every bale,
pound, and yard had been submitted to the closest
expert inspection. The most formal contracts are unblush-
ingly repudiated, or, at best, their execution postponed
when their prompt fulfilment involves a loss of even con-
temptible insignificance.

Maoriland’s Load of Cohens.
Premier Seddon (M.L.) has been telling the
Employers’ Association that “despite increased
wages the workers found themselves no better off
than formerly, on account of the increased cost of
living. People not employers or workers scooped
the money.” A few days before Seddon said this,
the chairman of the Bank of New Zealand had
remarked that “the great prosperity of farmers,
pastoralists, and dairymen is prompting them to
pay excessive prices for land.” Also —but the
chairman didn’t mention that—somebody seems
to be paying excessive prices for money; anyway,
the Bank of N.Z. shows a return of about 40 per
cent, on its shareholders’ funds, while in Aus-
tralia it’s a good bank that shows 7 or 8 per
cent.; and this vast profit is going, to a great
extent, to the absentee and the local do-nothing.
The other Maoriland bank, the National, which
is purely an absentee concern, makes a profit of
about 14 per cent, on its shareholders’ funds. The
Westralian Bank, working under conditions which
involve great risks, might be expected to show
great profits; yet the National Bank of Maori-
land, working under settled conditions, makes
profits nearly 50 per cent, greater than the Wes-
tralian Bank’s. Then, there is Seddon’s own
share in the business of sucking the country.
Maoriland’s public debt was £57,522,215 twelve
months ago ; and while 201 per cent, of the N.S.
Wales debt was locally raised, all but 16| per
cent, of the Maoriland debt was foreign. Further,
of the total of £57,522,215, Seddon, in the past
five years, has spent £11,429,685. Of course the
old yarn is told that all this has been sunk in
“permanent and reproductive” undertakings.
Even if this were true, it would not alter the hard
fact that it is foreign debt, and that the interest has
to be sent out of the country. Interest has to be
paid where Bull-Cohen lives ; that is to say, Bull-
Cohen’s debtor has not only to pay interest, but
to cart his goods to Europe and pay all charges
until the proceeds are put into Cohen’s hands.
And, anyway, the yarn that Cohen’s money has
been sunk in permanent and reproductive works
is just about the same lie in Maoriland as it is
elsewhere. Maoriland has to pay in interest and
charges £1,911,004 a year ; and the undertakings
in which Cohen’s money has been sunk earned
net in 1904 only £1,053,284. Cohen’s money thus
earned only £55 10s. of every £lOO Maoriland had
to pay for it. Also Maoriland has built up a local
government debt of nearly £10,000,000, which is
far ahead of even the Australian average. In
1902-3 Coghlan (in his great statistical work,
“Australia and New Zealand”) made a cal-
culation that, of £50,377,000 supposed to have
been sent out by Cohen to Maoriland, a
miserable little £383,000 had actually been
received, against £49,994,000 retained in London
to meet interest charges and pay for goods.
How the matter works out with regard to the
private Cohen can’t be said with much definite-
ness regarding Maoriland alone; but for all Aus-
tralasia Coghlan showed that for 30 years, from
1871 onward, £145,422,000 was brought into the
country (mostly in the shape of manufactures
which Australasia should have made for itself),
wlyle £183,152,000 was sent out of the country.
In other words, Australasia, in 30 years, paid the
whole £145,422,000 it had borrowed, and
£37,730,000 besides, and still owed the original
£145,422,000. And Seddon is surprised to find
that Maoriland, prosperous as it is, isn’t as really
prosperous as it ought to be, and he has wakened
up to the fact that something is gnawing at its
vitals. It apparently never occurred to him before
that even Maoriland can’t carry half-a-dozen fat
Cohens on its back without feeling tired; and
even now it is none too clear that he recognises
who the Cohens really are. At least, there is no
word yet from Seddon that he has joined the no-
more-borrowing party, and thus stopped adding
to the one vast blot on his administration.
Let Us Advertise.
It is difficult nowadays to pick up an issue of the
ordinary kind of morning paper which isn’t weep-
ing more or less about the failure to “ advertise ”
Australia. Canada advertises enormously—adver-
tises its land, its minerals, its industries, its wheat-
growing possibilities, its scenery, its climate, its
cold blue winter nose, and its snowballs—and it
gets multitudes of immigrants. Maoriland adver-
tises largely, and it gets immigrants—a wonderful
number of them, considering the great distance
that divides it from Europe. Australia doesn’t
advertise, and it gets very few immigrants.
Therefore the remedy for all the dry-rot of Aus-
tralia, according to the orthodox publication, is
advertisement. It is a supremely foolish idea, but
as a herring to draw across the trail of genuine
reform, it is probably as good as any other depre-
ciated fish. Yet few things seem more obvi-
ous than the obvious fact that no amount
of advertisement will bring people to settle
permanently in a country which hasn’t work to
offer them; to bring people here by advertise-
ment, and then leave them to starve would be
sheer inhumanity. The first essential of reform
is to make the country good enough to support
its own people; the next is to make it good
enough to attract people from outside. And
Australia hasn’t yet done the first of these ; in the
latest six years covered by Coghl.an’s statistics
(1898-1903), this country actually sent away 8582
more people than it attracted. It received im-
mense numbers certainly, but it exported still
larger numbers. The arrivals, by sea alone, in
the single State of N.S.W. amount to over
70,000 a year, but the departures, as often
as not, leave a balance against the State.
Advertising wouldn’t retain these people whose
departures make up our too-frequent surplus of
emigrants. The Australian Government might
paper the globe with pamphlets, pictures, lies and
decorations, and still the people who know the coun-
try already—the people who can’t get land because
land-grabbers are allowed to lock it up, and
who can’t get work because a miserably unpatriotic
tariff sacrifices our factories for the benefit of the
importer—would continue to drift away. The
most beautiful book of views ever printed
wouldn’t retain them, for men can’t eat views.
And when our own people, who know the coun-
try, won’t stay, it isn’t to be supposed that other
people, attracted by advertisement, would stay
either. Australia is told that it should advertise
its land; but which land—the Manifold and
Chirnside properties in Victoria, the A.A. Go.’s
and the Peel JRiver Co.’s great monopolies in
N.S.W., the locked-up Bibbenlulce estate,
and others of its kind; the S.A. Co.’s miles of
country, or the Van Diemen’s Land Co.’s gigantic
steal? Also, it is informed that it should adver-
tise its industrial prospects—the industries which
employed 154,775 male workers in 1902, and had
got down to 149,665 male workers in 1903.
These are hardly things to be advertised. Maori-
land and Canada have high tariffs, and growing
industries. Canada still has decent land to spare,
and Maoriland makes room for settlement by
breaking up its big estates. These States found
work for their own people first, and then they
advertised for good white immigrants and got
them. But the individual who would propose
advertising for white immigrants to come to Aus-
tralia’s locked-up areas and its stagnant factories
would only get his deserts if his dupes, after sizing
up the country, suspended him carefully on the
nearest telegraph-pole that was bought out of loan
moneys.
