Uric Acid: The Cause of Hany Diseases.
(By “Esou.**
The health of man is influenced to a very great extent by the condition of the kidneys. Any
disease of the kidneys, or interference wuh their action, affects the whole system, because the purity
of the blood is dependent upon the proper extraction from it of certain specific poisons, which it is the
duty of the kidneys to remove.
The kidneys are situated in the small of the back, one on each side of the spine. Human kidneys
are similar in shape to those of a sheep, but are somewhat larger. They are encased in fat, and are
supplied with arteries which convey blood to them from the heart. By a process akin to filtering, the
kidneys take any excess of water from the blood, and they also extract from the blood Various waste
poisons with which it becomes laden in its journey through the veins.
When a person is in good health these waste poisons are dissolved in the water taken from the
blood by the kidneys, and the solution is passed through narrow passages leading from each kidney
into the bladder, and is thence expelled from the body. This solution is known as urine. Some of
the poisons contained in urine are animal matter, such as urea and uric acid; others are mineral
matter, being salts of lime, magnesia, potash, and soda.
Unless the kidneys do their work thoroughly, the waste matter is incompletely extracted from
the blood, and becomes actively poisonous, causing us to suffer from various disorders which cannot
be cured until the kidneys are restored to health and activity.
After the blood is filtered and purified by the kidneys it enters the veins, and is, in due time,
returned to the heart, having, in the meanwhile, traversed the body and taken up a fresh supply of
waste matter. The heart again sends the blood to the kidneys, which once more filter it and extract
the impurities. This process goes on without ceasing day or night.
The kidneys of the average man filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine in
twenty-four hours. In this quantity of urine are dissolved about an ounce of urea, and ten or twelve
grains, in Weight, of uric acid, together with other animal and mineral matter, varying from a third of
an ounce to nearly an ounce.
The bloo 1, in the course of its circulation, carries nourishment, derived from the food we eat, and
oxygen to every part of the tissues of the body, and receives from the tissues matter which they have
consumed and for which they have no further use. This waste matter it is the duty of the skin, lungs,
and kidneys to throw off and get rid of. In the form of carbonic acid, the lungs throw off the equiva-
lent of eight ounces of pure charcoal every twenty-four hours, and the kidneys do their share by
eliminating urea, uric acid, etc., as described.
The principal disorders directly attributed to failure of the kidneys to perform their alloted task
are Rheumatism, Gout, Lumbago, Sciatica, Persistent Headache, Neuralgia, Anaemia, Gravel, Stone
and Bladder Troubles. When the kidneys are properly doing their work none of the complaints
mentioned can exist, because the causative poisons are then duly removed in a natural manner.
The only remedy known to science which is able to establish a regular and healthy action of the
kidneys is Warner’s Safe Cure. This remarkable medicine is the outcome of years of research, and
has been in use throughout the world for more than a quarter of a century. It is the only known
medicine which has the power to expel uric acid from the system, and it owes this power simply to its
ability to restore worn, weak, or diseased kidneys to their natural vigor. Nature does the rest.
Thousands suffer from kidney disease and do not know it. The commonest first symptom is pain
in the back. A simple test to make as to the condition of the kidneys is to put some of the urine,,
passed the first thing in the morning, into a bottle or covered glass, and let it stand until next,
morning. If it is then cloudy, or there is a brick-dust like sediment, or if particles float about in it,
then the kidneys are not healthy, and no time should be lost in adopting remedial measures or-
Bright’s Disease or some serious illness will be the result.
Remember that Warner’s Safe Cure will in all cases restore the kidneys to health and will con-
sequently cure—and permanently cure-all- diseases arising from their feeble condition and the reten-
tion of uric acid in the system.
A treatise containing a full description of the curative action of Warner’s Safe Cure and many
accounts of cases cured will be sent free on application to H. H. Warner and Co., Limited, Austra-
lasian Branch, Melbourne. [3]
Knocked Out.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ORDERED ashore for a spell,
Here on the grass I lie,
Where the great trees ripple and swell
As the singing winds sweep by.
There are roses everywhere
By hawthorn hedges girt;
And there’s never a fear or care,
Or anything else to hurt.
So I lie’mid the flowers a dreamer,
Stretched full length on the sod,
Sick of the sight of a steamer,
And the swing of a piston-rod.
Over the garden hedge,
Down by the willows there,
The grass is green to the edge
Of the Maitai River, where
The ripples chatter and play
In the shallows of the ford—
Where the teams come past all day
To the crack of leather and cord—
To the tug and mutter of leather,
And the stamp and clatter of steel,
As they get to their work together,
With the river high on the wheel.
The thrushes call from the elms,
The blackbirds play on the sward,
The fragrance of flowers o’erwhelms,
And sweet is the splash of the ford.
And sweet, ah ! sweetest of all,
Are the sounds of battle and strain,
When the voices of teamsters call,
And the loads sway upwards again,
To the music of chain and snaffle,
And a snort when a leader gains
The road, to scatter the gravel,
And throw his weight on the chains.
It is so pleasant to sleep
Here on the sun-warmed sward,
Lulled by the rush and sweep—
The musical rush of the ford.
Drowsing away to a tune
Of marvellous sweetness and peace,
But I’ll feel the blood grip me soon,
And the song and the slumber will cease.
And at night as I drive through the weather
I’ll hear, through the noise of the seas,
The creak and the mutter of leather —
The whips and the “ Whoas ” and the
“ Gees.”
But I’m ordered ashore for a spell,
So here on the turf I lie,
Nothing to say or to tell,
No one to question Why ?
There are roses around me abloom,
And daisies white on the grass.
Who would think of an engine-room
Near the fair wisterias ?
Leave me in peace, I’m a dreamer,
And I want to dream I’m a god.
I’m sick of the sight of a steamer,
And the swing of a piston-rod.
M.L.
WILL LAWSON.
In the Clutches of Comedy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SHE was reading in the bedroom when
he entered. Her eyes were like steel as he
stole tip-toe upon her. The color flew from
her cheeks; she stood up in white-lipped surprise.
“I had an idea that you would miss the train,”
she began, confusedly. “Is anything wrong?
Why are you so excited?” An atmosphere of
great calmness surrounded her suddenly; she
walked to the verandah doer, and half closed it.
A venomous curiosity was in the man’s eyes; a
glance unforgiveable as murder shot from him as
she swept round.
“ The express i 3 on its way to Melbourne two
hours ago. It is now 10 o’clock. I won’t bother
you with questions, Emily; I will merely sit
here by your window until” —he nearly snapped
out his words —“ until ”
“Until what, James?”
“Don’t play Bernhardt, Emily; don’t act,
don’t lie.” He regarded her for a moment, while
a new hate seemed to gather in his throat.
“Your studied gestures, your infantile poses
won’t help you this time. I shall sit here until
HE comes.”
The woman flinched. The fury and speed of
his attack snapped her thin line of resistance.
She stared at her book with eyes that could
not see, and he watched her with the air of a
hanging judge.
It was the fourth year of their married life.
One thought gripped her as she stared at the
hard-faced book—she was glad there had been no
children. He had returned to her suddenly when
he ought to have been on his way to Melbourne.
Other men had played the same trick on their
wives with varying success. He was sitting
opposite her, his back to the door. For her there
was no escape now; she could only sit and watch
his iron fingers tapping the half-open door.
There was a sharp click at the gate below.
The night was clear and still. A cart rumbled
slowly past the house. A piano in the adjoining
terrace banged mercilessly. Then came a few
moments of quiet respite.
A click, click, click at the gate below happened
incidentally, but it sounded to Eldgaarde as if it
had come from the fingers of a telegraph operator.
Mrs. Eldgaarde sat like a frozen image in the
chair. She looked at her husband’s face and shut
her eyes. With a swift movement he lowered the
lamp slightly. When she looked again she saw
him half crouching in the dark of the verandah,
his revolver resting on the rail.
“I am going to shoot him—you understand.”
Eldgaarde spoke in a quiet voice. He made no
gestures, but as the lamp-rays caught the revolver
barrel, she saw that it covered a figure in the gate-
way. She screwed up her eyes like a child, ex-
pecting the pistol-flash.
Eldgaarde leaned over the verandah rail.
“Stop!” he said; “if you cross that border or
move an inch. I’ll shoot.”
A muffled assent came from below. Eldgaarde,
still covering the figure, addressed his wife:
“ Before I shoot him, tell me his name. I may
give him one chance. If you care for him tell me
his name. I’ll give you 12 seconds.”
Her fingers tore into the book, and her _ tongue
grew like a snake in her head, almost choking her.
But the seconds moved with the lightning-beats of
her heart.
“ It’s Fred Westhouse.” Her voice was harsh
and savage now. She sat back and wiped her
ashen lips.
“I don’t know the man.” Eldgaarde spoke
again from the balcony. “What a damned
f arC e i
He slipped over the rail, slid down the post
and in a second had crossed the garden. A thick-
set man confronted him. “Westhouse,” said
Eldgaarde, hoarsely. “I want to see your face
before shooting you.”
“My name ain’t Westhouse, boss.” The thick-
set man removed a villainous hat, exposing a face
that was blotched and pimply with drink. “My
name ain’t Westhouse,” he “Strikeme
dead ! what yer goin’ ter shoot fer ? ”
“What are you doing here?” thundered Eld-
gaarde. “ Who are you ?” _
The man fawned. “Give us a chance, boss.
People next door ain’t paid a stiver rent since
Christmas. I’m only a bailiff, waitin’ ter get in.
Strike me dead—don’t shoot a bloke ’cause he’s a
bailiff.”
Eldgaarde pocketed his revolver and returned
to his wife.
ALBERT DORRINGTON.
The Human Ostrich.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE Human Ostrich giggled. Knowing
him to be a man who never giggled with-
out good reason, I invited him, and the
Fat Man, and the rest of the show round the
corner, and there was an interval for refreshments.
Said the Human Ostrich: “I didn’t sleep a
wink all last night. About half-a-hundred red-
hot devils got under me jaw, and started tearin’
things about. I swallered a hull chemist’s shop,
and rubbed a gallon or so o’ stuff outside. I
cussed, and banged me head on the wall. I
tried everything anybody told me to, but the
devils under me jaw only knocked things about
more willin’.
“Well, this mornin’ I goes to a dentist bloke,
and told him to harpoon a hundred or so of those
demons.
“‘Ah, toothache,’ says he, soft and easy like.
‘ We’ll soon fix that up. Which one is it ? ’
“‘Which one?’ says I; ‘how can I tell, when
one hull blanky jaw’s tearin’ away f’r all it’s
worth?’
“‘Jusso—jusso,’ says he, an’starts jabbin’ an’
hittin’ away at me an’ wantin’ to know if it hurt.’
“‘Course it hurts,’ says I. I knew he was
doin’ it a-purpose, an’ felt riled.
“ ‘ Yes, yes, ’ says he, ‘ that’s the one; we’ll soon
have him out,’ an’ he purred away like an old
tom-cat.
“Then he jabs something up me gum and
fastens on to me jawbone an’ pulls for all he was
worth. I was just goin’ to give him a settler in the
wind when out comes the tusk. It was the wrong
’un. He yanked another out. Wrong ’un again.
I had half a mind to chuck him through his
bloomin’ winder, but thought I’d give him
another go.
“This time he got the right ’un, and those
devils started amblin’ off the premises, though
they left things mighty sore.
“But the bloke wasn’t satisfied then. He
started pokin’ round, an’ quizzin’ with his little
mirror, anVtold me I had three wanted stoppin’.
Said if I didn’t have ’em done they’d soon give me
the same trouble. But I reckoned I’d had enough,
an’ asked him the figger.
“‘Let’s see,’ says he, ‘three hextractions at
seven-an’-six —twenty-two-an’-six, please. ’
“ Well, that kinder took me breath away. I’m
glad now that it did ; cause if it hadn’t, I’d a’
swore pretty willin’. Jumpin’ Jehoshaphat! Pay
him nine half-crowns for pullin’ three teeth, when
he only oughter pulled one ! Pay him twenty-
two-an’-six for makin’ a gory battlefield o’ my
mouth ! Not me.
“ Just as I was gettin’ me breath an idea struck
me, an’ I managed to work up an innocent smile.
*Oh is that all ? ’ says I. ‘ Well, I guess I’ll have
those three stopped while I’m at it, if it won’t,
figger out more ’n a tenner.’
“Y’ oughter heard him smile. He put his little
mirror on the little table in front o’ me, an’ when
he turns round to get some rag, I swallered it.
“ He jabs the rag inter the side o’ me mouth,
then hunted round for his mirror.
‘ Why,’ says he,’ ‘I had it just now—most mar-
vellous thing.’
‘ He put some borers an’ a picker on the table
while he went to get another mirror. I swallered
’em.
“When he Comes back an’ found his picker and
borers missin’, he eyed me pretty suspiciously.
But I kept solemn-like, an’ he got some more. He
poked about me teeth a bit, then put his other mirror
down. I swallered it when he turned to fix up
his whirly-wirly.
“ When he found his second mirror had vanished
along with some other things, he looked sur-
prised like an’ opened his mouth, but shut it again
without speakin’. I could see he was tryin’ to
persuade himself he wasn’t a lunatic.
“ But I kept on swallerin’, every chance I got,
an’ at last he says to me, ‘ Look here, old man,
a lark’s a lark, but you’re goin’ too far. Just hand
over those instruments. ’
“Course I played ’possum, an’ he told me
pretty plain that if I didn’t part up he’d have to
consider me a thief.
“ Course I get riled an’ calls him a liar, an’ he
calls Aubrey, an’ Aubrey calls the p’lice. When
the bobby comes, the bloke tells him what’s up. I
called him a liar again an’ told him to search me.
Course they found nothing.
“ ‘ Ye’ll have to shtrip,’ says the cop.
“I stripped an’ they searched me clothes, but
o’ course couldn’t find nothin’, an’ the dentist
bloke went as white as a ghost.
“‘I—I—I must a’ been mistaken,’ he splut-
tered.
“ ‘ ’Tis what ye are—an’ ye’re a fool,’ says the
cop, in a bit of a rage.
“‘Yes,’ says the bloke, ‘but it’s most as-
tonishin’—most astonishin’.’ Then he gives the
cop harf-a-sov. to drink his health with, an’ the
cop goes. I climbs inter me duds pretty smart,
then sits up in the chair again, an’ tells the bloke
to wire in. But he gasps like an ol’ hen on a
hot day, and sinks down on the sofa, an’ waves
his hand at me.
“ When he managed to speak he could only say,
‘Go —go—go f’r Gawd’s sake.’
“‘Well, I’ll pay ye f’r the lot when ye’ve
finished,’ says I.
“‘Pay—pay the devil,’says he. ‘lf ye come
back here ”
“ ‘ I’ll bring a lunatic asylum,’ says I.
“ Then how did he tear his hair ! He starts
callin’ f’r Miss Jones an’ f’r Aubrey, an’ tellin’ me
to go ; an’ the bit o’ style from the waitin’-room
comes in, an’ the kid in buttons comes in, an’ the
bloke starts havin’ hysterics or somethin’, an’
they looked as if they’d like to have some along
with him, so I left ’em at it. Hope he hasn’t
gone clean off his head altogether.”
And the Human Ostrich giggled again.
LES LEE.
Two Sous.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE long, white road seemed to writhe
and twist in the fierce heat of the afternoon
sun. Two lines of cocoanut palms, one on
either side, stretched away to where the hospital-
gates formed a vanishing point in the distance,
but instead of casting a shade they seemed rather
to enclose the heat as between two walls.
Surveillant-militaire Bassini crouched under the
hedge and cursed the Fates that made him a
guard of convicts in New Caledonia. Before him,
in the full glare ot the sun, clad only in canvas
trousers and straw hat, worked convict Bicot,
“lifer.” The pick in his hands rose and fell with
a mechanical regularity that was almost sickening.
The lowering, clean-shaven face with the hard
mouth, seamed brow and keen black eyes never
altered. It was hard and set, and showed no
trace of the tumultuous thoughts within.
The sun-browned body streamed with sweat,
and glittered as the muscles rolled and twitched.
Bicot was in the pink of condition ; otherwise he
could not have stood the awful strain.
The surveillant took the cigarette from his lips,
and blew out a cloud of blue smoke that rested
in a haze round his head, so heavy was the air.
“ How hard my dear friend Bicot works ! ” he
said, sneeringly. “And to think that it is for
our dear France and not for himself that he toils !
Oh, admirable patriot! ”
The convict might have been deaf for all the
notice he took, but the muscles on his jaws began
to twitch, and into the small, black eyes came an
ominous glitter.
“And how great his love for his dear friend
Bassini !” drawled on the surveillant. “Has he
not already made him a present of five golden:
sovereigns, the nice yellow English money that
everybody is so ready to take ? And is he not
now going to give him two more.”
Pick—pick—pick. The convict took as little
notice as before.
Bassini’s face grew black with malevolence.
“See here, my friend !” he said, “I waste no
more time with you. I have already asked you
twice ; this is the third time. I know you have
two sovereigns. I saw the glint as Goutrin passed
them to you last night. If you don’t tell me
where you have put them by the time I count ten
I will kill you where you stand, as I had to do
with that fool, Durand. You remember Durand’s
death, and how I was complimented on escaping
from the attack of so dangerous a scoundrel. You
will be the same, and your carrion will be shoved
into a hole in the ground, and what good will your
two pounds do you then ? ”
Pick, pick, pick. The convict never slackened
or changed his stroke, or took his eyes off the
ground.
Bassini slowly drew his revolver, rest.ed it on
his knee, and began deliberately to count. Bicot
made no sign but the muscles on his arms swelled
into knots of whipcord.
‘ ‘ One—two—three ” (pick)— ‘ ‘ four—five—six
(pick)—“seven—eight—nine” (pick)—“ ten.”
Bassini darted a look up and down the road
before firing. Bicot sprang forward with uplifted
pick and the light of madness in his eye.
The surveillant had time to fire just once, but
the bullet glanced off the hardwood pick-handle,
and the next moment Bassini was a senseless mass
on the ground.
When others arrived, attracted by the shot,
they found Bassini’s body riddled with pick-holes,
and close beside lay Bicot, a bullet-hole in his
temple and the surveillant’s revolver gripped in
his right hand.
Clenched in his left were two bright one-sou
pieces.

My Visitor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He came to me at close of day,
When lamps were lately lit,
And men along each dusky way
Like shadows seemed to flit.
He came so softly in the room,
His step I did not hear ;
He seemed a ghost from outer gloom
That sought for light and cheer.
In truth he was a figure odd,
And quaint as quaint could be;
His clothes were of a period
Far back in history.
An old patched suit of faded red,
And short black cloak he wore ;
The queer-shaped cap upon his head
A broken feather bore.
Yet in his manner, easy, free,
His quiet, easy ways,
There was a battered dignity
That told of better days.
I made some mention of the road
He might have travelled on ;
He smiled a weary smile that showed
His teeth were mostly gone.
The night was warm as it could be—
My shirt-sleeves were uprolled—
And yet, somehow, it seemed to me
He shuddered with the cold.
And then he blinked his old weak eyes
Of faded china-blue,
And said, “ I must apologise
For calling thus on you.
“ It was not proper etiquette,
Of that there is no doubt,
But friends of yours whom I have met
Said you might help me out.
“ The fact is that I want your aid
To float a Company,
In which you shall have shares prepaid,
If you will stand by me.
“There is a syndicate below—
You know the place I mean—
A certain crowd with morals low
But business instincts keen.
“A corner they have made in Coal,
This wicked, selfish Ring,
And now each imp and each lost soul,
Sits sadly shivering.
“ Hell now is as an ice-house chill
The tears ran down his face—
“ There is not fire enough to grill
A chop in all the place.
“ And so I’ve come, with hopeful heart,
To see some business men,
And float a Company, and start
The good old fires again.
“ I offer as Security ”
He shed some further tears—
“ My gates, and all the gate-money,
For, say, a thousand years.
‘ ‘ The Parsons now might take some shares—
They make the most by me—
And, if I close the shop downstairs,
I ask—where will they be ? ”
N.S.W.
CREEVE ROE.
Mr. Barnes, Desperado.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
LJAID Mr* Barnes: “Mine’s a beer.
Just a plain* ’onest beer. It alus is,” he
added, thoughtfully. “Talkin’ o’ members
o’ Parlyment, though, reminds me of a little
epi—epi—o’ summat what took place up at
Gaskir Crik afore y’u young fellers wuz born.
“One night we wue all in at ‘The Lucky
Bowyang,’ as usual, when up comes the coach
Wat called every now an’ sometimes. ‘Yous is
goin’ to ’ave visiters—distinguished visiters,’ sed
Freddie Wilkins, the driver. ‘Ho ! ’ sed we.
‘Yuz,’ sed ’e. ‘The right ’onerible mister al-
migh by Fortyscue blanky Smith ! ’ ‘ Gor’-
struth ! ’ larfed everybody ’cept Sly Mick—’im
wat owned the ‘ Bowyang,’ the on’y pub within 60
miles (a gold mine it were—Mick admittin’ that
if on’y ’e could collect wat we owed ’im ’e would
buy a brew’ry an’ retire).
“ Sly Mick ’e got quite excited, an’ walked up
an’ down, an’ invited everyone to drink at ’is
expense —which we did twice. ‘ Think o’ the
’oner ! Think o’ the ’oner ! * ’e kept sayin, ‘l’ll be
able to put up “Under the extinguished patronige
o’ the Right ’Orrible Mister Blankey Wat’s-’is-
name Smith.” ’
“‘Ho, will y’u?’ larfed Freddy, the driver.
‘D’ y’u think ’e’d stay at sich a low-down pub as
this ’ere ? Why, ’e travels round with a whole
cary van—tents an’ cooks an’ secretaries an’ things.
Very perticler ’e is—a Inglish Parliament bloke
lookin’ round Australia. Talks a lot about the
Empire. Wants to ’ave a chat with a ’Stralian
gold-mining camp. So, bein’ pretty chock of ’im
over yonder, they’re passin’ ’im on ’ere. Timid
young feller ’e is, with watery eyes an’ a corf.
’E’ll pitch a few tents out there by the timber,
&n’ worry round yous all day an’ arst questions.’
“ * The devil ’e will! ’ shrieked Mick, in a ter-
rible rage, an’ started in an’ cursed all the Right
’Oneribles what wuz ever born cyr likely to be.
‘ Wants to see a minin’ camp, do ’e ? My ’otel
ain’t good enuf, ain’t it ? Blast me, we’ll show
’im ! We’ll ’and ’im out a Wild West Show ! A
Right ’Onerible! I never yet met one as wuz
any good. Bah ! ’ Sly Mick spat to show ’is con-
temp’, an’ then, as ’e turned nasty an’ wanted to
charge us fer the drinks which ’e in ’is excitement
’ad shouted, we dispersed.
“ We never lost sight, ’owever, fer a moment of
that scheme to surprise our ’igh-class visiter, an’
by the end o’ the week we ’ad formed our plans.
After much difficulty we managed to raise six
rusty revolvers an’ a few ole fowlin’ pieces. Sly
Mick, ’oo ran the store as well as the pub—’e ran
most everythin’—’anded round, on loan, all the
red shirts an’ colored ’andkerchiefs ’e could find —
which is the things all miners wear in picter-
books. It wuz a ’straordinary sight to see the
boys swaggerin’ round in fancy shirts an’ belts
with revolvers an’ knives stuck in ’em. It looked
jist like a the-ater play.
“Well, late one night, along comes ’is royal
’ighness with is circus, an’ erects three tents, well
away from th’ camp, under a clump o’ wattles.
By express, orders, no one was to go near ’im till
next mornin’, an’, ’cept fer a few o’ the young
chaps crawlin’ down an’ growlin’ an’ sniffin’ an’
scratchin’ round the tents, like tigers an’girafs
an’ sich wild beasts, ’e an’ ’is retinoo ’ad a quiet
an’ peaceful night o’ it. At daybreak, next morn-
in’, a deputashun went down to ’is tent an’ woke
’im up orfer ’is spring bed, an’ tole ’im ’ow de-
lighted we wuz to see ’im.
“ ‘We ’opes as ’ow y’u’ll be comfertible among
us,’ sed I. We’s rough-an’-ready, but good-
’earted —an’, if some on us is a bit quick-tempered
an’ shoots a bit ’asty like, why we’s sorry fer it th’
next minit, an’ alus looks arter th’ widow an’
orphans ’
“ ‘ Th-thank y’u, my good man,’ sed ’e, lookin’ a
bit funked an’ kinder shyin’ at the array o’ iron-
mongery stuck in our belts. ‘ Th-thank y’u, my
good man ’—(s’welp me, I near passed ’im one in
th’ eye—talkin’ to me like so—but e’ wuz so little
an’ pink an’ talked through the top of ’is neck so
pretty that I ’eld off). 4 1 ’ave come among y’u to
study your mode o’ livin’,’ sed e’. ‘ln this great
Hempire of our’n ’
“ ‘Excuse us,’ sed I, ‘but we must be movin’.
A little matter o’ justice—we alus like to finish
these affairs afore breakfast. ’
“‘Ho,’ sez ’e, ‘jist wat I wanted to inquire
into. A trial, I presoom ? ’
“|Not exactly,’ I answered back. ‘Jist a
’angin’! ’
“‘ A what!’ shrieked ’e.
“I led ’im to the door o’ the tent an’ pointed in
silence to where a group o’ the boys stood, about
200 yards off, round Jerry Lane. Jerry knelt
under a big gum with a rope round ’is neck t’other
end of which wuz passed over a ’igh branch. In
the cold grey o’ the mornin’ it was a creepy sight.
“ ‘ Oh, my Gawd ! ’ gasped Mr. Onerible Smith,
an’ covered ’is eyes with ’is ’ands. I left ’im then
an’ made me way down to the tree, where we cast
off Jerry an’ ’oisted a stuffed dummy in ’is place to
the top o’ the branch amid ’orrible gurglin’s. We
’ad a little difficulty wi’ Jerry when ’e wanted the
dummy corpse cut down, cos ’e ’ad discovered it
wuz wearin’ ’is best pants, but we quieted ’im at
last, and ’eaded by Sly Mick playin’ ‘ The Dead
March ’ on the accordeon, filed bareheaded pist
the tents. ’ls nibs wuz standin’ in ’is night clothes
where I left ’im, ’is ’ands an’ jores workin’ ’orribly.
*1 s’pose it's alright,’ I ’eard ’im gasp. ‘I ’s’pose
it’s alright. Rough justice ! Rough justice !
Tell me,’ ’e called to me, ’arf sobbin’, ‘ Wot wuz
the poor wretch guilty of ? ’
“ ‘ Stealin’ a pipe,’ sed I.
“ ‘ A what?’ shrieked ’e, foamin’ at the mouth,
—-‘ A pipe ? ’
“ ‘Well, it were a good pipe—belonged to this
gent ’ere,’ sed I, pointin’ to Mick. Now, Mick
’ad one ’and on’s ’ead, an’ t’other ’and fumblin’ in
’is pocket.
“ ‘ By ’Eavins ! ’ ’e cried, tragically, ‘l’ve made
a mistake ! Jarge never stole it, arter all. By
’eavens, no ! It muster got caught in the linin’ o’
me coat.’
“ ’E slowly pulled a broken black cutty from ’is
pocket, an’ we all gazed at it in ’orror-stricken
silence. ‘ Oh, my Gawd ! that this can be ! ’
groaned poor Smithy. ‘ls it a nightmare, O
Gawd ? ’ ’E staggered into ’is tent, an’ fell on to
’is bed, and, from the noises we ’eard, I should say
’e’d never bin so sick in all ’is life afore. Then we
went into the scrub, an’ lay up agen each other
an’ shrieked.
“ Our distinguished visitor took all that mornin’
to recover, an’ in the afternoon e’ an’ ’is cook an’
sec-retery an* the bottle-washer an’ chamberlain
an’ aidy-kamp shifted camp to t’other side the
crik, outer sight o’ the dummy corpse, an’ started
in to write up the ’orrible details o’ the atrocity.
“In the evenin’ ’e ventured out —an’ we wuz
ready fer ’im. Every man on the crik wuz sham-
min’ drunk (some few ’ad the luck not to be
shammin’), an’ as ’e an’ ’is pals strolled past the
‘ Bowyang ’ there wuz no less than six couples
busily engaged knifin’ each other ; while Jerry an’
me wuz blazin’ away at each other with revolvers
acrost the street, an’ Sly Mick, seated on ’is door-
step, wuz dealin’ with imagin’ry snakes with a
tommy’awk. Then the explorin’ party retired in
disorder to their camp, an’ barricaded ’emselves
in, an’ did sentry-go in turns all night.
“ Next day wuz quiet, an’ me lord thinkin’, no
doubt, our howlin’ bender wuz over fer the time
bein’, crept cautious out o’ ’is camp to study life.
Then our great koop came off ! A party o’ the
leadin’ an’ most inflooenshal citizens wuz showin’
’im round in the dusk when, with blood-curdlin’
yells, like football barrickers, ten o’ the Belli-
Belli blacks (’ired at the cost o’ a bottle o’ rum per
’ead) burst from a clump o’ timber an’ charged
down on us like we wuz a counter lunch. We
fired a volley in the air an’ retreated ’1 fer leather
—the Inglish coves doin’ the pacin’. D’y’u ever
notice ’ow a sudden shock takes different blokes
d iff’rent ways? Some on ’em shivers an’some on
’em sweats, an’ some on ’em swears. I alus swears
meself,” added the old man, absent-mindedly
taking his neighbor’s beer. When the said neigh-
bor had been placated, Mr. Barnes continued :
“ These ’’ere Inglish blokeslay in the pub, where
We planted ’em, sweatin’an’ shiverin’, an’listunin’
to the clatter an’ the groans, an’ the firin’, an’ the
shrieks, an’ the bangin’, an’ the cursin’ outside,
till Jerry, all over blood an’ rags, staggered in an’
gasped: ‘Fly fer yer lives, mates we’re done
there’s millions on ’em—millions. They’ve
slaughtered all the wimmin an’ child’en, an’ now
they’re marchin’ on ther pub. Tell me poor ole
muvver I’ ——■ With that’e squelched on to the
floor in a silent ’eap. ’E wuz a born play-actor
wuz Jerry.
“Very cautiously we led ’em out to the back,
an’ ’elped ’em on their ’orses what we ’ad waitin’.
Then we put ’em on the road to Glede, an’ left
’em.
“A week later, ole Bill Leckie, the J.P., rode
over wi’ some troopers to inquire into the reports
made by Mister ’Onerible Thing-me-bob Smith.
But, as no one seemed to know nothink about
anythink, an’ as all the Right ’Onerible’s camp
an’ swag ’ad been cut up long since, Bill gave it
up as a waste o’ time, an’ ’im an’ the troopers got
tight, like sensible bein’s an’ gentlemen.
“Ah, them wuz the times! A man did’ave
a charnst to git a bit lively now an’agin. The
beer bit all the way. Seems to me it’s losin’ its
flavor now’days.”
ARF. BRASCH.
